THE RED PAGE
ATHENA.
I bring all problems to the light,
The sacred and profane ;
No wind-winged lie conceals its flight,
Nor midget truth is slain.
Profound conceptions grey with age,
Beliefs immune from doubt
In passionless repose I gauge,
Patch up, confirm, cast out.
Poetic dream, religion vain.
Rhetorical excess,
’Fore my tribunal I arraign
With blue-eyed deadliness.
And structures with embrasured Fears,
Too huge for outer thrust,
My patient termite pioneers
Shall analyse' to dust.
The truth my worshippers record
For its pure sake alone;
Who gazes on me for reward
Falls, a brainless stone.
You may build a god from thought or stone,
From words your sires have said,
From fears or powers your sires have known
Or books that they have read.
All life may recognise his sway,
Creation seems his word :
If he is an idol, he fades aw’ay
When hoots Athena’s bird.
You may by passion, pride or zeal.
For the sake of a beautiful lie,
My stern criteria repeal.
While dull Time trudges by :
You may build the lie in law, in rite,
In custom, blood and brain.
The time shall come when, in her might,
Athena returns again:
Returns, and relentless batters the lie,
The pride and the passion, though
The law, the rite, the custom die,
And the race fall with the blow.
Yea, when I seemed myself a lie,
My temples down I tore, ®
Commanded ages let me die,
And men forbade adore.
Tho’ sage Illusions beckon hence
To systems more complete,
On autochthonic Common Sense
Rigid I plant my feet.
While Space conceals a coign unknown,
While riots fear, disease,
Athena’s priesthood shall intone
Her limpid litanies.
Yet I but rule while I obey,
For Thought, my region wide,
Is a dim atoll, wet with the spray
Of a lapping Higher Tide.
Yea, I am Wisdom, for I see
In life, death, heaven, earth,
The Unknowable, the Mystery,
The Brain that gave me birth.
BERNARD O'DOWD
(in “Dominions of the
Boundary ”).
BERNARD O’DOWD
DIPPING into slang—and all these
tight little stanzas packed with
thought and capital letters forces
us to fly to slang for relief Bernard
O’Dowd is “ a tough proposition.” The
man-on-the-ferry says, “ Yes, that is all
prettily put, and compactly phrased ; he’s
got a wide scientific vocabulary ; he’s read
all the latest books upon evolution, and
picked up all their big new words ; he is apt
at a definition, picturesque and forcible at a
phrase, logical in his deductions ; but
And a swarm of questions buzz round him
with an exasperating persistence. “ What
is O’Dowd after ? What’s his game ? Where
does he hope to get to, or does he hope to
get anywhere ? Is he forever going on
cutting up science into neat little snippets,
eternally dosing us with compact little rime-
coated couplets ? Has he nothing 61se to do
but define Pate, and Yulcan, and Juno,
and Hermes, and Athena, and Isis, and
Bacchus in the precise terminology of
science ? What call does he feel to inflict
upon us these dicta of modern thought ?
What is his philosophy '? What ecstasy does
he seek to communicate i What—oh, what,
is his little game ? ”
And Bernard O’Dowd goes serenely on
with his interminable neatly-rimed stanzas,
for this is his third book of verse ; it is
called The Dominions of the Boundary (T. C.
Lothian, Melb.) ; and it does not differ from
his former books, and the stanza is the same,
and the big scientific words show no sign
of abating, or the capitals of slipping sur-
reptitiously into the lower case, or the neat
definitions of becoming any more final. For
give this poet an umbrella or a parsnip, and
he would promptly open out to us the inner
meaning of umbrellas, or snip off for Us the
scientific symbolism of Still, in
this latest work there is a theme, a sugges-
tion of a philosophy. O’Dowd is doubtful
of the value of the science he has so labori-
ously studied. Accurately and almost auto-
matically reflecting the present attitude of
science, which, having proved that matter
does not exist except as a form of motion,
now leans toward mysticism, O’Dowd,
Science’s faithful scribe, begins to doubt the
scope and finality of this new knowledge.
After we have probed and mapped and
measured, is there not something left over,
something incommensurable, some hinter-
land of the senses where strange things may
yet wander, some shadowy place whose
mysterious twilight may shelter —let us
imagine—a soul 1 The dominions of the
■boundary are real dominions, the gods are
still alive, the world still plastic to the
touch of inspiration, the casements of the
soul not yet closed to the vagrant winds of
wonder.
But tho’ we mumble sceptic saw,
Or sweet old prayers forget;
And tho’ we dream of Higher Law,
The gods are living yet.
For they who will no .Monitor,
Save One Unnamed, allow,
Allegiance deep to Love or War,
Or Chance or Wind avow :
Avow by no mere cult of names,
No pattering of creeds,
But by the sacrificial flames
Of lifelong thoughts and deeds.. ,«
All is not daylight in the day,
Not knowledge in the known ;
The life we live, the prayer we pray.
From deep, to deep, is blown.
Though Reason claim omniscient worth,
And lush her dogmas thrive :
Our present home is more than earth,
Our senses more than five. . . .
Though mountain watchmen daily see
Horizons widen far,
Dominions of the Boundary
Have ever ruled, and are....
Yet, shaping slowly through the storm,
And bidding darkness fade,
Evolving eyes discern a Form
No clay-creator made:
A Symbol Form that mirrors ours,
That is, yet ih not we,
That seems to hint of Higher Powers
Than Fate or Memory..,.
All this is not in itself symbolism ; it is an
admirable and lucid definition of symbolism.
It is the deliberate description of a well-
informed onlooker; it is the song of thd
skylark reduced to its notes. Valuable, cer-
tainly ; but Meredith observed the skylark :
Shelley was the skylark.
But O’Dowd is a slave to the striking
word. He can say things with an admirable
simplicity, but he prefers to say them with a
shout. His skylarks do not reach Heaven ;
they, and you, arrive at a “cobalt landing”
—and it’s a bit of a shock. Maybe he will
say that the shock is good for us ; but he
must remember that he depends upon the
clarity of his medium to bring out the full
value of his meaning, and peppering his
verses with big, uncouth, prosaic, scientific
terms only impedes his delivery and baulks
his listeners. Has a poet any right to im-
pede the flow of his message with such
ungainly word-chunks as “ earthquake sala-
manders,” “psychopathic,” “prophetic
glyphs,” “universe’s phagocytes,” “ions,’
‘ ‘ spectroscopic synthesis, ” ‘ ‘ lava-j etted
leads,” “ hyperbolic space,” and “ asymp-
tote ” 1 And where is the poetry in a stanza
like this ?
The sanctions aboriginal,
Taboo and wizard’s fear,
Aversion and its ritual
Of frown and “ cut ” and sneer,. ~
And what means this ?
The selvage of Infinity
My cameos adorn.
From such wilful obstacles as these it is
almost with relief that we , flounder into
cheerful jingles that suggest a quite unmeant
humor :
And he who boards my lithe canoe
And paddles away with me
Shall land anon on Mount Meru
And all the Devas see....
And Hope's immortal ibis drinks
From my impartial Nile,
With Love the lotus, Fate the sphinx,
And Death the crocodile.
But not content with his snippet-stanza,
this persevering poet economises in words.
His one aim seems to be to get as few words as
possible ill each line. Frequently he achieves
a two-word line, as in
Clairaudient interpreter.
Clairvoyant,Pythoness.
Anri at last he reaches his goal in this re-
markable one-word line,
Amanuensis-annalist.
But having called attention to these
obvious and apparently wilful faults in
O’Dowd, let us indicate why he still can be
termed a poet, and a poet of worth. Though
it remains a pity that so often he says it in
prose, O’Dowd has something to say. Out
of his scientific terminology he often
rises to inspiration; the earthiness of the
phrase cannot disguise the loftiness of the
thought. His work is always, suggestive ; it
“gives furiously to think.” Thus “Fate”
speaks:
Ah ! but you say that woman’s love
Delivers from my land !
Nay, she is but a dainty glove
Upon my wizard hand.
And his phrase is often something more than
picturesque, sometimes perfect:
Ye spring to life, the weak, the great,
The singer and his song,
When lo ! a Wind that seems a Fate
Besoms them all along.
While there is the pulsing of human blood,
the universal appeal, in stirring verses like
these :
O come, Australians, maid and youth,
Enlist in our crusade !
From wan Gomorrahs of untruth
Flock to the Bacchic glade !
Away, away from antique wraiths
And clammy deities !
On ! on ! to transcendental faiths
And young Democracies ! . . . .
Away with Mammon and his woe,
Nor his Holy Places spare ?
Bacchantes, Maenads, at them ! 10,
■— His bale-eyes stranglers, there ! . • • »
Extirpate from your virgin soil
Exotic hate and wrong ;
And thrill the torpid nerves of toil
With vision and with song. '. . . .
Finally, in the closing stanzas of “Athena,”
the poet rises to the peaks of song, and sees
all the kingdoms of earth spread out beneath
him. The majestic close of this poem shows
O’Dowd far above the thick mists where he
so often seems content heavily to wing his
way.
MAY SINCLAIR
IT must reluctantly be admitted that May
Sinclair has lost the divine fire. Her
second “big” novel, The Helpmate
(Constable, per Sydney Book Club), has
not fulfilled the great promise of that aston-
ishing product of a woman’s brain, The
Divine Fire. That was a novel which, by its
unconventional treatment of an exceptionally
interesting theme, its satisfactory rendering
of character, its broad knowledge of life, its
freedom, except in its final chapters, from the
feminine touch, aroused the expectation of a
new woman writer of the first rank. In The
Helpmate May Sinclair drops back a little
from that pride of place. Yet this fearless
treatment of a conventional theme —
the discovery by a “pure” woman that
her husband has had a “past”—dis-
plays the author’s wide sympathy, her
ability to free herself from her sex and see
life steadily from the aloofness of the true
artist. It would have been hailed as a great
book had it come from an unknown pen ;
but May Sinclair had led us to expect a
greater. Yet she has given us a remarkably
convincing character in the person of the
“bad” husband, and despite the wife’s
irritating “purity” she contrives to keep
us interested in her to the end. Anne is a
product of the English country vicarage ; her
religion to her is of infinitely .greater im-
portance than the happiness of her husband.
It seems impossible to conceive an Australian
Anne ; the type is purely English.
/
When Anne, upon her honeymoon, makes
the great discovery that her husband,
Majendie, has had an “ affair ” years before,
disregarding the common-sense advice of
her husband to go back to her warm bed,
she prays.
Never before had Anne hurled herself into the
heavenly places in turbulence and disarray. It
had been her wont to come, punctual to some holy,
fore-appointed hour, with firm hands folded, with
a back that, even in bowing, preserved its pride ;
with meek eyes, close-lidded; with breathing
hushed for the calm passage of her prayer ; her-
self marshalling the procession of her dedicate!
thoughts, virgins all, veiled even before their
God.
Now she precipitated herself, with clutching
hands thrown out before her ; with hot eyes that
drank the tears of their own passion ; with the
shamed back and panting mouth of a Magdalen;
with memories that scattered the veiled pro-
cession of the Prayers.
And then it seemed that the storm that had
gone over her had rolled her mind out before her,
like a sheet of white-hot iron. There was a re-
cord on it, newly traced, of things that passion
makes indiscernible under its consuming and as-
piring flame. at the falling of the flame,
the faint characters flashed into sight,upon the
blank, running in waves, as when hot iron changes
from white to sullen red. Anne felt that her union
with Majendie had made her one with that other
woman, that she shared her memory and her shame.
For Maj end ie’s sake she loathed her womanhood
that was yesterday as sacred to her as her soul.
Through him she had conceived a thing hitherto
unknown to her —a passionate consciousness and
hatred of her body. She hated the hands that
had held him, the feet that had gone with him,
the lips that had touched him, the eyes that had
looked at him to love him. Him she detested, not
so much on his own account, as/because he had
made her detestable to herself.
That is conceivably the only possible
attitude an English country vicarage girl
could take ; but any other wife would after-
wards simmer down. The “sin” had
occurred in the past: she would contrive
to make the best of the present. But—
possibly because her name was -Axme :
nothing can be done (in literature)
a woman of the name of Anne—she does
not simmer down. She takes re _g
in her religion, and overlooks tne
giveness which that religion enjoins. *
ultimately, by her very piety, she dnveaher
long-suffering, and still lovmg, hus
again to the very sin she had hoped to enable
him to ‘ ‘ forget. ” Anne suffered from excess
of religion, and though the pathos °i der
position was very real to her, it was a P atho
self-created. To be candid, Anne lacked
common-sense. . , , .
The other characters m the book are
drawn with that fine touch that we have
learnt to expect from May Sinclair, par-
ticularly human is “ the spinal lady, Jtditn
with the spinal disease :
Edith Majendie was a loving but shrewd ob-
server of the people of her world. Lying on her
back, she saw them at an unusual angle, almost as
if they moved on a plane invisible to persons who
go about upright on their legs. _ The four walls of
her room concentrated her vision in bounding 1 •
She saw few women and fewer men, but she saw
them apart from those superficial activities whic
distract and darken judgment. Faces that she
was obliged to see bending over her had another
aspect for Edith than that which they presented
to the world at large. ... It was better to lie on
a couch than stand on a pedestal; you knew, at
least, where you were.
And May Sinclair talks—for a writing woman
—surprisingly good sense about the question
of the sexes, in particular about the infidelity
of loving husbands. Here is a sample of the
straight talk one of the men gives Anne,
when it is almost too late :
“You good women are apt to runaway with
the idea that—that this sort of # thing is tremen-
dously important to us. It isn’t.”
“ Then why behave as if it were ? ’’
“We don’t. That’s your mistake. Ten to one,
when a man’s once married and happy, he doesn t
think about it at all. Of course, if he isn’t happy
—but, even then, he doesn’t go thinking about it
all day long. The ordinary man doesn t. He s
got other things to attend to —his business, his
profession, his religion, anything you like. These
are the important things, the things tie thinks
about, the things that take up his time.”
“ I see. I see. The woman doesn’t count.”
“Of course she counts. But she counts in
another way. Bless you, the woman may be his
religion, his superstition. In your husband’s case
it certainly was so.”
Her face quivered.
“Of course,” he said, “ what beats you is—how
a man can love his wife with bis whole heart and
soul, and yet be unfaithful to her. ...”
And very plainly this rude man tells her :
“If you want to get down to the root of it, it’s
as simple as hunger and thirst. When you’re not
thirsty you don’t think about drinking. When
you are thirsty, you do. And sometimes —not
always—when you can’t get clean water, you’ll
drink water that’s—not so clean. Though you
may be very particular. Mind you, the more
particular a man is, the thirstier he’ll be. And,
supposing he can never get a drop of water at
home, and, every time he goes out, some kind
person offers him a drink —can you blame him
very much if, some day, he takes it ? ”
The real trouble with Anne was that she
was not spanked. The tragedies of all
wives named Anne—and of several who are
not named Anne—would never have hap-
pened if they had only regularly been
spanked. But if Majendie had spanked
Anne, we would not have had a fine novel. —
F 2. In the end, tardily, Fate came along
and did the spanking with a satisfactory
thoroughness.

Murray issues two remarkably cheap reprints of

George W. Lambert was recently afforded two
indications that, in a 'popular sense, he ha 3
“arrived” as an illustrator and artist in England.
Harmsworth’s offered him, at big prices, the illus-
tration of a number of their new sixpenny editions,
and Pearson’s wished to give first place in the
magazine to an illustrated article upon his work.
Lambert declined both offers.
T. Fisher Unwin (1 Adelphi-terrace, London)
offers another one hundred guinea prize for a
“first novel.” All MSS. must be typewritten;
all stories must be more than 75,000 words, and
preferably less than 100,000; and novels, accom-
panied by an explanatory letter, and remittance
to cover postage for return, will be received up
till April 30th, 1908. Meantime T. 0. Lothian
(Melb.) offers a £lOO prize for the best Australian
novel.

LIGHT VERSE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Maudie’s Lament.
We wander on the crinkling yellow sands,
Beside the sea.
Paul’s poet heart with ecstasy expands—
He raves to me ;
Points out the cloud effects, with eager hands,
Exultingly.
Together do we tread the ti-tree shade,
Some lines he quotes
Of Shelley, or some bard of lofty grade,
Whose golden notes
Were sweetly piped, to gladden man and maid.
Or he devotes
His thoughts to dreamings, as beside him I
Walk on apace,
With downcast heart, or just a smothered sigh,
While smooth my face
Appears to his regard. I trust the lie
Is done with grace.
We linger in the twilight, and his voice
Grows gentler still.
The charmed hour for lovers to rejoice
His soul does fill
With thoughts exalted and with dreams most choice,
But lacking thrill.
The moonlight patterns on the path begin
My sense to tease ;
But Paul enraptured tries my ear to win
With ecstasies
To Nature all the time —think what a sin !
He’s on his knees.
Our sleeves e’en brush. My hand is close to his,
Yet he’s content
To poetise right on. He thinks it bliss
To give thought vent,
To scorn emotion, and from hearts dismiss
Sweet sentiment.
I earn nice compliments for sympathy—
We are in tune ;
But, oh ! there’s nothing new, it seems to me,
Beneath the moon.
We walk for hours, and talk of poets dree,
And never spoon.
VIC.
E.S.
“This is Lamb!”
In this deadly moral Age,
When the Righteous ramp and rage,
If you find your little frolic
Marked taboo,
Take a lesson, friend, to-day,
Ere your chance has passed away,
From that artist of the Ages,
From the Jew.
In his diet, as you know,
He must certain things forego
And avoid the flesh of porker,
Must the Jew;
He may eat of beef his fill
And on mutton do his will,
But the thrice-entrancing bacon
Must eschew.
But Ben Adhem in the night
Saw a vision all of light
And it gave him cunning counsel
In this guise ;
If thy soul for ham doth burn
If for pork thy palate yearn—
Go—baptise thy bacon Mutton,
And be wise.
Brother Gentile—ls it true
When thy meat is marked taboo
By the Censors who thy little
Fun would damn
Thou dost sometimes change a name
And baptise forbidden game
With that good old Hebrew label—
“ This is Lamb ”?
QUEENSLAND.
FURNESS BORN.
Madam Moneybags.
Her lips were laughter-laden,
Her eyes were stars afire ;
Of ev’ry earthly maiden
She was my heart’s desire !
Her ways were sweet and childlike,
Symbolical of Love;
On me she ever smiled like
An angel from above.
We used to fondly talk of
The sun, and moon, and stars
When ours was like the walk of
The gods on Luna’s bars !
She said the crimson sunset
Was grand to gaze upon;
I trow there wasn’t one set
But thro’ her tresses shone !
But ah ! a Goth who traded
In tallow, wool, and hides,
My Love’s affections raided
With dollars for his guides.
His ruse spelt doubt and danger
For me, and so to-day
She met me as a stranger—
And looked the other way !
They talk of hides and tallow,
Of “ trusts,” and “bulls,” and “bears” ;
The world to them is fallow
Of all save stocks and shares.
Her lips are laughter-laden
For Moneybags, Esquire—
And yet of every maiden
She was my heart’s desire !
N.S.W.

How to Address Letters.
Con espondents to The Bulletin would oblige the paper
{and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor
out to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “copy.” Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
ofiicials ._ The person may not be there; the official
always is.

lishers. The right to reproduce or translate any part of
the contents of this number is hereby expressly reserved by
the publishers.

social, or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or Sketches

acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper Clippings,
communications will be regarded as strictly confi-
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed But no
Mo. 'will be returned, or preserved, unless these conditions
are observed. When MS. is forwarded viithout stamps
for return it i will be understood that the copy is to be
destroyed if not acceptable. Personal applications for
return of MS. cannot receive attention. The Bulletin
desires, further, those sending MS. to distinctly under-
stand that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Made and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous
or other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient
merit and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or
any interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduction
—t.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be paid
for as a suggestion only. The conditions under which
such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, NOVEMBER 21, 1907.
A STRIKE THAT NEEDN’T HAVE BEEN.
The N. S. Wales public played with fire, and now
its fingers are burned. Day after day, pitilessly,
relentlessly, the papers attacked the Arbitration
Act. When a hole was found in it they prised it
open. They invented flaws in it, and dwelt
malignantly upon them. They pointed out how
it could be evaded. When it was evaded they
magnified the evasion. They never suggested,
when a hole was discovered, that it should be filled
up; when an evasion was practised they never
suggested that it should be punished. They made
it their whole business to show that Arbitration
was “ impossible” and a failure. Employers, on
the whole, never liked the Act very much. The
system really is in the interest of the fair em-
ployer, because it prevents unfair competition on
the part of the sweater and the man who
makes his money by sharp practices. But that
virtue in it was carefully smothered ; and, deluded
with the bogey cry of Socialism—which is all
that Arbitration isn’t—the employers generally
accepted the word of the daily papers that Arbi-
tration is an interfering curse. It was easier to
convince them of that because in one or two cases
awards had not been as honorably accepted by
workmen as they ought to have been ; wherefore
the yarn was spread that Arbitration was a game
of two-up played with a double-headed penny : if
the workmen won, the employers had something
to lose, and therefore had to obey the award;
while if the workmen lost, they usually didn’t
have anything to lose, and they couldn’t be gaoled
in a mob ; also they couldn’t be made to work on
terms they didn’t like, wherefore no award was
really binding on them. So the Arbitration Act
was covered with contempt, and was turned into
a newspaper Aunt-Sally.
* * * *
And the public—the infinitely bigger public
which wasn’t a party to the actual disputes—
swallowed the daily paper misrepresentations with
its morning coffee, and either openly approved or
let the thing slide as not having anything to do
with its own pocket. Two of the bricks aimed at
the Act seemed to the general public (considering
that animal in the mass) to be quite fatal. One
was the yarn that you couldn’t enforce the
law, because the gaols wouldn’t hold an
army of two or three thousand workmen against
whom an award had been made, supposing
they wouldn’t abide by that award. The other
yarn was that you can’t force men to work under
conditions they don’t choose to accept. The
.P ubllc likes to see a fair deal, and it said
that this wasn't a fair deal. The fact, however,
was that the general public only saw what the
papers wanted it to see. It didn’t see that in
these respects this law differs from no other law.
Persistently throw mud at any Act, ridicule it
and bring it into contempt, and it cannot be en-
forced. Ihe average man does not obey the law
because there is a policeman on the beat and a
lock-up down the next back-lane. He bows
to it because he instinctively knows that obedience
is necessary to peace, order, good government,
and national prosperity. If 20,000 men in Sydney
made up their minds to-morrow that the butcher
was demanding too much for meat and the baker
too much for bread, the gaols would be just as
inadequate to hold that crowd if it pillaged the
shops as they are to hold the same-sized crowd of
strikers. Still nobody suggests that on that
account the law which protects the butcher and
the baker should be abolished. Nobody suggests
that, on account of the impossibility of gaoling
everybody who might possibly break the law, the
L f wis , . impossible” and a farce. That law is
obeyed in general, then, not because of the hand-
cuffs and legs-irons in the distance, but because
the public through generations has been taught to
respect the law, until now it does so instinctively.
* * * *
Instead, however, of encouraging respect for
this Arbitration law, and setting to work to cure
whatever faults experience found in it, the papers
aimed a daily handful of mud at it, and en-
couraged disrespect for it. And that is about the
worst use to which any paper can put its power.
How full of danger it is to tell the public that no
law need be respected which can’t gaol all the
people who break it, one Newcastle incident of
last week shows. The miners faced their strike
notoriously not very well supplied with funds,
.but, said their president—if the newspaper re-
ports can be believed—- 4< a battle was never won
with money. While there is food in the country
the men will take it if they are hungry, and
what is more, if necessary they will organise
to take it.” That is the first result of
hammering in the argument, day after day, that
one law was a farce, and couldn’t be enforced,
because the gaols wouldn’t stretch wide enough to
take in all who might brea.k it. That dangerous
argument, for which the people who have been
attacking the Arbitration Act are wholly re-
sponsible, has soaked in; and now the miners’
president applies it to some other law. If it was
a good argument as applied to one law, it was
good as applied to the other. As a matter of fact
it is a rotten argument all the time. Yet Peter
Bowling (assuming he has been correctly re-
P° l rte<i ) is neither more mischievous nor more
ridiculous than the Sydney Telegraph and
Herald and the rest have been.
* « * *
Peter Bowling was neither more ridiculous
nor more mischievous than those papers, because
while breaking down the Arbitration system’
they offered nothing in its place. Of course’
there is but one alternative, and that is precisely
what the public is getting—the strike. And now
that the public and the papers have got it, they
don t like it, and they squeal for an “ independent
tribunal. But an independent tribunal that
only arrives after the row has started, is liable to
find that it has to deal not with one trouble, but
with a dozen, and not with reasonable beings’ but
with men whom the lust of fight has made
bitter and furious. The process in such
cases is always the same. Once the row starts an
appeal has to be made to passions and prejudices.
The men have to be whipped up to the sticking
point; the story of their wrongs has to be painted
for them, and for those whose support they hope
to get, in black and crimson; small grievances
have to be magnified; they have to be told that
the eyes of the world of labor are upon them, and
that their battle is the battle of unionism The
other crowd has its own part to play. Its busi-
ness is to weaken the funds, and—the cold
brutality of it! make the cry of hungry wife and
child lash the unwilling workman back to the
collar. Even from the employers’ point of view,
it is a mad policy. Once the workman might
have been crushed by that flogging. To-day
b® i® only bitter, and he takes the remembrance
of it with him to the polling-booth. The
Fat employer, who knows that his coal won’t
waste during a strike, may lash the workman
into submission; but at the polling booth they
meet as equals. The employer and the capitalist
may think they can win strikes; but the fruit
of those victories will be verv bitter politics
btnkes became a dangerous weapon for Capital to
have anything to do with when universal suffrage
came. °
* * * *
Apart from the difficulty of getting at the true
tacts of the case once the row has been allowed
to start, there is the difficulty of getting an°ry
bitter men to agree to terms, or to abide by
agreements. Premier Wade last week proposed
a tribunal. The 8. M. Herald, approving of
the creation of a hurried scratch court after
it had helped to destroy one which had
been constituted deliberately, said that, even
it the parties did not accept its findings the
latter would “give the public a tremendous
weapon against the strikers or the masters as the
case may be,” so the two of them might “as well
face the inevitable.” In other words, the S M
Herald, having tried a strike, is driven back upon
this : that public opinion, shaped by the findings
of an impartial court, must, after all, prevail.
Exactly! Public opinion, thus arrived at, must
be supreme. But its supremacy ought not to be
left to chance ; and it is left to chance so long as
the public opinion is not embodied in an award
of a court, which, if necessary, will be backed up
by the whole power of the country. The Arbi-
tration Act set out to do in an orderly way what
it is now admitted must be done, even if, for want
of time, it has to be done in a disorderly way.
And so the one alternative—the strike—having
been tried and proved intolerable, the whole hostile
public attitude with regard to Arbitration must
inevitably be thrown overboard, and a new and
saner one adopted.
SOME DEFENCE CONSIDERATIONS.
Rents are cheap in a Fools’ Paradise. But
security has to be bought with dear money. The
Australian Government, having decided to take
the first steps towards enforcing a system of uni-
versal training for military service, must face the
fact that this re-organisation of the defences on a
sound system will call for money. By altering
the basis of service from pay to patriotism,
and abolishing the system of bribing citizens
to do their duty, an enormous saving in cost will
be made (Australia could never hope to support
a huge standing army or a paid militia represent-
ing anything like a reasonable proportion of its
manhood); but even by following the rational
economical Swiss system of unpaid, universal
training for service, there must be a big increase
in our present defence responsibilities. A fight-
ing force of at least 250,000 must be aimed at; the
provision of rifles, should be of the
simplest and least expensive) and the payment of
the necessary permanent staff to carry on the
organising work, will assuredly make the mili-
tary Estimates very much larger than in the past.
But the spirit in which the position should be
faced is that security must be obtained, in spite
of the disorganising influence of the cheap rents
m the Fools' Paradise.
* * -* *
Many indications give promise that that spirit
can be evoked. Present Defence Minister Ewing
is converted to a faith in universal service. It is
on his orders that one of the most capable
and intelligent officers of the forces is now
drawing up plans showing the adaptation of
such a system to Australian conditions. The
Prime Minister has expressed himself publicly—
in London more decidedly than here—in favor of
universal service, though probably his irritatingly
philosophic mind still weighs small scruples of
doubts as to whether it should be pushed on with
m Australia now. A majority of the Cabinet is
understood to favor the same system; almost
certainly a majority of Parliament does. And
the principle cause gains friends with every
election and every debate. All this represents
great progress since The Bulletin first brought
the subject into prominence, and the National
Defence League, in the Constituencies, and W. M.
Hughes, in Parliament, began the agitation for
compulsory universal service. Now the idea of
buying defence with subsidies has been practi-
cally abandoned; the patriotic duty of the
citizen to help to defend his country, not
for pay as a means of livelihood, but as
a matter of right, has been generally recognised.
Ail this gives reasons for hope. What remains in
doubt is the sincerity of the Cabinet, and the
question of its desire and ability to take a big and
a sure step forward. The cause could be verv
easily ruined at the present juncture by the adop-
tion of some half-hearted expedient or of some
basically unsound method of organisation. Sin-
cerity and practicality are the essentials; and it is
more important that a small, safe, sure step
forward should be made than an ill-considered
and hasty rush made for a position which would
have to be afterwards abandoned.
* * » *
The ultimate ideals of national defence are
That every physically fit man in the country should be
able to take his place in the defence ranks either on land
or sea in case of danger.
That the uniform should be the least that is necessary
to distinguish the soldier, when he is on duty, from the
citizen.
That the warships and equipment should represent the
last word of modern military science, and should be
manufactured exclusively in Australia.
That the citizen-soldier called upon for the minimum
amount of training-service should be taugh t to regard that
service as a matter of citizen duty, deserving no monetary
reward, but that the extra service required from non-
commissioned and commissioned officers, and specialised
units, such as engineers and gunners, should be fairlv
recognised. J
That promotion should be strictly by merit, without
reference to social rank.
That the necessary permanent; staff should be kept
to the lowest degree possible consistent with efficiency
but should be paid at such good rates as to com-
mand the best of talent, and should have the advantage
of a fair pension system, so that fairly early retirements
might allow of quick promotions.
Mostly the above state only the obvious. But
they represent all the principles necessary for a
sound defence system ; and not one of them can
be safely neglected. Every step taken should
be towards the ultimate end which they repre-
sent ; and the closest consideration should be
given now to the financial arrangements necessary
for this radical re-organisation.
* * * *
In the past, Australia has spent a ridiculously
inadequate sum on defence. An extraordinary run
of good luck has kept that carelessness from being
disastrous, but has in no wise vindicated it. For
the future, more, much more, must be spent, and
the time is fitting now to consider the
sources from which the extra money can be
recruited. The taxation of the people must of
course, represent the main source. Taxation is
always unpleasant—as is the payment of life and
fire insurance premiums. But, when itis necessary
the unpleasantnessm us tbe put up with. The burden
of defence expenditure may, however, be lessened
very much if wise statesmanship consents to take
lessons from other countries which eke out the
resources of taxation by State monopolies. The
nationalisation of the Tobacco Monopoly in Aus-
tralia has been already much discussed. It might
well form a part of a defence reorganisation
scheme. A State Tobacco Monopoly would not
only help the revenue without burdening the
people, but it would provide an excellent
means of pensioning off aged military and
civil servants (as is done in France). A Common-
wealth Alcohol Monopoly suggests another easy
source of revenue, and it could be made at the
same time a potent engine of social reform.
These are not really extraneous issues. The posi-
tion is that Australia is threatened with national
extinction by the overflowing Asiatic wave;
that within a very short time a navy and an army
must be created at necessarily great expense ; and
that, to carry out the work without unduly
burdening the people, all possible means of rein-
forcing the Treasury must be made available.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Police Court Muck-Battling.
There is need in N.S. W. for a Minister of Justice
strong enough to deal stringently with the appall-
ing Sydney police-court solicitor, his awful clients,
and the magistrates who tolerate them. Every
day the lower courts are besmirched by cases
wherein the parties and their lawyers muck-battle
unchecked, and in defiance of all decency. The
litigation arises, often enough, over trifling
causes. The city police court, as it exists,
offers a useful field for the ventilating
of bitter family hates. Brown has quarrelled, in
the past, with his neighbor Smith, and the rift
has never healed. A shadow of an opportunity
crops up, and Brown takes Smith to Court, or
vice versa. Ostensibly the issue is as stated on
the charge sheet; in reality the moral and other
characters of Brown, Smith, and such of their
relations and friends as can be induced into the
witness box, are at stake. It is a dis-
tinguishing mark of these cases that in cross-
examination there is no question too outrageous
and irrelevant for the hideous police-court solici-
tor to ask. Conventionally secure by the fiction
of legal practice, which lays down that, what-
ever a client tells his lawyer to be true in regard
to a witness must by the lawyer be accepted as
true, this class of practitioner hurls the mud he
is hired to hurl with enthusiasm. His “questions”
are in effect so many assertions. Every moral
degradation is imputed to whomsoever happens
/° r * e ot^er side.” The imputations
(wild, preposterous charges of criminality mostly)
may not have a vestige of foundation. The point
is that a proportion of them will be recorded in
the evening papers that afternoon, to the confusion
of the victims, which is the main object sought by
those inspiring them. Everybody in court recog-
nises this, but no one seems to mind. The magis-
trate—some of him, at any rate—has his little
joke on the subject, whereat the police laugh
dutifully, and the reporter wearily resumes his
shorthand. Then the case over, the litigants trail
homewards with such characters as they have
beenspared. A measure of blame in the matter
may be laid at the door of our cross-examination
system. It assumes that the cross-examiner will
always be more or less reputable, or that the
presiding magistrate will have sufficient strength
to control him when he gets beyond bounds. But
the defender of a system that is found to work
well elsewhere may fairly urge that when a
magistrate fails to preserve the decencies in
his court it is Parliament’s duty to step in
and put him where he won’t be responsible for
further maladministration of justice. The incor-
porated Law Society might consider the cases of
solicitors employing such methods as have been
instanced, and debate whether that line of con-
duct accords with the Society’s standard of ethics.
And then there are the people who litigate for
trivial causes, with the object of blackening each
other’s characters. Probably nothing will dis-
courage them but the passing of a Bill authorising
a magistrate to fine parties who waste the State’s
time and money hurling irrelevant muck at each
other through the agency of a “gentleman by Act
of Parliament.”

The following correspondence is interesting (lest
it should be considered a violation of confidence
we suppress the name of the advertising firm
which didn’t renew): —
Representatives of Indian and Colonial Newspapers and
Advertising Agents.
D. J. Keymer and Co., 3 Whitefriars-street,
__ „ , London, E.C., 11th October, 1907.
The Manager, the Sydney Bulletin,
George-street, Sydney, N.S.W.,
Australasia.
Dear Sir.—We have been applying to our friends Messrs,
for a renewal order for advertisements in your
paper, and they have written us as follows
“ Owing to the increased Australian tariff, which affects
us considerably, we regret that we are unable to consider
the renewal of our advertisement in the Sydney Bulletin.
We suggest that it might be advisable for you to com-
municate with the proprietors of The Bulletin with a
view to inducing them to use any influence they can to
create as much opposition as possible against this high
tariff, especially as they will suffer from the reduced
advertising which is sure to result from the imposition of
such an onerous tariff.”
We thought that perhaps the above remarks might be
of interest to you ; we regret very much not being able
to obtain the renewal order.
Yours faithfully,
D. J. KEYMER & CO.,
L. D. Falk.
MEMORANDUM FROM “THE BULLETIN,” SYDNEY.
214 George-street, 14th Nov., 1907.
To Messrs. D. J. Keymer and Co.
3 Whitefriars-street, London, E.C.
Dear Sirs.— Re your favor of October 11th.
We would be glad if you would inform your friends,
Messrs. -- — , that The Bulletin believes that a
high tariff is necessary in Australia ; and that it would
not dream of suggesting any modification in the tariff in
order to serve some business advantage of its own.
We thank you for your attention to our interests in
writing us on the matter.
Yours sincerely,
WM. MACLEOD,
Managing Director.
This sort of threat addressed to other papers may
account for some of the frenzied press opposition
to an Australian tariff. The Bulletin prints
the correspondence for the public good, not with
any offensive assumption of virtue. It is, after all,
not so much virtue as common sense to refuse
£lOO for your honesty when to remain honest
means £lOOO or more. But the pity is that others
don’t seem to recognise the fact always.

The New Protection.
Practically all the Victorian agricultural imple-
ment makers have closed down their works, in
consequence of the decision of Mr. Justice
Higgins in the Excise exemption application.
The argument in justification is that the industry
will not pay the wages granted by Justice Higgins,
and live. If this is true, it can be easily proved.
All that is necessary is for the manufacturers to
hand their books over to the Court, and, if the
figures bear out the statement, higher Protection
Defenceless.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They say that Land is rich whose argosies
Bring in of wealth and goods a mighty tale,
Although her sons slink round with knocking
knees,
And never breast a gale.
They tell us, if our city hutches swarm,
And rear a puny race, whose noble aim
Is but to keep outside a uniform,
And back a gambler’s game ;
That such a land, in sooth, may wealthy be,
And proof thereof they give in lengthy lines
That tell a tale of millions in the sea,
Of fleeces, flocks, and mines.
And yet—on second thoughts—it seems to me
The land that prizes not a manly breed,
And takes no care of human husbandry—•
That land is poor indeed !
If work be wanting in the human hive,
Can countless foreign imports carry cheer?
If Manhood wilt—can Man uplift and thrive,
Or fight when foemen near ?
We flout the Mongol—but the Mongol comes;
Yestreen he broke the Cossack’s weighty spear;
To-day he proudly beats a myriad drums,
And nations hark—and fear.
And we, who boast us sprung from Britain free,
Who share her ancient pride of derring-do—
We cannot cast one gun to guard our sea,
Or shape one rifle true.
And should some sea disaster cut us off
From far Britannia’s vague protective plan,
To get us arms, our hat we needs must doff
And beg them—from Japan ;
Or from some other half-enlightened land
Whose simple children do not seem to know
How noble ’ tis like helpless sheep to stand
While o’er them sweeps the foe !

PRO PATRIA.


can be given. The object of Protection is not in
the mam to create a class of wealthy manufactu-
rers (though it should seek to give the manu-
facturer reasonable profits), but to build
up a nation of strong, well-fed, self-
reliant people ; and any system of Protection
that fails to do that misses its purpose. That
nation-building is impossible on a wage of six
shillings per day is obvious, and an industry that
can only pay such wages isn’t a desirable national
industry in the full sense. It needs to be improved
or abolished. In this case Australia cannot afford
to abolish it, therefore it must be improved.
But the onus of showing that higher Protection
is wanted falls on the shoulders of those in the
industry, as they have the information in their
business books. The idea of appealing to the
High Court to declare the Excise Act unconstitu-
tional is simply foolish, for if it is declared so the
chances are that the Protection necessary to
enable the local industry to live in the face of the
competition of the American Trust, will be swept
away. The present Australian Parliament is solid
on the fact that protected industries shall pay fair
and reasonable wages; and for the manufacturers to
fight that determination is folly. The sensible
course of action is to accept that fact, and
settle down to demonstrate beyond refutation
exactly what rate of Protection is necessary to
enable them to pay the wages that have been
declared by the Federal Arbitration Court to be
fair and reasonable. The other course can only
end in disaster.

A Lesson.
If there were any suspicion of “ pose ” about it,
the Melbourne Cup-Day attitude of Prime Minister
Deakin and his wife wouldn’t be worthy of any
serious notice. But neither can be suspected of
pose; a nervous dread of publicity, rather, is
their mutual characteristic. So it’s worth while
Australia getting a little sermon (to be taken as
preached) from the text that, on Melbourne Cup
Day—the greatest holiday of the Commonwealth —
Alfred Deakin stuck to his work in the Australian
Parliament, and Mrs. Alfred Deakin nursed
babies at the Women’s Exhibition. That Aus-
tralians are a pleasure-loving people is often
brought forward as a reproach. It has no claim to
be considered as a reproach unless to the “plea-
sure-loving ” can be prefixed “ unduly.” A love
of pleasure is a good thing in a nation. It is a
sign of sanity and of healthy optimism; it is a
means of preserving a reserve-fund of national
vitality for a crisis. The object of life, after
all, is to live, and there is no real living in such
existence as is mapped out for us by gloomy
apostles of Purityranny. But —isn’t there a sus-
picion that at the present time Australia might
be said to be “unduly pleasure-loving”? When
it is remembered that the time is one of grave
anxiety; that the sands run rapidly out to give the
signal for a struggle for very existence —there
seems reason to suggest, for the national good, a
little less horse-racing, a little more attention to the
serious things of life. Prime Minister Deakin,
attending to the tariff which is to give the Austra-
lian more work, more defence, more chance of
attracting desirable brother citizens, and turning
his back to do so on the carnival of pleasure (and
the debauchery of roguery) which the Cup repre-
sents, should suggest some long, serious thoughts
to his fellow citizens. Mrs. Deakin, neglecting
the claims of Fashion and piquant Folly to devote
herself to the cradle, should reinforce those
thoughts strongly, in the minds of women par-
ticularly. In truth, we can do with a little more
attention to the factory and the cradle, a little
less to the racecourse, in our Commonwealth.

The Jap Cleans His Slate.
Reijiro Wakatsuki, lately Japanese Vice-Minis-
ter of Finance, and now Imperial Financial Com-
missioner, has written a memorandum which shows
that the Jap is humping a burden that is calcu-
lated to take some of the crimp out of his tail.
Converting Monkey money into Bull currency at
10 yen = £l, the Jap revenue from taxes was
£9,300,000 in 1898; for this year the Budget esti-
mates a revenue of £26,900,000, or nearly three
times as much. There are no positive figures,
Wakatsuki admits, concerning incomes; but
taking the amount of non-taxable incomes (under
£3O a year) to total the same as the taxable in-
comes, then—
Japan is paying- only 24 per cent, out of her income, in
order to support the Government, to pay off the debt, to
educate her children, and what not.
Wakatsuki is amazed at the moderation of the
claim of the country upon the people. True, he
says, he has left out something :
There is indeed also the amount of local taxation for
counties, municipalities, towns, and villages, but that
does not come up to 30 per cent, of the National tax ; so
that the whole burden of tax, national and local put
together, on the shoulders of the people, is only some-
thing like 30 per cent, of their income.
Then there are certain State undertakings, “the
revenue from which may be regarded as partaking
of the nature of a tax ” ; but, allowing for those,
“ the percentage of the taxes will not turn out to
be more than 35 percent, of the people’s income.”
And again Wakatsuki is amazed. “Why,”he says,
“ before the Restoration the people had to ear-
mark half their income to pay the taxes ! ” So
nobody need be frightened, he concludes in effect
—Japan will do it all right. And perhaps it will.
It is just because it is most likely that the-man-
with-the-name-that-is-getting-monotonous is right
that The Bulletin feels an iceberg creeping down
its spine. For while Japan is doing things like
this, men talk in Australian Trades and Labor
Councils:—
Fight ? I’ve got nothing to fight for. If any foreign
invaders come, they can have it. I’m not going to boost
up things for somebody who’ll stand on a hill 10 miles
away and give orders.
And at the same moment, in an Australian Par-
liament, on the strength of a rare surplus which
wasn’t a real surplus after all, the Treasurer was
proposing to cut down taxation, so that, among
other things, nobody who hasn’t an income of
£lOOO need pay any income-tax at all. In
1900-1 Coghlan computed that N. S. Wales
incomes amounted to £64,000,000 (no computation
has apparently been made since); and last year the
total N.S. W. revenue from taxation was £4,403,056,
including £3,022,351 from the Commonwealth
Customs; so that, even allowing only the same
income last year as in 1900 (and it was probably a
third more), the taxation amounted to less than 7
per cent., or, including Commonwealth taxation,
it was only 8 per cent. Now even that 8 per cent,
is being reduced. Japan has undertaken to wipe
out the whole of its war debt in less than 30
years, and so far has done in that matter more
than it undertook to do ; and it is paying off the
cost of its railways—that is to say, it is wiping off
the railway debt, and having the railways to the
good. On the other hand, N. S. Wales, the Ma
btate of the Australian group, at the very moment
that it is cutting dovyn its taxation below 8 per
cent., announces that it this year proposes to go
deeper into debt to the extent of about £2,500,000.
Japan knows that in any great struggle of the
future it will have to borrow, and borrow heavily.
No war can be fought on taxes : to carry through
even the little Boer War, Great Britain had to
pile up its debt from £635,040,000 in 1899 to
£798,349,000 in 1903. In the time of supreme
trial the country that cannot borrow is lost.
Japan prepares for that time by wiping the slate.
And Australia —well, Australia goes piling up its
debt; goes on advertising itself as the country
which cuts down its poor 8 per cent, of taxation
at the very moment that it adds something more
to a burden of debt which in bad times is already
intolerable. Nothing is more certain than that
the country that cannot borrow cannot put up
much of a fight under modern conditions, yet in
peace Australia is exhausting its ability to borrow
—while up North is Japan, thinking no sacrifice
too grea.t to clear the old lumber out of the road,
so that it may go to the world’s Cohens with the
indescribable advantage of a clean slate.

The Years Eoll On.
Richard Arthur, M.L.A., writes
Nearly four years have gone since the guns at Port
Arthur thundered their menace and warning to the
people of Australia. But Australians have given no
sign. Pour precious, inconceivably valuable years have
gone, years in which a nation bidden to hear its flat
of death might have armed itself to the teeth and awaited
defiant and unafraid the coming of the worst. But we
have done nothing. In four years, we have not added
20,000 to our garrison from outside Australasia. We have
turned deaf ears to the call for universal service. We
have built no fortress and mounted no guns. Our naval
defence is but the fabric of a dream.
Yet there has not been wanting further warning
through these years. The ominous retreat of the Im-
perial battleships from the Pacific and the Mediterranean,
the ready assent to the proposal that the Australian
naval subsidy should be withdrawn, the insistence on her
treaty rights by Japan to the United States, the transfer
of th<* American fleet to the Pacific, the flooding of Van-
couver by Mongolians—are these not writings on the
wall that the blindest and most ignorant might read?
Pour years hence, the Australian people will be as they
are to-day. It needs no prophet to proclaim this.
Then, as now, they will be awaiting, with feverish
anxiety, Tattersall’s numbers, and another English
cricket team. There will be no difference except
that they will be four years nearer their doom.
Their blind politicians will still be found denouncing the
iniquity of immigration in their Parliaments, and affirm-
ing vehemently that this free people will never submit to
the yoke of militarism. We will have our De Blocks to
prove conclusively “the impossibility” of war and
invasion; Peace Society fanatics, and dreamers in
the efficacy of Hague Conferences will hail the
near approach of the millennium. And we will
clutch eagerly at their doctrines, lest some glim-
mering of the things that are should shatter our
dreams of ease and mirth, and reveal to us the gulf of
despair that yawns before us.
Add to these another four years, or yet another, and, in
all human probability, the concrete tragedy will have
begun. How it will come, no one can say. But whether
pacifically, or by blood and iron, when Asia begins to
flood this empty land, another infinitely tragic and heart-
rending drama will have been written in the pages of
history.

The Coal Strike.
After N.S.W. Premier Wade had talked things
over with the two sides to the Newcastle trouble,
he drew up a scheme for submitting the whole
dispute to arbitration—all except Hebburn. The
men were to undertake to go back, and the owners
were to undertake to put them back, in every mine
except Hebburn. This special treatment of Heb-
burn is in itself enough to suggest that Hebburn is
Newcastle’s barrel of gunpowder that needs very
careful handling. Yet there is nothing special
about Hebburn in the matter of the selling or
hewing rate, and this Newcastle trouble is sup-
posed to be all about the selling and hewing rate.
The owners agreed to put up tne selling rate Is. a
ton from January 1, which meant fourpence more
per ton for the miners; the miners wanted the
selling rate put up 2s. per ton, so that they could get
eightpence per ton more. Hebburn was not
interested in that more than any other mine.
But Hebburn has coal-cutting machines, and the
miners refused to work with them, on the ground
that possible electric “sparking” was dangerous
in a mine where gas had been found. At the
back of the owners’ minds, to be arrived at by a
little tunnelling, is the belief that the coal-cutting
machines are at the root of the whole trouble. If
they could afford to be perfectly frank—
which parties rarely can be when a fight
has started —they would say that the new
machinery is what the miners aim their brick
at. To the objection that nothing that the
miners have said can be quoted in support of that,
the frank proprietor would reply that the miners
are too politic to say anything so unpopular. It is
just about as certain as anything can be, then,
that the owners—or their chief representatives—
believe that they are fighting for the right to
use this modern machinery, and that the selling
and hewing rate trouble is largely a pretext. If
however, they were asked for evidence, they would
probably be driven to confess that they hadn’t
got anything definite : it is just a vague, misty
general idea. The superior employer-person
gets a lot of smug self-satisfaction in assuming
that the benighted workman is opposed to ma-
chinery ; but the truth is in most cases (there are,
no doubt, lamentable exceptions) that what the
workmen object to is that they don’t get a
“fair cut” of the extra profits that eorne from
modern inventions and methods. And even apart
from the machinery side of the question the
Newcastle miner is undoubtedly moving along
towards the idea that the producer is entitled to
all the product. You can call him Socialist
or what you like, but he still refuses to see why his
own share of the selling price of the coal should be
so small. Coal companies have paid 20 per cent,
dividends on their capital, and he wants to know the
reason. That idea undoubtedly sticks in the men’s
minds, and grows; and the demand for nationalisa-
tion of mines, so that nobody should pouch big
profits, also grows steadily. The demand for
eightpence more per ton instead of fourpence is a
practical effort to put his belief into action. But
it is quite likely that it isn’t the right way.
The leading mine-owners see that clearly, and
therefore they stipulate that no fresh questions
shall be raised in any arbitration proceedings.
That is a cunning move. If the miners can’t
prove that the world’s market for coal is strong
enough to stand 125., then the owners win. The
question whether the miner ought to get
more than 4d. of the extra shilling cannot
then be raised. The miner, in short, will
be barred from securing the increased reward that
he believes he is entitled to by any other process
than by increasing the selling price; and in the
natural order of things the mine-owners would not
fix the rate at 11s. if they believed they could get
12s. All of which explains why the owners, who
have not been friendly to arbitration as a general
thing, come along promptly now, and accept it on
the condition that questions that have not hitherto
been raised shall not be raised now.
Japan, Korea, and the United States.
A well-informed correspondent from Nagasaki,
Japan:—
Secretary Taft’s visit to the Japanese capital is over
and gone ; and the public mind here is now chiefly co
cer "?d with apprehending: the exact object of his mission
u decree of its attainment. It cannot be forgotten
that, though Mr. Taft’s last trip to the Orient was os ten-
flibly on a tour of inspection in the Philippines, he vet
found tune for a conference with the Tokyo authorities
so important in its nature that it involved the immediate
dispatch of a £5OO telegram to Washington. Whether
the present visit involved anything so important will not,
of course, be known for some time ; but, as the negotia-
tions of the visit of two years are believed to have
seriously affected the status of United States citizens in
K ° r< r a > the results of the present meeting with the Tokyo
officials will be watched with much interest by all
foreigners in the Orient.
It is known that for some time negotiations of a desul-
tory sort have been goingon between the Governments of
Japan and the Uniced States, with, as yet, no satisfactory
adjustment of the immigration problem. It will be re-
membered that color was given to this rumor some time
a B' c, » when the Japanese Minister of Foreign Affairs inti-
mated that Japan had been asked for acquiescence in a new
treaty formally excluding Japanese immigrants from the
United States, and that she had refused. It is averred
that since then Japan has been asked if she would con-
sent to a revision of the immigration clause in exchange
for America’s concession of the right of naturalisation to
Japanese domiciled in the United States, and that Japan
likewise declined this offer. The last move on the part of
the United States Cabinet is said to have been the pro-
posal to approve a new tariff union between Korea and
Japan in exchange for the latter’s consent to a new treaty
excluding immigrants; which, it is affirmed, the Japan-
ese also declined to entertain.
According to the opinion of those most likely to be
familiar with conditions in the Far East.it is much less
probable that the American Cabinet has made such a pro-
posal as the last-mentioned than it is that the Japanese
authorities would have hesitated to consider it; for at
the present time Japan is doubtless very anxious to win
the consent of the Powers to her formal annexation of
Korea. Nevertheless, American residents of the Far Ease
will remember that, should their National Government
make this move, it would be no more surprising
than what the Washington Cabinet has already done
in the same connection. It is well known that, in
the first clause of the treaty of 1883 between Korea and
the United States, it is stated that if either of the high
contracting parties is injured by a third party, the other
shall interfere with her good offices to effect an amicable
settlement; it practically guarantees the independence of
Korea, as other treaties do the integrity of China and the
provinces contiguous. But in November, 1905, when the
surrender of Korean independence was demanded at the
point of the sword, and an appeal in accordance with the
treaty was made to Washington, it was refused, and
the treaty approved by the American Congress, and
which could not be annulled without the consent
of that body, was abrogated by the American Cabinet.
The immediate result of this arbitrary action was that a
protectorate of Korea was enforced by Japan, with Korea’s
consequent loss of sovereignty and independence. Every-
one out here feels that Japan must have had some secret
understanding with the American Executive, else this
extraordinary procedure would not have been followed ;
and the lengthy and expensive dispatch which resulted
from Secretary Taft’s visit to Japan, two years ago, is
naturally connected with the solution of this mys-
tery. One rumor has it that America’s concession
of her Korean rights to Japan was the price she had to
pay for Japan’s acquiescence in the Portsmouth Treaty
without indemnity from Russia. At any rate, if the
Washington Cabinet can abrogate a treaty made with a
foreign Power without the consent of Congress, it may
very well be that the same Executive can exchange the
rights which American citizens now enjoy in Korea for a
settlement of the troublesome immigration question.
It is, however, the hope of all foreign residents of the
Far East that the United States will refrain from making
any further concessions to Japan in Korea, even though
by withholding them the final adjustment of the emi-
gration question should be indefinitely delayed.
If we are to judge from the sentiments of the
public press, Japan is desirous of hastening the
annexation of Korea, which she cannot formally
do without the consent of those Powers having
special treaties with Korea, guaranteeing specific
rights to their nationals resident in that country.
Chief among the rights so guaranteed in Korea
are those of ex-territoriality, placing foreign subjects
under the legal jurisdiction of their own con-
sular authorities ; and, furthermore, these treaties fix in
a general way the amount of Customs duty to be levied
on imported goods. In the enforced agreement of
November, 1905, Japan promises to respect the conditions
of these treaties and the independence of Korea.
The latter promise she has failed to keep ; and if so,
what reason is there to suppose she will ultimately
adhere to the former ? As the Japanese populace is,
apparently, rampant upon the annexation of Korea,
nothing is more natural than that the authorities might
seek to turn the attention of the disaffected masses from
the immigration agitation to find appeasement in the
glory, and freedom for spoliation, which formal annexa-
tion of Korea would bring. But American citizens
have already suffered serious financial losses through
their Government’s abrogation of the treaty with
Korea; and if they should now be handed over
to the tender mercies of a Japanese tariff and
taxation systems, the extinction of American trade
in Korea would be only a matter of time. The
material interests of the United States in Korea are
greater than those of any other nation ; and if the Ameri-
can Government could be persuaded to surrender the
advantages that these interests have secured, it would
not be so difficult for Japan to get the other Powers,
whose interests are less, to follow suit; for if the more
important interests are compliant, it would at least be
inconvenient for the lesser to refuse.
Wanted : “ Gentlemen’s Sons.”
This advt. recently appeared in some Maoriland
papers :
rpHE EASTERN EXTENSION TELEGRAPH CO.,
-*• Cable Bay, Nelson, has a few Vacancies for gen-
tlemen’s sons, between 16 and 18 years of age, to
qualify for appointments in the service ; matricu-
lated preferred. Apply, by letter, to
THE SUPERINTENDENT.
And thereon hangs a tale. For years it was
thought that the youngster who could get into
the Eastern Extension service was one of the
lucky ones of the earth. Many think so still, and
officially the idea is fostered —for obvious reasons.
Yet last year, when the staff at Nelson (M.L.)
petitioned for better treatment, it was alleged
that, of eight operators whose service ranged from
five years down to 20 months, the best-paid man
got £96 a year, and the worst-paid £4B. It was
further alleged that of three men, who had in turn
to take charge of the station, two were paid only
£72 a year; also that juniors, who had to take
turns at the instruments, got £24 a year—less than
10s. per week ; and there are deductions for pen-
sion, breakages, etc. In reply to the Nelson staff’s
petition, the general manager wrote: —
The argument they use—that their salaries are inade-
quate to meet the cost of living—opens up a very wide
question, and if we were to take it into consideration the
Board would probably have to revise the scale of pay
throughout the service. I cannot, therefore, see my way
to bring the matter before the directors. We may, later
on, have to transfer some, if not all, the men who signed
the letter, but nothing definite just now can be promised.
Presumably it is because the co. can’t take into
consideration whether its salaries are equal to the
cost of living or not that it wants “ the sons of
gentlemen”; the “gentlemen” won’t see their
sons starve, even if the co. won’t take into con-
sideration the matter of a living wage. Observe,
however, that the vague promise of a transfer
was held out; and that has been the co.’s enticing
bait for years—transfer to an “ Eastern” station,
where the pay is supposed to be something
Oriental in its magnificence. But though the
bait is still there, the Eastern Extension officer
finds that it is getting a harder job to reach and
swallow it. For the Eastern Extension has tasted
the cheap yellow operator, and its governing
officials have pronounced him “ the best opera-
tor in the world ”; so the old plums of the
service come more rarely the way of the young
white officer, and the pay is wretched —de-
graded by that yellow competition, which drags
down everything it touches.
Monopolies, trusts, combines, and all the rest of
that family are never good enough to he allowed
out of sight. Like Australia’s black brother
Combo, it is best to keep them in front of you, or
you are liable to get hurt, even when they have
been cooing in the most friendly way. The New-
castle coal-mine owners fixed up a combine, which
they called a vend, something over 12 months ago.
The object was to put up and keep up the price of
coal; and as the price of coal fixes the miners’
wages, the miners swallowed the vend whole, and
said it was excellent. But the existence of the
vend has consolidated the owners in a way never
before approached ; and when they jump on the
miners they now come down with one dull, undi-
vided thud. Moreover, they know their power,
and on that account stiffen their backs —they
know there is practically no danger this
time of this colliery and that one break-
ing away and granting the miners what
they ask, in the sure and certain knowledge
that the extra cost will come back in the shape of
trade they will be able to collar from their rivals.
That used to happen in the days of the mere loose
federation of coal-mine owners, and it made them
careful. To-day the miners find themselves bump-
ing against a great iceberg, and when they look up
they see that it is the same iceberg that they
poured their blessing upon quite lately ; and they
dimly realise now that a trust is always good
enough to mistrust.

It is hardly likely that those who plead for the
abolition (or at least reduction) of duties on
strawboard have the slightest intention of
posing as barrackers for sweating. But that
is what it boils down to. At present, behind the
protecting wall of a duty, the lowest wages paid
to an ordinary laborer in the Broadford Mills,
the home of the Australian strawboard industry,
are 365. per week of 48 hours, while experienced
workmen earn from £2 ss. to £3 per week of the
same number of hours. According to some of
the English paper journals, the wages paid in
Holland are the lowest, with the exception of those
in Japan, among manufacturing nations, and a case
was cited where a man, his wife, and three children
toiled 70 hours each per week in a strawboard mill
for the munificent wage of 28s. 3d. in English
money, for the whole family. The family system
is rampant in Ereetrade Holland, and during the
hours of toil the Dutch operative’s home is
empty, for from the grandfather to the baby,
they are toiling in the factory, struggling to make
sufficient to supply just a bare living. And if
the duties are taken off strawboard, the Australian
industry will have to either go down to the same
level, or go right out. Some remarks have been
made about a recent increase in the price of the
Australian strawboard. There is an increase, but
it goes into the pockets of the man on the land,
and not those of the people who make the straw-
board, and it was made weeks before the tariff was
announced. The farmers have raised the price of
the straw—the board makers’ raw material. In
this community, in which the man on the land is
everything and the manufacturer nothing, the
mere statement that the increase of price has gone
to the farmer should satisfy everybody. Australia
exists for the man on the land—when he can get
there.
“ J.Y.” writes dolefully from Maoriland :
The small Dominion with the big debt is getting into
difficulties ; which is bad, because it led the way in some
advanced legislation. State leaseholds, loans to farmers,
old-age pensions, Arbitration Courts, liberal labor laws,
and women’s franchise were all successfully passed into
law and run with marked success by dead and gone John
McKenzie and Seddon, whose chief failing was a mama
for borrowing and jingoism. Now, however, the State
leaseholder, helped by cheap loans from the Government,
has become an arrogant Tory person with a desire to buy
the earth and help the Massey crowd ; the Labor party
is dissatisfied ; the women want seats in Parliament; and
the pensioners are not satisfied with 10s. a week from the
State. Joseph Ward has his hands more than full, and
shows a wobbly inclination to amalgamate with Massey
and fall generally. In the meantime, in these fertile
islands, potatoes are £ll a ton, a 41b. loaf is 9d., mutton
and beef 6d. to lOd. a lb. (both the latter can be bought for
less money in the Smithfield Market in London than they
cost in Wellington), and house-rent is absurdly high.
This state of affairs is largely due to the fact that about
50 percent, of the population are non-producers of any-
thing that is useful. A small army of able-bodied men
is in Parliament, while another lot is struggling to get
there. Another large mob is made up of Inspectors of
Rabbits, or Weeds, or Stock, or Agriculture, or Crown
Lands, or Fish, or Abattoirs, or Dairies, or Something.
Another crowd is fillingin time as commission agents, swop-
ping things, while quite a number is inthe Bookmaking and
Betting profession. Some more just travel about the
Dominion from one race meeting to another (there are
about four race meetings a week in Maoriland) picking
winners. The remaining small section of the population
is endeavoring to produce enough foodstuff to keep the
Islands going, and from present appearances many are
likely to go short of enough to eat in the near future.

The S. M. Herald is coming on. It is now print-
ing a series of papers on “ British Mismanagement
in the Pacific Islands,” in which it allows the writer
to put British officialdom in the pillory :
From first to last this mischief has been wrought, not
by the folly or idleness or temerity of Britons on the
spot, but by the timidity, or the apathy, or, in many cases,
the deliberate neglect of the departments in England, by
the contemptuous and contemptible parochialism of men
who, from a London office, with a Londoner’s narrow
outlook (from Dovvning-street to Throgmorton-street, at
most), pretend to administer a world-wide Empire.
Well, what does the Herald propose to do about
it? For the same crowd who, with this ‘‘con-
temptuous and contemptible parochialism,” “pre-
tend to administer” the affairs of the South Seas,
also pretend to administer the affairs of Australia,
and the Herald takes its cue from them—in the
matter of being very friendly to the Jap, for
instance. What has the Herald done these last
few years to help Australia “buck up” against
“ the contemptuous and contemptible parochial-
ism ” ? Did it help Deakin ? Did it not belittle
both his office and himself ? Has it been whipping
up an Australian national sentiment, strong
enough to secure fair treatment from these paro-
chialists in London, or has it been a poor
paroehialist itself, filled with snarls at Australia ?

Arbitration has entered so deeply into the soul
of Riley, workers’ representative on the N.S.W.
Court, that in the Sydney Trades Council, the
other night, he scorned all defence preparations.
“ This council,” he said, “ should stand for arbi-
tration. To establish a military system here
would have the effect of creating a class that
will be against the industrial class.” Well, is
Mr. Riley prepared to go the whole logical
hog ? Will he propose that policemen be abol-
ished, the force disbanded, and the detectives
given a job at tract distributing? And, after
that, will he take the locks off his own doors and
the fastenings off his own windows ? There’s
nothing like being thorough in these matters. If
Mr. Riley is prepared to implicitly trust the Jap
not to be offensive, he can’t do less than place his
whole confidence also in his own countryman, W.
Sikes.

♦
Numerous “last days” are announced at
the Tivoli; .but what will happen when these
last moments have expired the bills don’t say.
Time was, and not long ago, when there was only
one music-hall in Australia, and Rickards could an-
nounce his coming star before it hove in sight;
but he has to be more careful these days. The
music halls have encouraged the art of mimicry and
of pretending to be somebody else, and now that art
is applied by one hall to rob another hall of some
of its glory. Therefore nowadays Rickards daren’t
announce on Wednesday that he intends show-
ing a calculating cockroach on Saturday, because
some rival all would instantly put a cockroach
into training and announce the wonder’s positively
first appearance in Australia for Friday evening,
. wherefore Rickards would strike a public for
which the novelty of calculating cockroach had
worn off.
** * *
Thomas Kingston is still furiously dissi-
pating “Brewster’s Millions” at the Theatre
Royal. But the problem of how to get rid of
vast wealth expeditiously doesn’t seem nearly as
perplexing to a lot of Sydney people as it did
when the season opened three weeks ago; for
between times these people have had to buy a ton
of coal, and the hole the transaction has left in
their purse has robbed them of a lot of the sympa-
thy they used to have for Kingston.

week; and the advts. don’t make any mention
yec that any other change is pending.

Magician Carter is still mystifying, with
satisfactory box-office results, at Sydney Palace
Theatre. He will produce the rabbit from the hat
and the lady from the cabinet for the last time on
Friday night week, and thereafter starts on a
tour with the object of baffling the simple
Northerner.

Laurence Dunbar ceases to be an implac-
able Russian villain at Sydney Criterion after
next Friday, and transforms himself into a scoun-
drel of another nationality. For on Saturday the
Bilanderson management will present “ The Land
of Gold,” which is a melodrama of local perpetra-
tion and interest. This play is understood to
have something to do with the Varischetti mine
rescue, but full details are unknown—only it is
sure that Miss Duggan will impersonate a lone
but upright female, and George Cross a manly
fellow with no more dread of danger than of so
much limelight.
