On account of “The Tyrant’’the forth-
coming close of the Holloway season at the Bijou
will be rather deeply regretted. “The Tyrant”
continues his way to the end of the season—
waving his shivering, long sword at every poor,
cringing female he meets, while the Avenger
moodily stalks him, carefully missing every oppor-
tunity for his revenge until he can use it for the
emphatic finale of the last act.
♦ * * *
In “ The Tyrant ” no one takes the ’ero
seriously enough. He is so smug, and has such
an easy time all through. The three leading
ladies do their best to pamper him ; they arrange
all the escapes (he never gives even a “Thank
you ”). Once he offers means of escape to his
loved Adrianne. “ Here’s a rope,” sez he. “You
let yourself down from the conning tower ” (or
somewhere). “But,” wails the lady, “it’sadrop
of 180 feet!” “My love,” thunders the ’ero,
“do it for my sake !” “I will,” she sobs—“but
are you coming, too?” “Nary a hundred-foot
drop for me,” chuckles the ’ero. “In disguise as
the arch-priest I walk out of here safely. Get to
work now —drop ! ” The odd thing is that they
never drop to the true character of the gallant
hero.
** * *
Three performances of “Rigoletto”—the
only Verdi opera attempted by the German
Opera Co. —have helped to strengthen the tail
end of its season at Melbourne Princess’s. In
“Rigoletto” the useful Rupp got a belated
opportunity to show all that was in him, and he
played and sang the name part with the warmth
and vigor of one who might have been born in
Italy and weaned on macaroni. Miss Stoller, as
Gilda, also did better than her previous best, and
the general effect, without being very impressive,
was unexpectedly satisfactory. Last Thurs-
day night Miss Sara Anderson completed
her contract with G.M. by singing “ Tannhauser ”
once again in a manner worthy of the flowery and
flattering demonstration that marked her fare-
well appearance. Melbourne had always found
Miss Anderson to be an admirable artist, who
kept faith with the advertisement. Duty usually
required her to simulate violent attachments for
a prodigiously stout tenor of superhuman ten-
dencies, and in every instance she answered to
duty’s call with Wagnerian enthusiasm. She
died for Tannhausor ; she suicided for the Dutch-
man ; Lohengrin left her a grass widow, and in
the “Valkyrie” case there was not even a
marriage license to mitigate her grief. The
Melbourne send-off to the lady was a recognition
of the fact that she had never shirked her suffer-
ings. The season closes on Friday with “The
Valkyrie,” wherein Madame Slap, or somebody
else, will have to take Miss Anderson’s place.
For to-night (Thursday) the inevitable appear-
ance of Miss Pinschof, as Carmen, is announced.
And on Saturday there will be a reappearance of
Andrew Mack and co. for seven nights only.
Beginning with “Tom Moore,” the broguey
comedian will produce four dramas within a week,
introducing his gentle top-note into each. From
“ The Valkyrie ” to “ Tom Moore ” is a consider-
able drop in musical melodrama.
** * *
At Melbourne Royal this is the third week
of “ The Midnight Wedding,” or The Bridegroom
on the Balcony. The same drama, indifferently
acted, might have been only a moderate success,
but with Gaston Mervale invading the sanctity of
MissMclntosh’s sleeping apartment, and Harcourt
Beatty chasing the invader off the premises, the
situation becomes thrilling. Oneof these Saturdays
the “ Midnight Bedding ” will be replaced by an
older drama, equally strong in emotional acting
scenes, but not so strictly moral in tone. The
management talks of staging “Camille” for a
week or so, with a view to displaying Miss Madge
Mclntosh in the name part, and Beatty aforesaid
in the character of Armand, which should fit him
like a glove. Meanwhile he appears seven times
weekly (including Wednesday matinees) as the
morganatic son of the Prince of Barataria, a tall,
hard-voiced gentleman, who looks young for
his age.
** * *
The Wirths, in quoting his Grace’s words,
have not made it clear that the Archbishop of
Paris was thinking of the new Olympia, by
Prince’s Bridge, when he said : “The circus is
the theatre of childhood, and the Priest goes
there as the Guide to children.” In this country
children don’t require guiding to the circus. They
get there by crawling under the tent, if possible,
and when it happens that the tent is an iron
structure, by the name of Olympia, they drag their
doting parents to the pay-box. All the same.
The Bulletin approves of priests and parsons at
the circus. (It has a fond recollection of that
manly parson, Brother Charles, who went to the
circus because he liked to see the horses and the
animals, “and, yes, the lady in green tights who
did a great bare-back act.”) Even Judkins should
be admitted, but only on the understanding that
he will not annoy the elephants. This (Thursday)
afternoon the circus will be the scene of a charity
carnival, and many theatrical people and things
will happen, including Dick Turpin and The
Kelly Gang. The Wirths are closing the show in
Melbourne on Saturday night, after which the
elephants will plod their way to another pitch,
where the old familiar canvas town will be spread
as usual.
** * *
The first of two Melba concerts at Mel-
bourne Town Hall comes due on Saturday next
(23rd). Persons desirous of hearing the Boss
Singer of the Universe trill her famous lay are
herewith reminded that the seats not already
booked are few, if any; and if any, they are worth
rushing for. Also those people who have never
heard Melba, and have no intention to buy the
privilege, are disrespectfully requested to refrain
from protesting that her voice doesn’t appeal to
them,
** * *
The Taits have added an up-to-date
attraction to the “ Robbery Under Arms ” Bio.
at Athenaeum Hall. The new film shows Jim
Morrissey toying with his snakes—Jim being the
charmer at whose bidding the copper-headed
reptiles stand upon their tails and dance around
the bar of the hotel. The Taits claim that they,
or the photographer, took the Morrissey pictures
at great risk ; but, thanks to the concertinas with
which they were armed, the Taits came off
victorious. One ferocious snake fastened its fangs
in a concertina, but the Tait continued to squeeze
charming music from the instrument until the
reptile was overcome.
** * *
At the Opera House the programme has
suffered no change of importance since last week.
It is a very good bill in a Rickards manner of
speaking, and it shows evidence of improved
musical taste in the “turn” of Miss Lottie Kay,
a cultured soprano singer who came from England
a few weeks ago, with a voice of very different
calibre to the ordinary imported whimper that
struggles with a ballad at the Opera House.
** * *
Great is the power of the mighty voice.
Clara Butt has been filling Adelaide Town Hall
at prices which would take one to the best seats in
the three local theatres twice, and then leave
something handsome in hand for supper. Julius
Knight has finished his season with “ Brigadier
Gerard,” which is a fine bustling play of action-
far better than “ Robin Hood,” anyway. Hubert
Willis and Miss Elbertorton have pleased Ade-
laide greatly. The Hippodrome has ceased from
producing “ The Bloodthirstiest Bounder in Bow-
den” type of drama, and gone back to vaude-
ville, which is mysterious, because it is run by
Bert Sayers and Brandon Cremer, and they are
giving much the same kind of show, ac the same
popular prices, at the opposition house, the
Tivoli.

puted possession of Her Majesty’s. There is no

“ Carl Preston ” : Some of the tragic
chapters of early Australian history are written
on the coasts of King Island. Have tramped the
whole 50 miles of its western shores, and have
seen evidence of no less than 30 wrecks, most of
which date back to the days of the gold-rush and
emigrant ships. At the back of one lonely beach,
rising out of the southern ocean, is a tree bearing
a now almost undecipherable iron-plate memorial
notice of the wreck of the British Admiral. It
tells that of 400 who sailed on the vessel, no less
than 399 were lost. Does any Bulletin writer
know the history of the survivor ?
AT POVERTY POINT.
Perth (W.A.) thinks it has discovered a new musical
genius in its 16-year-old violinist Harold Mayer. As is
always the case in -such circumstances, the natives are
hastening to export their find to Europe; and he leaves
loaded with good wishes and the viands his fellow-towns-
folk are determined to fire into him at a farewell banquet.
Herbert Flemming’s new season, opening in Melbourne
on Boxing Night, looks promising. “ The Morals of
Marcus,” “ The Barrier,” “ Barry Doyle’s Rest Cure,” and
“The Beloved Vagabond” are the new plays. Miss
Beatrice Day will be leading lady, and eight new players
are being imported for the productions.
“La Milo” Pansy Montague’s pa was for years the
verger at St. Columb’s Anglican Church at Haw-hawthorn
(Vic.). Pansy sang in the choir. Singing in the choir or
being educated at a convent seems to be the first step
always to a theatrical career.
“ Hamer ”: Poverty Point doesn’t spend much sympathy
on George Musgrove over his Grand Uproar company. In
Auckland, where George met the worst of his troubles,
he told the papers, some five years ago, that it was im-
possible to form a theatrical co. of Australian artists—
there were not enough good people available.
“Locust”: The scratch-cat German sausage-opera
company has killed the market for Germans for a long
time. What a crowd they were ! What arrogance ! and
what appetites ! I shall never hear “ Carmen ” without
calling to mind those 16 chops disappearing down a
golden throat in Adelaide ; or the ten or a dozen cigar-
ettes smoked by a fair soprano in Melbourne in about
half-an-hour after supper. But, oh these haunting
memories ! I never hear the Jewel Song in “ Faust ”
without being reminded of a waiter running out of the
private room of a Maoriland hotel, with blood, and
gravy running down his face. He told me afterwards
that a singer had hit him with a “ portion ” o< duck
because he had handed her a dish over the wrong
shoulder.
Three musical pieces already bespoken by Meynell and
Gunn for Australia are “ Miss Hook, of Holland,” “The
Belle of Mayfair,” and “ The Girl Behind the Counter.”
The first named will be ready for discussion at Melbourne
Royal early next year. The music and book of “Miss
Hook ” are by Paul Rubens, and whereas the comedy is
said to be as dainty and coherent as his “ Three Little
Maids,” the humor of it runs on broader lines. “ The
Girl Behind the Counter,” by the way, was written by
Bantock—not the fleshy Bantock of Williamson’s, but
his brother, who came out to Australia j'ears ago with
“ A Gaiety Girl ” Co. A stage manager, four comedians,
and two leading girls, have been definitely booked for the
M. and G. campaign, and there are other engagements in
view. The leading lady, Miss Alice Russon, is the
charmer who took the place of Edna May in the “Belle
of Mayfair,” what time that much-paragraphed American
was suffering from indisposition, or tantrums, or some-
thing. Miss Russon’s success served to cure Edna.
Dear Bulletin.— Looking through some old programmes
the other day, I saw one of the concert of concert-Meister
Kruse, which is remarkable for the fact that this fine,
capable player of the violin (a credit to his Australian
birthplace) was “ assisted by Mrs. Armstrong, the
charming soprano,” and others whose names don’t
matter. The first concert was at the Theatre Royal on
Saturday, July 4, 1885 (Eheu ! Fugaces, Postume /); of
which theatre the lessee was Samuel Lazar ; the treasurer,
Geo. L. Goodman (still with us). It was under the
direction of Messrs. Williamson, Garner and Musgrove.
The “ charming soprano ” sang “ Sweet Bird” (Ganz) and
“ Best of All” (Moir) ; and I remember, being very young
then, raving for encores, and helping to get “ Bid Me
Goodbye and Go” (Tosti). She also sang “ Maying” with
Mr. Frank Boyle, tenor. At the second concert she flew
higher, and did the Jewel Song from “ Faust,” and “ Thee
Only I Love” (Abt). Though Kruse had the big type on
the programme, I remember that Mrs. Armstrong (now
Madame Melba, world-star) got most of the applause.
The houses were woefully thin. Oh, Time ! Time ! Our
fiery patriotism moved us to proclaim Mrs. Armstrong as
the greatest soprano in the world ; but knowing old
British musical chaps smiled pityingly at us. As if
anything good could come out of Australia ! Good old
Time. He vindicated us. But lam too grey to boast.—
Yours, Locust.
George Darrell has sold a new four-act thrill—“ The
Land of Gold ” —to W. Anderson, and Eugenie Duggan
will start saving Darrell’s hero from the wiles of the
wicked at Sydney Criterion on November 23. In
Darrell’s version of history, the hero “seeks refuge from
his pursuers by assuming an alien’s name and a miner’s
garb,” and that gives a chance to play the rescue of
Va,rischetti all over again. This will make the required
third-act sensation, and gives Eugenie a chance to get
her second wind, so as to complete the hero’s salvation in
time to get a snack before the last train leaves.
Marie Narelle, her health recovered, is touring the
Monaro (N.S. W.).
The Bondi Lady Godiva is not up to specifications. Her
abnormal wig, while it makes powerfully, for propriety,
gets an effectual hammerlock on the illusion aimed at.
The Coventry dame, as portrayed at Wonderland, looks
rather more clothed than does the average Chief Justice
during business hours. In fact, where she doesn’t look
like a wild (and stout) gyurl from the woods, this par-
ticular Godiva resembles a C. J. very much indeed.
“Spango”: Re concert “hogwash” (“Mallet,” B.
24/10/’O7). The kid may be tiresome, but the adult per-
former with an eye for the Sickly and Romantic side of
life is the absolute limit. Heard, the other night, an
apparently sane, sensible Australian human being of the
male sex telling a story about a Tramp and “ Home,
Sweet Home.” It told that an auctioneer was trying to
sell a piano, and wanted one of his fashionable audience
to play a tune on it. No one responded. Suddenly The
Tramp appeared. His raggedness was pathetically out
of place in that haughty gathering. As he hobbled pain-
fully forward, the ladies swished their skirts aside, while
the mensaid, “Puthinj out,”and “ Damn me ! ” But The
Tramp did not hesitate. With his great eyes glowing in
strange fashion, he reached the piano. Even the
auctioneer fell back, realising instinctively the presence
of A Soul. First, the stranger softly played a few notes
of Wondrous Sweetness. Then he BURST into a Flood of
Delicious Melody. The audience was spellbound. Still
the unknown played on : Mendelssohn, Beethoven,
Mozart, Dan O’Connor—he knew them all, and rendered
their works with a master-hand. And at last came
“Home, Sweet Home!” And now handkerchiefs are
held to all eyes, while many, to express their feelings,
crowd around the strange musician. His head is sunk
forward upon his breast. He is apparently sleeping.
They try to rouse him. No answer ! They note the pale,
unearthly calm of his features. They raise him tenderly.
Ah ! why prolong the suspense ? He is dead. (Loud
applause, the females being particularly affected.)

“Hiphen”: Why all this excited yap because Mrs.
Clarionet Butt festoons her photos with infants, husbands,
and other'domestic utensils ? This is the present fashion
in Cold Smoke musical circles. It is more usual among
musical comedy stars. Formerly, it was a good ad. for a
leading lady to have a name as a high-stepper. It
nightly filled the 10s. 6d. stalls with the baldheaded
brigade. Now, however, the position is reversed. The
lady who sings naughty ballads all the week in a costume
that would shock a Manly councillor, has her photograph
in the magazines, teaching Sunday school or tending
the poor and weedy. She gives the public to understand
that she only engages in the theatrical game to support
a widowed father or a drunken mother, or something.
Her ambition in life, she informs the interviewer, is to
settle down and do the rural by coaxing juice from cows.
Virtue is now the long suit. Hence the domestic photo.
If the lady possesses a husband or kids, she plays them
out boldly. Lacking these, she steals or borrows an aged
mother with snow-white locks. She is photographed
with her arm (or arms) round the mother’s neck. She is
dressed in white. She is unjewelled. She looks young,
innocent and fluffy. The Society girl says “How
sweet!” or “How Sad!” She fills the seat which
formerly sheltered a Bald Head. Virtue used to be its
own reward. Now it is made to pay dividends. This is a
commercial age.
Tom Burrows, champion club-swinger of the world,
has just put up one of those curiously-pointless records
which appeal so surely to the great heart of the Anglo-
Saxon people. At the Tivoli Theatre, Cape Town, he
whirled his clubs last month for 61 hours 15 minutes
without a stop. Thomas, like the world’s champion axe-
man, and J. Neild (conversation expert), and many
another endurance marvel, is an Australian.
“ Bobsfcay ”: Please may I throw a handspike at
‘Stag-eland” (“P.P.,” Bulletin, 14/11/’O7). Stage-
land maintains that Captain Stirling Whyte, skipper
of the yacht in “ Brewster’s Millions,” is right according
to his lights, and remarks that the usual night signals
of distress, two red lamps (?) 6ft. apart, are hoisted
nightly. Should “ Stageland ” ever own a yacht and get
into difficulties, and signal for help, he had better not
hoist two red lights, 6ft. apart, for he would get a shock.
Everything afloat w r ould shun him like the plague, for
this signal is exclusively reserved for any vessel "which,
from any accident is not under command, and cannot
therefore get out of the way,” and a good thing to avoid
accordingly. Captain Whyte’s only excuse for this signal
is that he is driving through the storm recklessly indif-
ferent to what is in front, but seeing that he is in a
powerful steam vessel with steerage way, he would be
morally bound to obey the steering and sailing rules, and
if he disregarded them anything he hit would have a
grievance which any Board of Trade tribunals would up-
hold.
“ 10-2-4 ” : There’s no local industry when all is said and
done that has the importing craze so badly as the variety
showman’s. We have pretty and clever soubrettes of a
home-made brand (Brightie Smith, for example, is
smarter, and certainly better-looking, than nine-tenths of
the foreign-trained serios that find their way here) ; also
a galaxy of comics of the Tom Dawson type, some of
whom are guilty of genuine humor now and again. But
they aren’t suffered to profit much in their own country.
The Australian—in this line at any rate—only gets a
“ shop " in the last resort ; and when he or she does, the
situation is low down on the bill—’way after the lady from
the Mile End-road, London E., with the calamitous voice,
and the limbs of a treadmill expert, and several lengths
behind the h-less young Cockney, newly arrived by s.s.
Windjammer, with a moderately fresh rendering of the
Lodger Jest.
The gentle savage of the Bed Sea littoral is to have the
dead monotony of his heated life cheered somewhat by a
circus. Geo. Warren (now in Mombasa, B. E. Africa) is
taking “ Warren’s Circus” through all the coast towns,
beginning with Aden. If Warren, together with his
animals, isn’t eaten by a hungry or disappointed audience
before reaching Suez, there ought to be big money in this
enterprise.
Hartley Davis, in Everybody’s Magazine, just to hand,
gives astounding figures about vaudeville (that is to say
variety) salaries, in America. The following are all pounds
sterling, not dollars :—May Irwin gets £5OO a week ;
Lilian Bussell got a bit over £6oo—the record ; Elsie
Janis has nearly as much ; Mrs. Langtry received £5OO.
Vesta Victoria came from London at £BO, but gets £5OO ;
she bounded up with “ Waiting at the Church.” Vesta
Tilly, another Englander, also has £5OO. Alice Lloyd (not
Marie), yet another, only had a paltry £3O a week in
London, but went to New York at £7O, and sprang to
£3OO. Henry Lee, well known in Australia, has £l6O. Nat
Wills, the Tramp, shuffles along with only £125.
Belle Blanch, a chorus girl, jumped up to £lOO. Barnold’s
dog and monkey pantomime stands him in £2OO. A great
majority of the vaudeville acts in such a city as New York
cost from £5O to £lOO a week. In the best houses no act
costs less than £l5 a week. This is given for the finale,
termed a chaser, because the audience begins to go out.
Hammerstein’s vaudeville house, New York, cleared
£50,000 profit last year. Yet the outlay was about £70,000.
At such an establishment there are 60 employees, costing
£3OO a week, in addition to the performers. Other ex-
penses will be £2OO a week.
“ Carl Preston ” : For sheer variety know nothing to
touch an Outback concert. Becently went to one which
was designed to finance the local cricket club. The pro-
gramme numbered about twenty-five events, as follows:
One piano solo, one piano duet; two sacred, three comic,
two sentimental songs ; vocal quartette; cornet solo
(unaccompanied) ; act by two dogs, who walked on their
hind legs and sat on chairs ; two gramophone selections ;
clog dance ; exhibition of club-swinging (limelight);
maypole dance by local kiddies ; three-rounds exhibition
sparring match ; action-song by State-school class ; tem-
perance dialogue by local Band of Hope; address by
h-less individual; pathetic recitation about a small boy
who fell off a bridge, and went to Heaven ; terribly
tragic recitation about a lifeboat; coffee handed round ;
God Save the King. After which the seats were cleared
away and we had a dance. Carriages 3.30 a.m. I reckon
Bickards’ show (whether Sydney or Melbourne) is a mere
decimal fraction to this.
It has always been a matter for wonder why, even
in the best of straight-out melodrama, all the people
on the stage just at the fall of the curtain assume
attitudes of contempt, accusation, perfidy, defiance or
indignation, as the case may be. Thus the heavy villain
cowers, a picture of snarling surprise ; the hero stands as
if clothed in steel, pointing a devilish forefinger with an
assumption of moral dominance ; the heroine appears to
be struck dead, drooping like a damp towel over a corpse
(if there is one) ; while all the others, if watched closely,
are putting on high moral “ side ” in a series of ridiculous
and unnatural postures. The ludicrousness of this sort of
climax was very noticeable at the Melbourne Bijou lately.
One evening, just at the end of a scene of moral anguish and
pain, the drop scene caught on some projection and stayed.
The hero clasped the heroine, who was moaning on his
shoulder, what time he accused the villain with an out-
stretched paw ; an old woman was weeping, and a male
creature held a heavy horse-pistol. The hero continued
to clasp and accuse ; the heroine continued to moan ; the
old woman continued to weep ; the pistol remained as
before, while the blessed curtain stayed. Then the whole
crowd became anxious, and gazed' fixedly at the flies,
where movements and mutterings could be heard. And
while the audience roared, the little living picture broke
up and crawled—literally crawled—into the wings.
“Hamer”: The dullness of the average super is only
exceeded by his scorn of the actors. He is mostly one of
three —a man w’ho has been supering for years, and now
looks upon himself as a member of “the profession”; a
stage-struck enthusiast, who hopes to “work up”; ora
casual who is hard-up. The casuals are generally the most
intelligent. The supers’ habit of being a crowd has a bad
effect on the old hands. One night, in the “Sign of the
Cross,” where, in the last act, the supers had to be lions,
a new crowd, when the stage manager jerked his arms
energetically upward as a signal for the roars, cheered
Stephanos enthusiastically and joyously as he approached.
The stage manager shouted wildly, swore, and tossed his
arms like a man seeing snakes; and a vigorous “ Hip, hip,
hurrah ! ” floated out from the lions’ den, and scared
Stephanos down the steps. Then suddenly the supers
realised that they were lions, and roared with all possible
ferocity.
“Maori Mac” (M.L.) hopelessly: How is the home
song a thing contemned, and set to naught in comparison
with that which has been imported ! At present there are
in my vicinity four boat-loads of people, and they sing
diversely and incessantly of “The Old Bull and Bush,”
“ Baltimore,” “St. Louis” and some place labelled “ Bom-
bombay.” None of these people will ever visit Baltimore
or Bom-bombay, yet if I wrote a song of “ Taikakariki ”
or “ Ngahauranga’’—which places they visit every time
the butchers or cordial manufacturers hold a picnic—my
warble would be treated with contempt. By next mail I
send to a London publisher the pathetic ballad “ Maori
Maude’s Beply to Jerry the Bake.” If it becomes the
vogue in Soho, some day I shall hear its melodious strains
drifting through my window', and “ My Kaffir Cocoanut”
will be a nightmare of the past. Then I shall be glad,
likewise drunk, and probably disorderly.
The “ Mother Goose ” co. becomes the “ Humpty
Dumpty ” co. in Melbourne next month. The Goose
female abandoned Newcastle owing to the coal dispute,
leaving behind her a record of 334 performances,-also a
travelling total of 15,000 miles by rail and sea.
“ Snell ” : Still another wedding drama to follow “ The
Fatal Wedding,” “The Midnight Wedding,” etc., etc.
The latest is “The Bun-aw'ay Wedding.” Considering
the great run on weddings on the boards, the popularity
of the kid as a conspicuous feature, and the frequent use
of murder, suicide, manslaughter, and death by visitation
of Providence, in plays of our time, I am setting to work
to embody all these features in the melodrama of the
century. My drama will be quite comprehensive. I have
got the chief feature already fixed—that is the title. My
drama will be called “ Births, Deaths, and Marriages.”

“ Franz Gr.,” in last Bulletin, argues that
the bush girl has no distinctive characteristics
peculiar to the bush girl. But bush and other
girls are quick to discern the distinctive character-
istics of wise males who are wedded to D.C.L. as
the beverage of their choice. The right brand of
girl, which is every girl, encourages her best boy
to imbibe gleams of mental sunshine from the
right brand of whisky—D.C.L/'
Horse and Hound.— Returning dead beat
from a long day’s shooting the other day, it luckily
struck me that a hot bath with plenty of Serubb’s Am-
monia in it would be the best pick-me-up possible, and so
it proved. Therefore, to all my readers I can with con-
fidence recommend this cure when feeling fagged out
after violent exercise, and am sure they will find it
satisfactory.”
Two Storms.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
ANGELINA and Edwin had been married
three months before they quarrelled, and
it is doubtful whether they’d have quar-
relled then, only for the storm. During their
engagement days, there were times when Edwin
had qualms about bringing Angelina to the bush.
“ Ange won’t like the bush,” he said once, as he
toyed with her waved hair ; but Angelina ached
for the bush with a great ache. “ Little white
hands,” said Edwin fatuously, “ they’ll have to
work, cook and wash and clean, and she’ll only
have a tiny, tiny little house with four rooms in
it.” Angelina adored tiny, tiny little houses with
only four rooms, and she’d just love to cook and
clean and wash for Edwin. So they were married,
and everything in the garden was lovely.
The time was n p.m. Edwin had retired.
Angelina sat by the fire in the adjoining room
thoughtfully putting her still-cared-for hair into
wavers. “If anyone had told me Edwin could
snore like that,” she mused, “ I’d have thought
him a person of very coarse mind.” Edwin
snored on. Angelina proceeded with the wavers.
Suddenly it began to rain—not ordinary rain, but
rain that drowned Edwin’s snores. It pelted
deafeningly on the iron roof, and streamed in
dirty black streams down the chimney on to the
whitewashed fireplace. The wind roared and
shrieked, and swept the water under the doors,
and little streams trickled on to the table, and
down the papered-slab walls to the bush-framed
photograph of Angelina’s relations.
“ Edwin,” she shouted, “ it’s raining.”
“ Urr,” answered Edwin, unintelligibly.
“ It’s raining, darling.”
“ Yesh ; shear it, pettie,” said Edwin.
44 The clothes are down by the creek, dearest,”
said Angelina, anxiously.
“No fearsh, darling. Creek wontsh come
down. ”
“ But you remember the bullock?”
“ Eh, bullsk ? ”
“ Yes, lovie, the last storm ; when I saw the
poor bullock feeding in the paddock one minute,
and the next hanging on the fence drowned.”
Edwin snored.
The rain, which had eased off, came on again
in torrents.
“Darling,” called Agelina again, “ listen to
the rain. All my things are on the line at the
creek.”
“ Needn’ be friten’, pettie. Creek wontsh
come down.”
“ Oh, but it will," wailed Angelina. “ I’m
going to the line.”
Edwin snored.
Angelina, in her efforts to get his best boots off
their trees (not gum trees, you know ; Edwin had
once been civilised), wakened him.
“ Whatsh doin’ ? ” he asked, sleepily.
“ I’m going down to the creek to get the
things ; they’re on the line.”
Edwin was eyeing fretfully Angelina’s struggles
with his patent leathers. He leaped out of bed.
“ I’ll go. Don’t be a fool,” he said crossly, put-
ting on a cap, and carefully covering his pyjamas
wfith an oil coat. “ What the Devil you want to
leave clothes on the line at night for beats me.”
Angelina flushed.
“You’ll never find the pins,” she said, with
dignity ; “ besides, you’ll dirty the things.”
‘ 4 Pins ! What do you mean ? ”
“ The things are pinned on. I have no pegs.”
“ Well, you ought to have,” snapped Edwin.
“ Pray don’t get excited,” said Angelina,
loftily ; but her calm dignity seemed out of keep-
ing with the soiled Turkish towel, which she had
rolled round her wavers. She led the way. They
did not possess a lantern ; the kerosene lamp was
out of the question, and a candle or match abso-
lutely refused to keep alight. Angelina tested the
darkness. She put her hand before her face, and
couldn’t see it. The creek roared loudly.
“Hey! Where are you?” shouted Edwin.
“Look out, I believe it’s up. Let the blasted
clothes go.”
But it w'as not to be expected that Angelina
would let the prettiest of her trousseau frillies go,
and she stumbled and slipped on determinedly.
Then she screamed.
“What’s up? Where are you? My God!
are you in it ?” called Edwin, hoarsely.
“ No, I’m in them," wailed Angelina.
“ What ? ” shouted Edwin.
“ The thistles.”
“Damn!” said Edwin aloud. '“Serve her
right,” he added under his breath.
“ They’re all over me—in my hands and face,
and I’m lying in them, and slipping down the
bank,” sobbed Angelina.
Edwin, in his efforts to reach her, fell a distance
of five yards and slipped seven more. And the
creek roared louder.
4 4 Aren’t you coming to get me out ? ” wailed
Angelina. “ Every time I put my hand out I get
more prickles.”
No answer.
44 Edwin, where are you ? ” she shrieked.
44 Oh, be d—d,” shouted back Edwin. “ Stop
where you are, or you’ll be in the creek.”
How Edwin reached the line in the rain and wind
and darkness is rather a mystery. Angelina had
sometimes thought him lacking in courage and
determination, but that was before the storm.
44 Why the h— don’t you order some pegs?”
he shouted.
Angelina, who had struggled painfully out of
the thistles, sat on the bank in sodden wretched-
ness.
44 1 knew you’d say that —why didn’t you let
me go ? I wanted to.”
Edwin did not answer.
44 Have you got them ? ” shrieked Angelina.
“ I’ve got two blanky hatpins,” roared back
Edwin.
“ Don t lose them ; stick them in the front of
your coat, and take care of the muslin petticoat
and the other muslin things. Don’t soil them.”
“ for G—’s sake shut up,” called Edwin.
“ What the Devil did you wind this thing round
and round the line for ? ”
“ Don’t lose your hair,” said Angelina, spite-
fully. ‘‘ If you don’t hurry up you’ll be drowned,
anyhow. The water came down like a black
wall last time. Good enough for you, too,” she
said, relapsing into childishness. “ I’m sorry I
ever married you.”
“Same here,” shouted Edwin, angrily, as he
stumbled away from the line. And from the
added roar it was pretty certain the creek was
coming in the familiar black wall.
“Listen to it roaring,” shrieked Angelina.
“ It’s coming.”
No answer.
“ Edwin ! Edwin, it’s coming—Edwin ! ” she
shrieked again. “ Where are you ? ”
“I’m in the blasted spring, and the blasted
clothes are with me,” answered Edwin, wrath-
fully. “And the blasted things can stop there,
too.”
Angelina’s tears added to the moisture that
already enveloped her. Edwin struggled up the
greasy bank, carrying a huge, round ball of
muddied clothes. The creek came with a rush.
Angelina, with only one mishap in the shape of
a fall of some feet into a muddy gap, groped her
way to the garden gate, and reached the house.
Edwin followed closely. They gazed at each
other in disillusionment. Never had either ap-
peared to less advantage. Angelina continued to
weep.
“What are you crying for?” said Edwin,
roughly, as he struggled out of the oil coat, and
viewed, with disgust, his mud-caked pyjamas.
‘ ‘ I brought them up. ”
“You’re a br-r-r -brick," said Angelina, chang-
ing her mind. “ Shall I make some coffee ?”
“ Here, let’s look at your hands,” said Edwin,
relenting. “ Many prickles ?” he asked, softly.
“ Edwin,” said Angelina between two very
moist sniffs, as she removed the sopping towel,
“You can swear beautifully.”
Edwin laughed and kissed her.
GIP.
“ Hamer” : The lack of bath is noticeable
on the West Coast of M.L. In one township,
where it was raining hard, writer and a friend,
being unable to get a bath at the hotel, took soap
and towels (and overcoats) to the creek. It was a
sequestered spot ; but the school children, having
somehow got wind of this exciting event in the
town’s history, assembled, and gazed, and made
remarks, and got wet. This annoyed some of the
elders of the town, and we were summoned for
cleanliness, or something. We remarked to the
Jay Pay that cleanliness was next to godliness;
and he inquired, “That’s in the Bible, isn’t it?”
and seemed impressed with our goodness. Then
he remarked that as we said we were leaving the
town, and were not coming back, he would accept a
promise that we would not offend by washing
again, and let us off with a caution.
“H.T.” writes from the hills
Out in the Tararu Ranges (Maoriland) an old man lives,
40 miles from anywhere, with a farm consisting of one
acre of flat and 200 acres on the nearly vertical side of the
mountain.
One day, through following the wrong creek, I came to
his whare, and, as it was getting late, I asked him for a
feed and a bunk.
As we sat smoking over a rata fire, he said, “ What is a
gohanna like ? ” I eyed a pile of about a thousand Bul-
letins and replied, “ I really don’t know. I’ve never been
in Australia. I believe it’s like a big lizard.”
He looked unsatisfied, and said, “I would like to
know what they are like, and The Bulletin never draws
one. I know a snake is like an eel, and a ’possum is a
grey rabbit what climbs trees, and an emu is a sorter
ostrich, but I can’t find out what a gohanna is like.
“The Bulletin is the only paper I’ve read for fifteen
years,” he went On ; “and if I knew what them things is
like I could enjoy it much better. I wish I knew what
those gohannas is like.”
When I set out next morning he came about five miles
down the creek with me to put me right, and as I left
him he said, “ I would like to meet a bloke what’s seen
one of those gohannas.”
It is plainly up to Norman Lindsay to draw one
of those gohannas.
“ The Prooshan ” : An animal’s instinct in
scenting danger is remarkable. The burnt child
dreads the fire, but animals have the sense in most
cases to dread danger without first experience of
it. Take the worn bat for example. If you find
your wire-netting bumped up and rooted under,
you know Womby has been through, and at once
bring a loaded gun and set it muzzlewards to the
hole with wire trigger attachment. But Womby
calls in the evening, notes the thin trigger wire
across the hole, and passes on a rod or so and
makes a fresh opening. Mount a gun there and
he shoves under elsewhere. The gun-trick comes
off at his burrow-mouth sometimes. The dog-
trap catches him, but is not strong enough to
hold.
“Larrikin ” tells of a weird colored brother :
He was an Indian snake-charmer up on the Fitzroy
(Q.). Lean as a whipping post he was, and bent almost
double, with a face brown and wrinkled, and the general
appearance of a mummy that had been overlooked.
Folks on the river said that he was well over a hundred,
and the oldest inhabitants of any veracity remembered
him as an aged man when they were children.
He never moved 100yds. away from his camp without a
praying carpet and a huge purple leather Asiatic valise
that was dropping to pieces with age. Latter was
crammed with glittering women’s garments, Asiatic
fal-lals, slippers, trousers and caps, all embroidered with
green beetles’ wings and gold thread tarnished almost
black. His clothes were heavy with roughly-cut jewels
sewn in them, and his old yellow turban was a regular
mine. To see him going along with his burden made one
think of an ant in the parcel delivery business.
The old man used to sit down and play upon a queer
Indian bagpipe to his pet reptiles, the snakes would
writhe gracefully all over him, hissing and darting out
their forked blue tongues engagingly, and the little alli-
gators that he had hatched out of eggs kept inside his
shirt would waddle about snapping at all in their way.
When they required a siesta, theyus'ed to crawl inside his
shirt and bask upon his heart.
He died recently in a struggling bush hospital. The
nurses still go pale when anyone mentions his name. For
half an hour after his arrival two wardsmen were chasing
and killing his snakes, which swarmed up on the walf-
plates, and wriggled up the bedposts in a fearsome
manner, while the old Indian cursed and jabbered with
his blazing eyes nearly starting out of his head.
“J.D.8.”: How does the Chinaman
reckon time in his own Flowery Land ? I was
recently shown a clock made for a mandarin in
Pekin. The face was marked with three concen-
tric circles. The outer had 24 divisions, the
middle 12, and the inner eight. There were two
hands. The shorter revolved once in two hours,
and the longer once in 24 hours. From these
particulars some genius may gather how the
Chinaman reckons time; I can’t. The inner
circle with its eight divisions is too much for me.
“F.H.R.” : There’s a lot of talk at times
about elms and other foreign trees in the Sydney
Mother Hubbard. Now, I’ve said it before, and
I’ll keep on at it, that the most beautiful orna-
mental trees in the world grow in our back country.
There are places on the Condobolin and Brewarrina
railways that can compare, when there is grass on
the ground, with anything I have ever seen in any
park. Near Compton Downs, on the Brewarrina
line, there is a large natural lake which is sur-
rounded by the prettiest currajongs I have ever
met. These are growing amongst large, gleaming
granite rocks of every quaint shape and pattern,
and the place is one of Nature’s gardens. On the
Condobolin line, compact sheoaks, wilgas, belars
and myalls can be seen from the train equal in
appearance to any ornamental tree growing in our
Botanical Gardens. They have the additional
grace of being evergreen, and, barring one class of
currajong, make no undesirable litter of falling
leaves and fruit as do the trees growing in so many
N. S. Wales towns. Take Orange, for instance.
In the fall all the unemployed of Australia could
not keep the streets clear of rotten leaves.
MR. PETER BOWLING, President of the
Miners' Federation.
As The Bulletin went to press on Tues-
day, news came through that the dispute in the
coal trade had been referred to arbitration, and
the men had agreed to return to work. It was
like hearing of three inches of rain over the Com-
monwealth.
From a man in the Far North :
.Japs still drift into Australia, in spite of Federal legis-
lation ; and Thursday Island is the centre at which the
swindle is worked. Stowaways on the Asia boats cable in
code from Hongkong as to the number of men wishing
to land at Thursday Island. Before the arrival of the
ship, friends or agents at Thursday Island obtain a
corresponding number of tickets from Thursday Island to
Townsville. On arrival of the eastern boat, each stow-
away is provided with one of these tickets, and exposes
himself to the light of day as a passenger from Thursday
Island to Townsville. Landing at Townsville, he takes
the earliest opportunity of returning to Thursday Island.
Jap women enter by ones and twos. The device adopted
in their case is for a dozen or so of Japanese “ ladies ” to
go on board “ to see the ship,” and for thirteen or more
to come ashore.
“L.F.”: Has The Bulletin ever noted,
in its survey of the Stinking Fish party, and the
many wonderful subdivisions thereof, that these
people rate all other “colonies” as cheaply as
they do Australia ? When I arrived in Australia,
I had had several years of experience in a country
more like this one than perhaps any other,
and I was seeking work of a land that varies less
with local conditions than most professions do.
But I found that the mere mention of experience
in another colony was sufficient to raise a snort of
contempt from the S.F.P. The only ray of hope
was when one anxious friend discovered that I had
not come here direct, but had made a trip to Eng-
land on the way. “ That’s all right, my boy,” he
gasped. “Never you mention t’other place any
more. Direct from the Old Country—valuable
English experience ; that’s your ticket.” And, so
far as concerns about two-thirds of Sydney and
the fossilised hamlets that lie in the backwash
behind it, observation convinces me that my
friend was right.
“S.” : Considering Teddy Roosevelt’s
great interest in Australia, our desire to propitiate
America, and our appreciation of our great
marsupial, I contend that the Commonwealth
should change its name to Roos’ Veldt.
“Hamer”: The suburbs provide some
circuses. One of them happens when the family
parrot escapes, and leads a frantic and appealing
family, who pour out piteous requests to it to
remember the sacredness of the home, on a chase
round the neighboring streets, what time the
dinner burns and the cat enjoys a free feed of
milk and fish in the kitchen. Then the head of
the family arrives on the scene, and hands
his bag and his paper to one of the family,
and says he will soon capture the bird. He
leads a procession, with the youngest girl by
his side pointing out the tree on which the
parrot has settled; his wife follows with the
baby, and the other children and the dog troop
after in disorder, but with firm confidence in their
leader. When they arrive at the tree, Mr.
Suburbs makes endearing remarks to the parrot
until he gets impatient. Then he tells his wife to
go home and get dinner ready, and sends Johnny
after her to get some corn, and Mabel round to
the house that owns the tree to explain things
and ask permission for Tommy to do some climb-
ing. Just as they disappear the parrot takes
another flight, the children scamper after it, the
dog barks, and the head of the family loses his
temper. He loses more temper later, and for an
hour or so an angry voice pierces the evening in
various directions, giving a dozen orders at once,
and calling everybody a fool but himself. Then
the chase subsides, and for another hour there are
sounds of pursuit after lost children, who are
wandering in neighboring streets in search of the
main chase. In the morning the parrot is dis-
covered roosting on its cage, and a man who seems
angry rushes for his train just too late to catch it.
The clog is the only thing concerned in the pro-
ceedings that seems to enjoy them.
“ L.F.” : Is there any clue to the mis-
creant who first landed in Australia with a
pot of laundry blue paint ? That deadly and
poisonous color fairly hits you in the eye wherever
you go. Not a suburban station but quivers with
it; not a business street or an empty hoarding
but offers its paralysing assault upon the optic
nerve. It is a taint that murders in a flash the
delicate blue of the Australian sky; and in the
fairest reaches of the harbor the deep-hued wave
flows shuddering in its shadow. Round Melbourne
way the pestilence is, if possible, even worse.
The Weighing.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE Greek-goddy footman ushered the Soul
of Smithkins into the private judgment office.
Mechanically the chief clerk scrawled the
formal entry in one of the huge ledgers— ‘ ‘ Soul
of Samuel Smithkins, F 4; age, 49 ; married,
large family; dairy farmer, Western District,
Vic., Australia, Planet B.”
The Soul clung nervously to its Guardian Angel.
It wasn’t a bad sort of soul, as souls go. It was
large, but badly battered and scarred; and its
snowy purity had worn down to a dull, dirty grey.
The Devil stalked malignantly in, bearing a
swag containing the Soul’s sins.
The usher, who had been dusting the Scales of
Justice free from old particles of sin, now placed
them in readiness. With a thud the swag of sin
was dumped upon them.
The Angel turned pale at their enormity.
The Soul shuddered as he saw the old familiar
things—the unhallowed Sabbath, the child-slavery,
the sweated hireling, the casual blasphemy, the
murderously-abused Preservitas, the watered
milk ! It shut its eyes. It could bear no more.
Satan himself appeared no more evil at this
moment than Cow.
But the Guardian Angel whispered pityingly :
“ Much shall be forgiven thee, for thou hast
suffered much ” ; then placed upon the opposite
scales the dainty envelope that held the Soul’s
virtues. They weighed heavily indeed for their
puny size, but were powerless to lift the heavy
swag to its own level.
The Devil chuckled.
The Recording Angel then added to the scale
the Soul’s Circumstances of Life and Occasions of
Sin. Like a true counsel for defendant she
pointed out how the Soul had been handicapped
by its name and by hereditary Smithkin instincts.
Much of its sin had been custom in the land where
the Soul’s shell had lived. Many were due to
Bad Influence. And being a weak Smithkins
sort of soul, it couldn’t help being easily in-
fluenced. And it had done some good in a human
little way.
As she laid these arguments in the scale, it rose
slowly in the Soul’s favor till it needed but a
hair’s breadth to outweigh the sins. But her
arguments were exhausted. Pale and sad she
paused nonplussed.
The Devil grinned in triumph.
The Soul writhed in silent agony, striving to
articulate. At length it gurgled forth with a
gasping shriek —“The landlord! The share-
system ! ”
Hope lit the Angel’s face. “ Yes, yes,” she
pleaded eagerly —“ The Soul was a tenant-farmer
all its life ! ”
As she placed this fact on the scale, it sank
many inches, jerking the swag of sin high in the
air.
With a shriek of rage and disappointment, the
Devil seized the swag and fled into the blackness
beyond.

* * * *
I cannot too impressively warn the
I< r>’ Chief Justice Forbes, on
the Bench of Sydney Criminal Court
over 80 years ago, “against the un-
necessary or indiscriminate use of fire-
arms. It is murder to shoot dead a
man who can be arrested.” The ease
T ma^ e the first of Australia’s
Chief-Justices use this strong language
was that of a prisoner who attacked a
constable in the George-street lockup,
considerably smashing the officer, and
getting outside. Evidence showed that
it was possible for the constable to re-
arrest, but instead he shot and killed.
Ihe Coroner’s jury found it murder:
but the jury acquitted, and the Judge’s
remarks followed. The use of the re-
volver by the police nowadays is more
free and frequent than in those far-
back times, and that casualties are not
more common is mostly owing to bad
shooting. But Justice Forbes’ words
should be framed and hung on the
wall of every police-station in Aus-
tralia. It is murder to shoot a man
whom it is possible to arrest, unless
indeed he has been outlawed.

One choice exhibit, at the
Women’s Show in Melbourne, attracts
notice because it is so suggestive of
the ways of Sassietv. It is a neatly bound
and prettily embroidered and embossed book
°f poems; and the ticket informs a paralysed
public The ornamentation and binding were
done by an Irish lady, from whom it was pur-
chased by the Countess of Dudley and presented
to Mrs. Earle, of Cobham, Surrey, sister of
Dowager Lady Loch.” The name of the woman
who did the work wasn’t worth mentioning, as no
Dowager Lady perched in her family tree.

“ Stony ” : The nine-o’-dock moths of the
city streets have their servants and sycophants
too, as have all money-makers. It must come as
something of a shock to “ respectable ” women
when they enter a certain drapery shop in Mel-
bourne, to find that the frail one is served first
is rushed with the rapid chair and the engaging
manner, and a bunch of attendants told off to find
out what madam will take. It’s a shop with an
informed man-manager; and there’s a horrible
name for that kind of man.
Mr. Pope Cooper, Queensland’s ex-Crown
Prosecutor C.J., is having sore trouble with
his judgments. His now-famous legal opinion
as to his own non-liability to pay income-
tax was pronounced so unsound the other day
as to border upon decomposition, and that by
nine other Judges, comprising one District Court
Judge, three of the Full Court, and five of the
High Court. In a recent Full Court appeal
(Doherty v. Walsh) Mr. Real, in giving a contrary
judgment to Mr. Cooper, said that “he (Real
would like to agree with his Chief, if he could do so
without disrespect to his own intelligence.”
Subsequent appeal to the High Court (October)
cast further aspersions on Mr. Cooper’s law, by that
Court’s unanimous agreement with Mr. Real. In
another appeal at the same sittings—a tram-car
case (Kelly v. Wigzell)—the High Court again
decisively up-ended Mr. Cooper.
THE HIGHEST CLASS.
FIRST ELDER (darkly): “Is Ber-ruther Bunthunder a good man think you, Ber-ruther? ”
SECOND ELDER (joyfully): "Oh, Ber-ruther, he is classed Al at Judkins!"
There is not in all Australia a more soul-
less and penurious skinflint than the Melbourne
Tramway Co. Its miserliness is manifested in
its treatment of the large army of odd hands,
known as “ battlers” —men who are called upon at
all hours to take up chance work, to put in an
hour, a day, or a night, whenever there’s a little
extra pressure, or when a regular employee goes
under from the tram man’s special banes—in-
fluenza and varicose veins. These “battlers” form
a very considerable proportion of the company’s
army of workers ; they are its special re-
serves, and are despoiled shamefully. When
generously admitted to the rank of a “battler ”
the recruit is given three weeks’ work, for which
he does not get a copper. The indulgent co. tells
him he is learning the game, but, all the same he
is- dome the co.’s work, and earning the share-
holders greasy dividends, cleaning dummies and
carriages, cleaning and filling lamps, etc. The
innocent moiler usually believes that when he has
served this term of tutelage he is right for a
regular grip. That’s his funny little blunder,
it the co. isn t pleased with the work he
has done for nothing, it outs him alto-
gether ; if satisfied, it graciously accepts him into
its large army of hangers-on. Often three or four
hours are wasted by the “ battler ” waiting round
for two hours’ work, as the graft may be doled out
m scraps, by the hour, or even the half-hour
Never in any circumstances does the company pav
one cent more than has been worked for, no matter
how many hours the hapless super may have
wasted at the nod and beck of the boss, but it will
clip off quarter hours with bowelless precision
Men are kept stringing on as contingency hands
for months, perhaps a year or more. It is the
M. 1. and (J. Go. s game to string them, for with
the help of the “ battlers ” the staff can be kept
right down to the minimum. The average worker’s
eagerness for a steady job is deliberately exploited
m the Melbourne Tram Co.’s system of “ battlers ”
and it is about time the local Labor party devel-
oped an interest in the matter.
London tailors have met, and formulated
an awful scheme, which will doubtless, like the
rabbit, also invade Australia. Briefly, it is a sort
of Bertillon system whereby each individual
“nark,” “bilk,” or habitual debt-contractor
is to be identified, and refused clothing for
all time. No one man is like another, it seems, in
the length of his leg seam, and this measurement
is affected neither by increasing age nor weight.
In future, tailors propose
to supply each other with
the leg-seam figures of all
suspects, and the
Tailors’ Association
expects that mem-
bers will thereby
save about 75
per cent, of
bad debts.

at war with them), he will discover that continence
and reverence for women were admittedly among
their leading traits. The same testimony is borne
by the same unbiassed critics, of the Saxon
peoples before they came to Britain. But it’s a
tar cry from Boadicea to the State-run Yoshiwara

" Yer see, I reckoned on it bein' wet, and so, sez

Ex-Constable Crowe’s latest circular,
which has had a limited circulation in Melbourne,
and is still travelling around, contains some more
challenges for libel prosecutions. Crowe’s persist-
ence in standing forward to be legally shot at
makes a stronger impression on reasoning minds
than the counter-allegations against Crowe’s
character. To say that the publisher of red-hot
libels is notoriously untruthful, is to say that he
could be easily convicted, and ought not to go un-
punished. Until action is taken by the people
whom Crowe publicly vilifies, their private vili-
fications of him will remain open to serious
question. A man doesn’t prove his innocence by
calling his traducer a liar.

Gone are the days when John was King ;
When every kitchen maid,
With heart aflame, the back bell’s ring—
The policeman’s call—obeyed.
When all the kitchens on our beat
Flew open at our sign ;
While cooks brought forth the bread and meat,
And also beer and wine.
Brought out the bread and meat, I said,
With pies and tarts galore,
The while the Are glowed bright and red—
What could a John want more ?
Alas, alack, the new John sighs,
The nights are sad and drear ;
Gone are the tarts ; gone are the pies’;
Gone is the supper beer.
Where are the cooks we used to meet?
Surety some still are true ?
Where are the hearts that used to beat
Only for us in blue ?

DUST TO DUST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was a day in Melbourne town,
The dust blew up, the dust fell down.
The cit the devil’s aid invoked,
But in his mouth his prayer was choked.
The heavy, devastating cloud
Oppressed the poor, abased the proud,
And adepts high at eating dirt.
They had a desert for dessert.
“Oh, what a mixing there will be,”
A grinning cynic said to me,
“ If north winds blow in such a way
Upon the Resurrection Day ! ”

ABOUT A CHRISTMAS NO.?
What would be the best form of a Christmas
Number? That problem puzzled The Lone
Hand for some days. Should there be attempted
a special issue, with special Christmas stories,
Christmas verse and illustrations ? That way dull-
ness seemed to lie. The specially-written-to-order
yarn is rarely as good as the spontaneously-created
one. If a man has a good story to tell, a good
article to write, it usually comes to the office
without other than the general invitation to all
good copy.” To ask anyone to write a particu-
lar sort of story or article is to invite him (or her)
to “ pump up ” inspiration ; and inspiration
doesn’t pump well.
To make the December number of The Lone
Hand an especially good one was the final de-
cision notable for good pictures, good stories,
good verse, good articles. The November number
was hailed by over 150 Australian papers as the
best magazine overproduced in Australia,” and
easily comparable with the best foreign maga-
zines. But wait till they see the December num-
ber !
The cover is, in the first place, notable—a
charming water-color by B. E. Minns. The
frontispiece will be an exact reproduction of
Bernard Halls “Sleep.” This lovely picture—
exhibited in Sydney this year, and priced at 300
guineas— suggests the best work of Alma Tadema.
It i 3 a rich bit of decoration, and The Lone Hand
reproduction does it full justice.
Norman Lindsay contributes a second instal
ment of “Great Stories ” —with “ Helen of Troy.”
These six full-page illustrations represent quite
the best work this talented artist has turned out
to date.
The World’s Beauty Quest ” has aroused to
soft emulation the womenfolk of the Common-
wealth and Maoriland. A selection from the
beautiful pictures now pouring into the office is
published in the December Lone Hand.
“The Big Five,” a serial story by Ambrose
Pratt, begins publication in December. It is a
stirring, vigorous, up-to-date yarn of romance and
adventure in the Northern Territory.
Edward Dyson’s humorous “Battlers and a
Bear” (illustrated by Hop), Alf Vincent’s
Vagabonding in Asia,” and Norman Lindsay’s
Ihe Intruder supply the main humorous
features. Half-a-dozen stirring short stories, a
good selection of literary and other articles, some
charming verse, of which the gem is Roderic
Quinn’s “ The Sea Seekers ” (with illustrations by
Mahony), help to make up the December Lone
Hand.
It won t be a happy month for you if you miss
it.
214 G-eorge Street, Sydney.

pay a visit to Wild’s hairdressing saloon, where six
first-class artists are engaged. The establish-
ment is under the Patronage of Lord Northcote.
Address, 30 Sydney Arcade (upstairs), entrance
from George or King-streets.*

Last week Bill Lyne made a gallant
attempt to save the lives of a lot of little Aus-
tralians by imposing a prohibitive duty on
flannelette. Unfortunately Batchelor, M.P., had
remarked to his wife that it was beastly danger-
ous stuff, and that lady had told the legislator he
didn t know what he was talking about, and
Batchelor took his wife’s view as ascertained
truth. It’s a pity for Australia that Mrs.
Batchelor was not better informed. Guided by
the remarks of Mrs. Batchelor, the duty was
fixed at 10 per cent. only. But there is a mass of
indisputable evidence that flannelette is so dan-
gerous that it is a morally criminal thing to dress
young children in it. A few examples of acci-
dents that have happened to children wearing
flannelette: A child playing on the side path
suddenly ran against a lighted match a smoker
threw towards the ground. Her flannelette frock
burst into flame, and before the man could remove
Ins coat and smother the flame the child was mor-
tally in j ured. A woman wearing a flannelette under-
skirt stepped over a match to get out of a tram-
car, and the underskirt simply exploded, burning
her so that she died. Another woman, wearing a
flannelette wrapper, was heating milk on a spirit
lamp for a child s bottle, A sudden gust of wind
blew the flame against her. wrapper. She was
burned to death before assistance arrived. At
North Sydney a woman, wearing a blouse of some
fancy-patterned flannelette, lit the gas, and
dropped the match, which fell against her sleeve,
with the result that she lost the use of her arm,
and came perilously near to losing her life. These
are but. a few cases, all happening in Sydney
within the last few years.

Truly, the solicitors in a looming Melbourne
divorcecase have a fineeye for effect. The bomb was

come to her assistance was the husband himself.
Could Bland Holt desire more ?

The Yic. Railway workshops at Newport
have just turned out the first of ten huge locomo-
tives for the Melbourne to Albury express.
Hitherto the heavy loads and stiff gradients have
necessitated two engines, but the new-comer will
do the work of the two, on a smaller consumption
or coal, and with two men instead of four. The
machine, which is 01ft. long, and weighs 108 tons
could do a shade over 60 miles an hour, if the
Oent otate tracks would allow of that speed.
In Victorian banks any receiving teller
who takes in a bad coin or a note that is a forgery
or the product of a decayed institution has to
make it good out of his own pocket. In other
businesses the proprietors have to run the risks of
the game, but the average bank clerk is a tame and
submissive employee, who is expected to knuckle
down to any imposition. Naturally, bad notes
and spurious coins are frequent callers at banks,
and tellers are often taken down where the bad
’un is the product of a clever craftsman. This is
generally known, but it is not generally known
that the cronk sovereign or dickie quid is not
taken over by the bank and destroyed, but in
most cases becomes the property of the young
man who had to set it right. If he likes to put
the forgery about again it’s his own affair.
* * * *
Canoodlers on a certain seat in Albert
Park (Melbourne) have incurred the animosity of
a gentleman, aged 88, and his wife (age not
stated), and a request to remove the seat
before the hot weather sets in has been
made to the South Melbourne Council.
The request opened : “My husband, being
in his 88th year, has deputed me, his wife,
to write and beg you to take away the
seat.” The writer went on to say that
the objection to the seat was that at night
it was frequently occupied by couples who
behaved improperly, and concluded with
the remark, “I write as the warm season
is approaching so that we may, after a
warm day, sit in our verandah or balcony
and get a little fresh air, which at present
is quite impossible.” There was a long, in-
definite debate on the complaint. It did
not occur to any of the councillors to sug-
gest that the best way out of the difficulty
was for the gentleman, aged 88, and his
wife to occupy the seat themselves on hot
nights.
* * * *
A deaf and dumb man was behind
the bars at Sydney Criminal Court the
other day, and the jury settled itself down
for a wearisome, halting trial. Every-
thing had to be “interpreted” to the
accused, and when they remembered how
long it takes the average casual person
to spell out words in deaf-and-dumb
signs on his hands, even the court attend-
ants groaned. But they didn’t know the pro-
gress that has been made in deaf-and-dumb
talk. Under the circumstances, the oral or lip
method (in the development of which the
schools claim that most advance has been
made of late years) was ouc of the question;
but by a combination of hand-spelling and the
deaf-mute’s natural language of mimicry, reduced
to fixed rules, the interpreter was easily able to
keep pace with the average talkers at the counsel’s
table and in the box.

There is ample proof that Alfred Boldner
is alive and well in Melbourne, and yet a life
insurance company had paid £2OO to Alfred’s
widow on the strength of strong proof that Alf
was drowned or devoured by sharks off Red
Bluff. Boldner was swimming with friends, his
clothes were on the shore, but he was not seen to
return from the waves. For two years he was
missing, but he turned up during the Spring
racing, and his supposed widow has handed back
the £2OO insurance, although Alf is still lying
low. The resurrection of Boldner is accepted
as a demonstration of the tremendous hold
Melbourne Cup has on Australians, since when
a man thinks he knows a sure thing he can’t
remain dead, even though it may be to his
advantage to figure as legally deceased. But to
our mind the case is most remarkable as an
astonishing instance of the restoration of the
apparently drowned.
* * # •*
The Limerick competitions now raging in
London, and imitated by some of the Sydney
papers, are a comical reflection on the Gambling
Suppression Act. In Limerick gambles all
“starters” pay 6d. and contribute a line of
doggerel. The gross proceeds of the sweep are
divided into prizes. The newspapers playing the
game in Sydney dodge Wade’s Act by solemnly
declaring that the prizes go to the most literary
efforts, and that therefore it is a struggle of brains.
Well, if some of the winning efforts are the
very best sent in to certain of the competi-
tions, the literary and artistic sense of this
community is at the very lowest ebb.
But the vast majority of the Limerickers believe
that luck of some description is the main factor
winch determines the results. Hence their popu-
larity. Perhaps the editors, whose “decisions
are final,” carefully weigh the hundreds of
thousands of Limericks, one against the other, but
it’s hardly likely.
* * * *
Time somebody gave attention to the con-
dition of theN.S.W. private schools. Bogusdegrees
and titles, fraudulent prospectuses, quack-like
advertising, and touting for pupils are rife. Many
elf-styled schoolmasters are impudent adven-
turers, finding easy victims in snob parents, who
think a private college is more “classy” than
the State school. In the whole miserable trans-
action, education is the last thing thought of.
The man who displays the greatest effrontery,
and is willing to charge the least, bags the boy.
For silly and pretentious parents one is not too
sorry. But it is the young who suffer, and
through them the country. The whole system of
secondary education should be taken in hand and
regulated by the Government.
How is this for good, all-round begetful-
ness ? Twenty years ago a photo was taken of 47
young Australians, who were being charged with
knowledge at Byramul (NtS.W.) public school,
io-day, by their own united efforts, and a little
assistance from outside, they represent 168—a nett
gam of 121. This notwithstanding three deaths
and five who have not faced the altar.

First effective prosecution under the re-
cently passed M.L. Tohunga Suppression Act
occurred t’other day. Case was heard at Auckland
Magistrates’ Court, the victim being one Deer
Singh, who for masquerading as a tohunga on
divers occasions was fined £4O and awarded a
month’s strenuous toil in quod. Deer’s nationality
was against him. It is practically impossible to
get a conviction against one of the home-born
practitioners. But when Asia was gripped by the
law whole battalions of disgusted browns
testified that this particular tohunga was a fraud
and a delusion, and entire regiments of dusky
persons swore that he was the biggest thief out of
gaol.

THE PLAINT OF THE PIG.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The pig market is being discussed as a suitable site for
the proposed new Melbourne Hospital.
Here drafted into pens at last,
Tired, thirsty, hungry, shaken,
We’ve come, the suckers and the stores,
Potential pork and bacon.
We’ve heard ourselves knocked down for cash
To porky Gentile buyers,
Been driven forth, a sacrifice
To Pan, and all his fryers.
We later pigs have trod the road
Of dust to our destruction,
Resigned, for here our parents came,
And made (we’re told) no ruction.
Our race is used, in short, to come,
And in these pens to tarry
Before the knife ... we know the place
By sense hereditary.
They threaten now to make the bourne
That sends us on for curing,
A refuge for the human sick,
Hospitably alluring.
But them they mean to cure alive,
Whate’er disease has caught ’em ;
With us, alas, that act’s delayed,
And only done post mortem.
So, if Bent pleases, from new pens
We’ll reach the butchers’ slashers,
And come no more to this dear spot—
Except as breakfast rashers.
VIC.
M.E.F.

The WILD CAT COLUMN
In the N.S.W. Assembly, the other night,
Treasiirer Wadddl repeated the exaggeration that “two-
thirds of the net proceeds of the sale of lands had been
transferred to the Public Works Fund.” Now, under
Alienations,” the Auditor-General shows that, for last
year, £1,029,439 was received, and the amount, trans-
ferred to the Works Fund was £368,051 (apart from the
special grant out of the alleged surplus). Therefore, of
the total set out by the Auditor-General under the head-
ing Alienations,” barely one-third, instead of two-thirds
was transferred to the Works Fund. First of all, under
Garruthers’ tricky system, interest on unpaid balances is
excised. That interest (2£ per cent.) was, however, taken
into account when the upset price of C.P. land was fixed
at £1 per acre—£l per acre for land which in many cases
has since been sold for £5. The interest is therefore part
of the purchase money. Still Carruthers, when he was
perfecting his Works Fund trick, excised it for a start.
Then out of the balance the Treasurer takes 20 per cent •
so between those two and any other possible little deduc-
tions, instead of two-thirds, only one-third of the land
sales money goes into the fund. And as the fund is used
for renewals ” and works of that sort, the thing which
even the one-third goes into is a fraud, because renewals,
as every business man knows, ought to be provided out
of revenue ; and the concern that doesn’t provide them
out of revenue must so smash sooner or later.
• * * *
Accountant David Fell came into the N.S.W.
Budget debate with his customary valuable analysis".
His chief point was that each department—the Bail-
ways, Water and Sewerage Board, and Harbor Trust-
should be charged with its own share of the public debt
and each such department’s surplus earnings, after pay-
ing interest on its own debt, should go to reduce that
debt, or be applied to reduce the department’s charges
The accounts were at present, he said, “fuddled up”
manipulation was therefore made easy, and consequently
IS. S. Wales’ 3 per cent, stocks were quoted at 85, while
the similar stocks of Canada were worth 97. But it is
precisely because the present “ fuddlement ” lends itself
to manipulation that tricky political leaders persist in it.
Further, if the debt were allocated in this way, the whole
sinful yarn about Cohen’s money being represented by
“permanent and reproductive undertakings” would
explode with a shocking smell. According to the most
generous division, the business undertakings accounted
for only £65,303,493 of the debt on June 30 fast; another
£3,430,647 is in works taken over by the Commonwealth •
and that leaves £14,188,222 of Cohen’s money repre-
sented by roads and bridges, tanks and culverts, public
buildings and sites, and other things that don’t return
twopence a year ; and when a N.S.W. Treasurer, setting
out to rope in another million or two, produced the old
frayed gag regarding all the borrowed money bein<>- iri
permanent and reproductive works, those 14 millions
odd would rise up and laugh scornfully. To put the
commercial undertakings of the State on a business basis
the system advocated by David Fell is indispensable ; but
until N. S. Wales gets a Treasurer pledged to no-borrow-
mg there is mighty little chance of the system being
adopted, simplj r because the sort of Treasurer who wants
to lean against Cohen won’t be prepared to expose his
hand, and show that for years past the State has been
playing with a crooked card.

An advt. in the M.L. Postal Guide a few
months ago announced—“ Deposits (unrestricted in
amount) are now being received under the N.Z. Consols
Act, 1894. Interest, 3£ per cent.” Now the ML
“Official Year Book, 1895,” describing the Act of 1894*
said—“ The purpose of the Act is, by providing for an
inscription of such Consols, to give further facilities for
the safe investment of savings. Practically, it establishes
another branch of the Government Savings Bank.
The Treasurer is authorised to receive, by way of deposits
from persons in the colony, sums of money up to £5OO 000
[in the aggregate, presumably]; but the amount deposited
in any one year must not be more than £250,000.” Yet
it is this year officially announced that under the
authority of an Act thus officially described, “deposits
(unrestricted in amount) are now being received.” Who
removed the restriction ? And, if it has been removed
in whose interest has it been done ? Maoriland needs to
watch very closely its growing back-stairs liability—
the money that isn’t borrowed straight out but
is obtained furtively through the post-office, etc.
The latest figures available only run to December 31,
1905 ; yet on that date, of £8,662,023 in the post-office
savings banks, £8,457,532 was “invested in Government
or local-bodies securities ” (which is the nice way of say-
ing that the Government and local bodies have borrowed
97 per cent, of all the savings bank deposits). Now the
Postal Guide has called for unrestricted deposits
under the authority of an Act which, according to the
Government’s official year-book, very plainly restricts
them to half-a-million. It is up to somebody to explain
things.

The Maoriland Public Trust Office, which
looks after the Dominion’s inadequate sinking fund, the
Police Provident Fund, the Coal Miners’ Relief Fund, and
other public trust accounts, and also acts as trustee
for private estates :

1904 .. 10,085 61 318
1905 .. 14,491 £20,000 45,809
1906 .. 12,706 58,515
1907 .. 12,558 7i’073
Of these reserves £23,521 consists of an assurance fund
and £47,552 is undivided balance. The income for the
year, apart from interest earned by the assurance fund
amounted to £33,556 ; and of this total £15,688 was
derived from “ interest, being surplus from investments.”
This item indicates the principal difference between this
public Trust office and the ordinary private trustee co.
If the Public Trustee is appointed trustee, and the in-
vestment of the interest-bearing funds is left to his dis-
cretion, the State guarantees both capital and interest,
which is about the largest benefit that could possibly be
conferred upon beneficiaries under a will. Apart from the
security of the principal, the fact that there is no delay
in making investments is a big advantage. The interest,
however, is only fixed from time to time ; so, though a
testator knows that what he leaves will be secured for
his dependents, so far as the State can secure it, he
doesn’t know what income they will have. It would be
possible, no doubt, to guarantee that, too, but only by
building up a large assurance fund ; in fact, it is work
rather for an underwriting office than for a Trust office.
Late in 1905 the interest payable under this provision
was raised from 4 per cent, to 4£ per cent, for sums under
£3OOO, and from 3f per cent, to 4 per cent, on all larger
sums. The item, “interest, being surplus from invest-
ments,” is the amount actually earned in excess of the
amount paid to the credit of estates ; in other words, it
is really the amount paid by estates for the valuable
guarantee which they get.
The progress of the office is further shown
hereunder

These private trusts are independent of intestate estates
and the estates of mental patients and of convicts, all of
which come automatically into the control of the Public
Trust Office. The figures refer, moreover, to only the
realised portion of the estates ; the unrealised portion is
not valued. The invested funds (£2,117,924, including the
office’s own reserves) are mainly in mortgages, which
.account for £1,883,533 of the total. When it was pro-
posed that the State should guarantee interest, the people
who knew all about it said that the Trust Office was
going to be merely another agency for raising loans for
the Government by back-stair methods ; but the figures
knock that criticism out in the first round.

A mining co.’s reserves are not, however, ordinary re-
serves, just as a mining co.’s profits are not ordinary
profits. A mining co.’s so-called' profits are made by eat-
ing up the mine, so to a greater or less extent profits
include a proportion of capital. In this case, however,
though the mine still stands in the books at cost
price, in spite of the great quantities of ore taken out of
it, there are very substantial liquid assets, estimated at
£492,657, in excess of liabilities. The assets themselves
total £631,224 ; but of that amount £276,952 is repre-
sented by copper not realised, and in a violently fluc-
tuating market it is obviously impossible to fix the value
absolutely. The copper stocks on hand at the close of
the half-year totalled 1072 tons more than they did six
months previously. So, as these stocks were merely
roughly valued, even the stated profit is based largely
on an estimate too.

The average price realised for copper sold was £96 15s. 6d.
as against £lO6 9s. 3d. in the previous term, and £B5 9s.
4d. in the half-year before that. Between the last and
the previous half-year there was thus a difference of only
£9 13s. 9d. per ton ; and that would not account for any-
thing like all the fall in profits. It is obvious from this
that the large copper stocks in hand have been valued at
a very much lower price than £96 15s. 6d.— probably
lower than £6O. And as the profits are largely
based on those unknown figures, the figures are
not of very much value. It is, for instance, impos-
sible to compute from them what the actual cost of pro-
ducing copper is. (It has since been stated that the
directors have cabled to London announcing that the
stocks in hand were actually valued at £5O per ton, and
that sales have since been made at an average of £6l 55.)
There is, however, another way of estimating costs. The
working account shows expenses amounting to £201,883,
and there are also administration and prospecting ex-
penses (though some of the latter are not to be rightly
charged against Mt. Lyell copper). The total outlay was
£227,836. The gold and silver produced were worth, sav
£78,000 ; that would leave £149,836 of costs to be met by
4090 tons of copper, or at the rate of £36 12s. 6d. per ton.
Probably that is approximately the cost of producin'--
blister copper. By-products (superphosphates, etc.) would
reduce this cost a trifle, no doubt. Six months ago
when silver was worth more (that is to say, when
that metal paid more of the costs), it was calculated
working back from profits then disclosed, that copper
cost £3llBs. 7d. per ton to produce and get to market.
On the figures given just now it would appear
that costs of producing copper have gone up during
the half-year. Two causes are responsible—a slight
reduction in metal contents of ore ; an increase in costs
per ton. The latter have moved this way :
