This profit for the past year is at the remarkably fine rate
of £l6 14s. 6d. per cent, on capital (£15,350) and reserves
combined. Trustee cos.’ profits are, however, un-
certain ithings ; one rarely knows on what basis they
are made up. Probably this co. charges up com-
mission on the corpus of a trust estate as the
estate is realised; and as S. Australia has been enjoying
a remarkable run of prosperity, during which realisations
have no doubt been frequent and free, that fact would
account for the large profits lately made. But in that
case the State’s inevitable lean years will show a severe
falling, off in this co.’s profits ; and that fact must be
taken into account. So far, certainly, there is very little
cloud on S. Australia’s prospects ; the drought which is
worrying so much of N. S. Wales and Victoria has missed
the centra) State. Twelve f months ago the value of the
trust estates in this co.’s hands was £1,452,874 ; this year
(Sept. 30) the total was £1,825,700—a very fine increase of
business indeed. As probably indicating how readily land
has lately been disposed of in S. Australia, the total value
of the landed estate in hand is only £2456 more now than it
was 12 months ago, notwithstanding that the total value of
the trust estates is £372,826 more. This co.’s own funds
are mainly in Government securities and deposits ; these
together represent £22,550. It has no liabilities to the
public—a point in connection with a trust! co. which
clients, and intending clients, can view with satisfaction,
because it means that the whole of the shareholders’
funds is available for safeguarding: the interests of
beneficiaries under trusts. The so-called trustee co. that
should be viewed with suspicion is the one which turns
itself into a finance co., borrowing and lending, so that
its funds have to protect that risky business as well as
the trust business. This is distinctly not a co. of that sort.

That is the best return the co. has had for some years.
Yet, though so much improved, it is nothing for Maori-
land to be proud of. On capital and reserves in the busi-
ness, the net profit shows under £3 Bs. per cent., and that
and the heavy imports of woollen goods constitute good
prima facie evidence that the Protection against foreign
shoddy is not high enough. It is doubtful, too, whether
enough has lately been written off for depreciation.
Anyhow, besides £l5OO provided in the ordinary way for
wear and tear, £195 has been spent on replacements, and
is also now written off. Yet if the amounts previously
written off for depreciation had been adequate, plant
due for renewal would have been written down to
nothing. Still, probably the different machinery has not
been written do\Vn separately; so the incident isn’t
conclusive. The plant now stands in the books at
£59,664, so the £l5OO written off is a shade under 2£ per
cent. ; and that isn’t formidable. Like almost every
manufacturing concern in connection with which the
State isn’t game to offer proper Protection, this co.
carries a load of debt ; but it is better off than most—the
total liabilities are only £15,000, while book debts (reduced
during the year) run to £7532, and stock (manufactured
and raw material) stands at £42,119. In connection with
every such trading concern as this a very great deal
obviously depends upon the valuation of stock ; and that is
a point upon which no two or three balance sheets enable
one to form any opinion at all. All that can guide one
is the reputation of the management, backed up by the
record of the co.

has also been provided out of current revenue. Besides its
mines the co. has a valuable asset in the railway ; the
railway accounts are not, however, presented separately.
The co. is in 150,000 paid £1 shares ; and its liabilities to
the public run to only £18,202 ; and, on the other side,
the book debts alone exceed that amount.

The by-products in the dumps were, some time ago, sold
for zinc-production, and payments and deliveries are to
be made gradually. The payments as they come in are
credited to profit and loss account ; and meantime Is.
per ton is being credited to working account in respect
to the currentproduction of the same class of by-products.
On account of the dumps sold but not paid for £98,333 is
to be received by Block 10—always assuming, of course,
that the people interested can carry out their contracts.
The co. drew £144,932 for its silver-lead concentrates
during the half-year ; but £9034 of that is set aside to
provide for refunds which may be called for when the
final settlements are made, on account of the fall in
metal values. Until then the amount is held as a special
liability reserve.
Assuming that the amount mentioned above
will be called for, and that the profits therefore are as
stated, the co. made £65,786 last half-year, including the
tailings payments. That was at the' rate of £9 9s. per
ton of the 6959 tons of lead contained in the concentrates.
The average price ruling for. lead last half-year was
£2O Bs. 10d., so, after devoting the silver and
the by-products to costs, it required £lO 19s. lOd.
per ton of the lead to pay the balance of ex-
penses. This is a better figure than some other
Broken Hill cos. have shown iately. But two things
must be remembered: (1) Nothing has been deducted
from these profits to cover wear and tear ; (2) a total of
£13,113 of the profit comes from by-products, and at
present the outlook with regard to the commercial value
of this material is exceedingly doubtful. Also silver sold
at a high price last half-year—an average of 2s. 7d. per
oz., while the present price is a shade over 2s. 2d. On
the total of the silver in the concentrates the difference
means £7625. And if £7625 less were got for the silver it
would throw that much more of the costs on to lead, and
raise the total cost of producing it to £l2 Is. lOd. per ton.
This puts the very best face on things at
Block 10. It is true that costs have been heavy at Broken
Hill during the past term, mainly on account of
the great demand for labor ; but as against that
all the costs have not been charged against
current production. Even the sinking of the new main
shaft has been charged to capital—a doubtful plan, seeing
that this new shaft is being sunk, not so much to add to
the value of the mine as to replace a working which is in
peril. That expenditure will have to be written off out of
profits some time; and a heavy contribution might weH
have been made during a period when metals books
such high prices. Even the main shafthavimrbeen
as an asset for £14,637, a miserable little £l ? 2 ? o h
provided for writing-down during the past*tivelv

handled ; but it looks just now as if a strong effor
being made to paint the situation in the very ft

* * * ■,
Writer was in Newcastle a few days ago, ana
only saw one motor-car, and it wasn’t a miner w
riding in it. Yet, judging by figures one reads, one
would suppose that just about every other Newcastle
coal-miner could afford a Napier turn-out
Melba’s. According to a list published “V
mine-owners the Co-operative men, for instance,
earned up to 21s. 4d. per day in October and 225. Id. in
September. But those highest earnings are very different
from what the owners themselves say the average ear -
i ngs are. For October, according to their list, these averages
ranged from 15s. lOd. at Lambton B pit down to 10s. 50. aG
Elermore. Those, however, are not average daily earnings.
They are only the earnings on the days when there was
work for the miner. A coal-mine owner doesn t DreaK
coal to let it lie at surface. He only breaks it w h®ri he
can dispose of it. If there is a ship in wanting coal there
there may be a full fortnight (11 days). If there
is no ship in, there is no work. Newcastle has
been filled with shipping lately, so _, th ® re , ha ®
been far more regular work than usual. The tact was
mentioned just now that on one day in October a coal-
miner earned 21s. 4d. But go back to the corresponding
month of 1904, and for all October the Co-operative colliery
only worked 2J days. Take the last fortnight in each of the
two Octobers—prosperous 1907, and only moderately pros-
perous 1904 : the men had lOdays work one fortnight, and
If days the other fortnight; total for four weeks, Ilf days.
Now, during October, 1907, the owners claim that the Co-
operative men averaged 12s. 9d. Well, take it at that:
Ilf days at 12s. 9d. = 1495. 9d., or an average for the
four weeks of 375. sfd. per week. And out of that the
miner has to pay for explosives and also the wages of the
check-weighman, whom the law compels the miners to
employ and pay.
* " ‘ t
Still it isn’t suggested that collieries often work
as little as 2f days a month, as the Co-operative did in
October, 1904. But it is a fact that, in many cases, they
do not work more than six days a fortnight. Last month,
Duckenfield worked full time (11 days) per fortnight; in
October, 1903, it worked only eight days in the four weeks.
Figures for 1905 are not available, but here they are for
four years :—October, 1903, 8 days ; 1904, 12 days ; 1906,
21J days ; 1907, 22 days. For the four years, that averages
just under 16 days a month (four weeks). During October
the owners say the average earnings were 10s. lOd. At
that rate, for those four Octobers, the average earnings
would be £2 3s. 4d. per week—again less cost of explo-
sives, etc. Duckenfield appears to be a fair average of
the lot. It is certainly not worse than the average.
Such an old-established concern as the Wallsend, for
instance, worked only 14 days in October, 1903, and 13
days in October, 1904—an average of 3f days per week.
It is tolerably clear, then, that the public must have been
badly deceived by the figures that have been published
about the earnings of the miners. To send such figures
out may be warfare, but it isn’t justice.
* » *
This strike at Newcastle will probably give the
old Stockton colliery its knock-out. Situated right at
the entrance to the harbor, the present co., which has
been digging away for just upon 25 years, has pursued the
seamoutto sea until the slopingbedoftheoceanisnowonly
about 120 ft. above the coal seam ; and at that point trouble
is bound to begin. In fact, British engineers would pro-
bably have stopped before ; Professor Arnold Lupton, a
well-known t’otherside authority on the subject, said
lately—“ The small thickness of ground left between the
bottom of the sea and the coal-seaifi (120 ft.) proves that
Australian engineers have strong nerves.” Stockton has
had troubles with fire, too ; and the Mines Department
has for some time past been frowning upon the further
working of the old mine. So the co. has come up the
line and acquired a leasehold of about four square miles
at Teralba, and it is not likely that there will be any more
regular coal-winning at Stockton.
* * * *
Broken Hill is beginning to have a very gloomy
feeling about its water supply. How much water there
is in its one reservoir, at Stephens Creek, nobody knows
—not even the co.’s officials. For the Stephens Creek
reservoir really consists of a big weir built across a sandy
creek where it passes through a gorge, and how much
the shallow basin has silted up no one knows for certain.
The official idea appears to be that the supply may last
till towards the end of February ; and to the visitor that
estimate seems to be stretched as far as it will hang to-
gether. The end of February looks a good way off in dis-
tricts that think they are parched if they don’t get some
sort of a shower once in six weeks ; but the Barrier isn’t
one of those districts. If rain doesn’t fall before the end
of February, all but one or two of the mines will have to
shut up shop ; for the concentrating mills can’t work
without water, and only one or two of the smaller cos.
can draw enough from underground supplies to keep
going. ' '■
* * » *
Broken Hill Block 14 report, just issued, must
be considered with caution. The profit was £78,170, less
depreciation £4179, leaving £73,991 net. Out of this the
10 per cent, preference capital (£30,000) took a first bite
of £l5OO, leaving £72,491 available for distribution among
all shares. This is a fine result for a co. which was
so hard up as Block 14 lately was—how hard up is shown
by the fact that it had to offer a 10 per cent, preference
plus ordinary rights, in order to raise £30,000 of capital in
6s. shares. Caution, however, is necessary because of
the large proportion of profits which came from
the carbonates. Deliveries of this ore amounted
to 10,622 tons, while only 5924 tons of sulphide
concentrates were produced from 46,301 tons of
ore. The receipts from the two classes of ore
are not separated; and, as even the “probable”
carbonates only run to 18 months’ supply at the recent
rate of extraction, the accounts are not much use without
that information. It is very doubtful whbther the sul-
phides, v/hich will have to be relied upon after the car-
bonates have gone, were responsible for more than
£25,000 of the profit; the balance came from zinc tailings
(£7500) and the carbonates, the latter worth 34'5 per
cent, for lead and 13oz. for silver.
* * * *
British Broken Hill has a lot of very fine ore
below the 300 ft. level of Marsh shaft. In the main work-
ings, however, rhodonite is making ore-breaking expen-
sive, and it will mar metal recoveries. The eastern vein,
on which the British has been compelled to stake a good
deal, carries a large amount of this mineral. The face
of the north drive from Howell’s shaft to Thompson’s, on
the 800 ft. level, has been almost filled with rhodonite for a
week past, and the ore only occurs in bunches. This is
the northward continuation of the eastern vein, where it
swings round a little from the eastward.
* » * »
Poorer ore is coming, in at both ends of the
900 ft. north stope in the Broken Hill Junction. This
stope has yielded well for a long time past, and has main-
tained a good grade all the while. It is disappointing to
find that its southern continuation seems to be so soon
ending. On the Junction North boundary the fact that
a bar of rhodonite appeared doesn’t much matter, as the
work at that end was only being done to cleat the ground
about the hauling winze, and could only have been
carried on for about 50ft., anyway. Difficulties are being
experienced in the sinking of Browne shaft.
* * It *
Yambulla field (N.S.W.) naturally divides
itself into two parts. The big mine—the Yambulla —is
owned by Miradian and Pola, one an Armenian, the other
an Italian. It carries 22 men, all told, with a 10-head
battery. The shaft is down 260 ft. on a reef from 14ft. to
15ft. wide. Indeed, the output exceeds the battery
capacity, which is 60 to 70 tons per week on a two-shift
basis. Last crushing yielded 1250 z. from 175 tons. In
addition, there were over a ton and a half of concentrates,
which gave an average of 7oz. per ton at Cockle Creek,
where a hand-picked parcel of over 10 tons resulted in an
average of 12£oz. of fine gold and 9oz. of silver. In
financially strong hands, there should be a future before
this big body of ore. Rest of the field is capped for about
50ft. with a soft, decomposed granite, which affords em-
ployment to 30 or 40 fossickers. At the depth, blasting
is necessary, and the stone doesn’t pay for the uncertain
sulphides in it. Through the cap run erratic, pipey veins
of rubble carrying free gold. A few miles further, and in
the wilds of Croajingalong, is Stephens’ New Chum, just
sold to a Vic. syndicate. On assay, this has repeatedly
given 600 oz. of silver, and from 6oz. to 12oz. fine gold.
The whole of this country, from Monaro edge to the coast
flats, would be worth inspection. At present it is
hampered'by being close to Nowhere, and bounded by
jumbuckB on one side and the Holy Cow on the other.

-The Aerial Tramways which have been erected
for the British and Sulphide Corporation (Central) Mines
at Broken Hill are fine examples of present-day skill and
technical knowledge. The Mines will effect considerable
saving by means of these Aerialways as compared with
any other known mechanical contrivance for handling
ores on a large scale. The British Mine will economise
to over 2s. per ton. These two plants have already been
subjected to severe tests, and have amply demonstrated
their worth and ability. Both Aerialways were supplied
by W. & J. Lempriere, of Melbourne, who represent A.
Bleichert & Co., of London, the largest firm in the world
for the manufacture of transport appliances.*

The introduction into the Broken Hill and
Western Australian Mines of a small hand rock-drill for
boring “ pops ” to break up large blocks of ore, cutting
“hitches” for timber, and squaring up shafts and levels,
has quite revolutionised the old method of working with
hammers and gad.- The best machine for this work is the
“Shaw’’Hand Rock Drill and is manufactured by the
Austral Otis Engineering Company, Ltd., Melbourne,
who are the sole agents. The machine weighs only 161bs.
and has onlv one working part—the hammer —which
serves the purpose also of a valve. One man with this
machine can easily do as much work as four men with
hammer and drill. Ir some of the hardest lodes in the
Boulder these machines bore over lin. a minute, and in
softer country as much as 2-tin. per minute. Unlike other
types, the operator does not experience any excessive
vibration due to the recoil of the hammer. The working
pressure is 801bs. per square inch and the machine uses
about 25 cubic feet of free air per minute. Ten of the
biggest mines in West Australia are now using the Shaw
Hand Rock drills. Fullest particulars may be obtained
from the Company’s offices in South Melbourne, where
the machine may be seen working.* [c]

The Electrolytic Refining and Smelting
Company of Australia, Ltd., have placed their order for
complete boiler-house equipment with Babcock and
Wilcox Ltd., Haymarket, Sydney. The order, which com-
prises five large boilers, each with superheater and
mechanical stoker, one economiser and two induced
draft fans, was obtained in keen competition with other
makes of water-tube boilers. Babcock and Wilcox have
also received an order from the same firm for a Reavell
Air Compressor for which they are sole licensees in
Australia.* ,

* * * «
It was plucky but foolish of the Pinnacles Co.
to pioneer the treatment of crude Barrier silver-lead sul-
phide ore by the Elmore vacuum process. After many
weeks of expensive trials, the management has evidently
realised that, although the Elmore process may give ex-
cellent results under certain conditions, it makes
but a feeble struggle with the heavily mineralised
crude ore of the Pinnacles. The two Elmore units in-
stalled at the Pinnacles mill were supposed to have each
a 25-tons a day capacity. They didn’t treat more than 30
tons per da}' between them. The co. has had to pay
pretty dearly for its experience, and now humbly reverts
to the old wet concentration. A new plant, to treat
about 400 tons per week, will have to be installed, and
this will take time. Meanwhile the co. continues to lose
the benefit of a good lead market. Also, it is left with
an expensive vacuum plant on its hands, which can only
be effectively used for the treatment of tailings. Had
the wet concentration plant been erected in the first
place, all this loss of time and waste of money would not
have occurred. It is only another illustration—Broken
Hill has had several—of the fallacy of accepting the re-
sults given by small experimental plants as proof of com-
mercial efficiency under all circumstances.
* vt * *
Cobar, 475. 6d. —the latest London quotation.
A month or two back, when the madness raged, they
passed £l2. It is poor consolation to the man who paid
£l2 to be told that in the long run he may get his
money back. Besides the long waiting for it and
the risk that he won’t get it after all, he
realises that he could get nearly three shares
now for. what he paid for one. In London they have
been trying to explain the slump. The decline in the
copper market is, of course, the biggest factor ; yet it is
stupid not to recognise that the price is still good as long
as it keeps above £53. Apart from that, there has been a
disquieting fall in the metal contents of the ore.
In view of the latter, the shareholders begin to look
anxiously at the heavy swag of debentures, and
the undertaking to put aside £lOO,OOO a year,
beginning with next year, to pay off this liability. With
poorer ore and a weaker market, this £lOO,OOO a year (to
be paid into a sinking fund and invested in the deben-
tures till the whole issue of £550,000 is provided for)
will make a big hole in even the Great Cobar’s
profit, especially when the 6 per cent, debenture in-
terest (£33,000 a year) has also to be cut out
of the same cake. The prospectus figured on copper first
at £Bsper ton, and subsequently at £7O per ton ;at £B5
per ton the present plant, producing 4000 tons a year,
could earn 15 per cent. —the amount that was actually
paid the first year—while the enlarged plant was ex-
pected to produce 13,000 tons of copper and make
enough profit to pay three times that dividend
with copper at £7O. The shrinkage in metal
value means a reduction in output of metal without
a reduction of total costs; and even a 10 per cent,
reduction, knocking off 1300 tons, means also knocking
off £91,000 of profit from the prospectus estimate, equal
to 12J per cent, on capital. The difference between an
output of 11,700 tons of copper a year at £6O and the
prospectus estimate of 13,000 tons at, £7O is £208,000,
equal to over 27 per cent, on the share capital ; and on
recent figures it seems prudent to write off no less
an amount than that from the promoters’ estimates.
* * * *
One little Australian industry that is steadily
battling away is graphite production, and the working-
up of the mineral into foundry facings, crucibles, stone-
polish, and, now, lead-pencils. The mineral comes mainly
from the Wide Bay district (Q.), and the manufacture
is carried on in Sydney. It was slow' work at first; but
the co. established a good name for its foundry facings in
particular, and now having reached, if it has not passed,
the standard of its European competitors, it begins to
look abroad for a foreign market, and has already made
some small shipments.
* * * *
Total dividends paid by Westralian mining
cos. to the end of October amounted to £17,158,285.
The principal contributors have been : Great Boulder,
£2,578,675; Horseshoe, £2,430,000 ; Orova-Brown Hill,
£2,011,241 ; Ivanhoe, £1,868,750 ; Finga'll, £1,543,750 ;
Lake View Consols, £1,378,750 ; Perseverance, £1,286,250 ;
Kalgurli, £585,000; Associated, £530,457; Associated
Northern, £525,000 ; and Sons of Gwalia, £458,175. This
year’s total will probably run out at a shade over
£1,600,000, as against £1,992,000 for 1906, and £2,167,639
for 1905. For some time past the Boulder, Ivanhoe,
Horseshoe, Kalgurli, Oroya-Brown Hill and Fingall have
accounted for nearly five-sixths of the money distributed ;
but as the two last-named are fading fast, and as the rest
cannot be expected to stand the present strain much
longer, J. Bull had better be prepared for a further
shrinkage in his income. What the West badly wants,
and what apparently it hasn’t got, are new' mines to fill
the gap when the few mines which now earn the bulk of
the dividends become worked out.
» * * *
Lake View Consols income for the year ending
June 30, 1907, amounted to £176,150, and a profit of
£21,593 was netted. This was equal to 3s. Id. per ton of
ore treated. The unappropriated balance of profit from
the previous year brings the total to £26,642. Of this
sum £7BOO has gone in interest in the Siberian Gold
Dredging Co., a Is. dividend (which entitled the general
managers to a cut of £174) has been paid ; and the balance
of £l2OO has been carried forward. Working results
for the year show that costs have been further
reduced to 18s. 6d., and this offsets the small decrease
show’n in the grade of the ore reserves. The mine’s finance
account includes all the outside interests acquired prior
to June 30 last. The 58,525 paid Broken Hill South
Blocks, which are now' marketable at a shade under par,
besides £28,770 of Burma Mines debenture stock and
shares (real value unknowm), stand in the books at £22,931.
The cost of the remaining shares and debentures, made
up of interests in Peru, Siberia, and elsewhere, has been
entirely written off. In addition, the co. has purchased
at a cost of £6400 more than four-sevenths interest in an
unnamed Westralian developing mine, with an option on
the balance of its shares. The report says that the ore iii
sight is estimated at 20,000 tons, from which a profit of at
least 15s. per ton is expected, and the co. owning this
show has a working capital of £ll,OOO and a calling
power of £17,600. Although the name of this property
has been withheld, it may be taken for granted that it is
the Golden Link, Kalgoorlie. Besides these assets, the
co. had at the time the accounts w'ere made up cash an d
liquid assets, including loans, amounting to £112,415.
The principal features of the year’s operations in the
Lake View mine itself has been the further development
of low' grade ore in the bottom levels, and the reserves
now in sight assure the payment of at least another
Is. 6d. in dividends. The life of the property as a divi-
dend earner hangs, how'ever, on a trembling balance.
Golden Ridges are being sought after in the
West. Twelve months ago shares were to be had for ss. :
now they cannot be bought for 305., and are being talked
up to £2. If they reach this last figure the property will
only have a market valuation of a little over £62,000. As
the monthly profit now exceeds £2OOO, and the ore re-
serves are quite 18 months ahead of the mill, the profit in
sight is from £35,000 to £40,000. For the rest, all depends
on what the reef is worth at 400 ft. If anything unfavor-
able happens there, shares may, on account of the limited
market in the scrip, fall sharply.
**‘ * *
Another estimate of ore reserves has been made
by the Boulder Perseverance officials. Contrary to
general anticipation, the figures show little alteration
from those given some time back, when 383,410 tons,
valued at £708,531, and 226,192 tons, nominally worth
bdwt. per ton, were said to be insight. In view of the
poor way this mine is shaping in depth, it is somewhat
astonishing to learn that the reserves are not showing any
serious depletion. However, the management says they
are not, and there the matter ends for the time being.
The Kalgurli Co. (W.A.) is about to try the
experiment of appointing E. S. Black—a commercial
man—as general manager. This arrangement may work
out all right, but, taking into account the many mining
and metallurgical problems involved in the running of
this mine, it is also liable to turn out all wrong.
Last month, Associated Northern (W.A.) made
a profit of £8509 from the treatment of 68s. ore. This
surplus is about £l7OO short of that for September, when
the average was 78s. 3d. Seeing that the grade is to be
further lowered—first to 605., and then to about 50s.—a
corresponding reduction in dividends must follow. Taking
the latest estimate of ore reserves as a basis, and allowing'
for losses in residues and cost of production, the profit in
sight now works out at about 65 per cent, of the mine’s
present market valuation. But, besides, the co. has a
cash reserve of about £150,000, equal to about Bs. per
share.

From an uninterested man on the spot: “Daily
papers infer that the underlay of the Oroya-Black Eange
shoot has been cut at a depth of 180 ft. in the adjoining
Sandstone Development Co.’s Juno lease. Wrong ! The
shaft in which the strike w’as made lies several hundred
feet north of the other co.’s workings, and the ore met
with is not part of the Black Eange shoot at all. As
pointed out in B. 20/6/’O7, the Sandstone Co.’s Juno lease
must get a bit of this shoot if it holds down, but as the
gold dips south, values will soon pass once more into
Black Eange ground. The yarn about the weakness of
Oroya-Black Eanges being due to the strike alluded to is
also wrong, while the tale spun by a Perth paper about
the public having been induced to buy Oroya shares up
to 30s. before the first crushing, on the strength of official
hints that ounce crushings were to be maintained, is pre-
varication. As far back as June last, it was announced
(The Bulletin printed it) that the management intended
to set its face against ‘ window dressing,’ and run on a
grade that could be maintained.”
* • * *
J. S. Read, of Perth, agent for the vendors of
the Wheal Ellen (W.A.), in defence of the Melbourne
flotation of that failure : “ The property was purchased (1)
on its past record—viz., some 250 tons of ore yielding
25000 z. gold, valued at £10,000; (2) on the specimens,
which undoubtedly came from the mine ; and (3) on the
Government engineer’s report, backed up by other re-
ports. ... The shares were simply rushed, although no
prospectus was issued. . . I say emphatically that no
misrepresentations were made, and if shareholders want
to take action, the sooner the better.” The same old
story about the reef being shipped over in a box for flota-
tion purposes, and the same old yarn about a new crop of
greedy fools rushing in and getting hurt.
* * * *
A split has occurred in the Callion camp, and
some dirty linen may be washed in public at any time.
At present the bulk of the scrip is held in the AVest, and
a strenuous effort is about to be made to get rid of the
Adelaide Board and remove the office to Kalgoorlie.
This, however, is not likely to be effected without a
struggle. Meantime, the mine is shut down, funds are
low, an overhaul of the accounts is being asked for, the
sale of another lot of forfeited shares is about to take
place, and more calls are looming ahead. The manage-
ment claims that a five-head mill would show a monthly
profit of £4OO from the oxidised ore; but that estimate
needs expert backing.

Randall’s (W.A.) is about punctured. Some
time ago the Westralian press was reporting almost every
week the great value of this field, and, although men
like Hamilton of the Boulder turned up their noses at it,
. bad mines were proclaimed good, and the boom had its
way. Finally the Government mill got to work, and then
the boom faded. So far only one shoot has been dis-
covered, and this seems to go to pieces at about 100 ft.
from the surface.
« * * •
Nothing more cheerful can be said about the
Moolyella tin-field (W.A.). Its mainstay—McDonald’s
claim—is easing off, while the returns froni other alluvial
shows are rapidly declining. The deposit occurs in the
form of a thin layer of tin scattered over a large area,
and bedrock is reached at 25ft. from the surface. Owing
to the extreme fineness of the particles, recovery is, under
present conditions, difficult. Perth Herald still beats its
little boomster’s drum, however, and resuscitates the silly
lie about The Bulletin “maintaining an attitude of im-
placable hostility to everything Westralian.” The last
time Perth papers trotted out that dear old gibe was
when they were busy booming Golden Pole and this
paper wasn’t. Where is that party now?
* * * *
Shierlaw’s Perseverance has been recommended
for forfeiture, and Webster’s Find has been abandoned to
its fate. These are two of the wild cats comprised in the
Westralia Esperanza, one of the assets of the Coolgardie
Gold Syndicate, which has “ great possibilities ahead of
it,” according to the official story told to the languishing
shareholders in London, less than three months ago.
* • * *
Bendigo miners want a bit more recompense
in return for contracting miners’ complaint in the deep
mines, and sweating out life below. Bendigo mine-owners
say “ No,” and it is well to tapeyard up the reason given.
Stanfield, speaking for them, says that most of the miners
in the face are getting. £2 ss. per week. Well, supposing
they are. Is £2 ss. a wage on which to bring up a family
while making provision for a possible early disablement ?
Stanfield says that 112 cos. are at work, and that calls
show up worse than divs. Here are his figures
Excess of divs. Excess of calls.
1905 £54,728
1906 23,300
1907 (six months).. £69,000
This certainly looks bad for the “Golden City.” Stan-
field adds that £400,000 is paid annually in wages in
Bendigo, and that the whole of the 1905, excess of divs.
would have been mopped up if the Bs. 4d. standard wage
had been universal. This may be true ; but the point he
has not figured,out is, do the cos. get better work by
sweating than by paying a fair wage? Qan they keep,
good men at Bendigo at Bs. 4d., which isn’t the average,
when 12s. to 14s. a shift can be earned elsewhere?. Poor,
wages mean poor service. Also, poor wages very often
tend to malpractice, such as gold stealing, simply because
Want has to be kept from the door.
* * * *
Band and Loch, Ballarat, is still the “stringer”
it has been for years past, and it will remain so until the
heroic step is taken. That step is to sink the main shaft
and work without any dog’s-hind-leg business. A break
cut off the shoot worked at, 2030 ft., so that groping is
being done at 2300 ft. The idea is that the gold has
pitched north. Somehow Nature, when playing pranks
with formations, has a sad knack of also cutting off the
gold or leaving it only close to the break. How the co.
has come down as a gold producer is shown by the record
of 624 tons for 3220 z. as the half-year’s work.
* * * *
Williams’ Fancy, Berringa (Vic.), after a long
rest, has hit upon a patch and got back to the div. list!
The sixpence per share disbursed raises total profits
divided to £15,000. It is told with great solemnity that
no one knows whence the specimen stone came
that it was only found in the bin at the
battery, and that it is supposed to have come
from No. 5 level north. This little bit of gossip gives the
history of a lot of Berringa mining. Pockets of gold are
found, and are used to keep the poor mass “ sweetened ”
until they are exhausted. Then the yields fall back to
about. 4dwt., and after that comes the tussle to keeD the
wolf out of the door.

Port Arthur, Piggoreet (Vic.), is passing into a
decline. Shares tumbled when the bottom level failed to
come up to expectations. The shaft is being sunk
another 100 ft., and the prospects for the next level may
be better if a slide does not coine in.
The Duke of YVellington, Bangamie (Vic.), is
now out of debt, owing to better returns from recently
discovered stone. A Melbourne crowd, opposed to a
Melbourne-Ballarat combination, is fighting for control
and between the two the public is liable to get some
bumping.
* * * *
General Manager Sticht, of Mt. Lyell, reports
ore in sight in North Lyell mine on September 30 as 438,020
tons, against 356,464 tons six months before. Average
grade of copper in this is 575 per cent., or £ per cent,
above the previous ore contents ; so that there is im-
provement in quality and quantity. Taking the output
of North Lyell ore at 120,000 tons per annum, the reserve
gives 3£ years’ silicious ore in sight; without further
finds the co. would, after that, be in extremis
with copper at £6O. The co. is, however, opening
out at 1000 ft., and next half-year should see
an addition to the reserves. Allowing another 120,000
tons for that development, a year’s reserves is tacked on.
Most of the ore now comes from 700 ft., with 600 ft. stopes
next, and the 400 ft. and 300 ft. drawn on slightly.
Shareholders, above all, have to keep an eye on what goes
on below 600 ft., if they want to tell how the wind blows.
Sticht’s estimates, made when the Mt. Lyell Co.
fused the North Lyell on to itself in 1904, was
Mt. Lyell ore, available for open cutting, 1,613,198 tons.
Now (Sept. 30) that total is 1,054,566 tons, notwith-
standing that meantime about 1,000,000 tons have been
beaten out. The difference is made up by the extension
of the upper benches, and the deepening of the point to
which open-cut work can be done. But there has been
a reduction of metal contents to 0.60 per cent, copper.
The North Lyell estimate in 1904 was 170,494 tons down
to 500 feet. In Sept., 1906, it was 322,161 tons down
to 700 feet. Now it is 438,020 tons down to 850 feet,
with the average 5.75 per cent, copper, 1.330 z. silver,
and 0.0050 z. gold per ton. Since 1904 some 400,000 tons
have been won, so that Sticht’s original estimate was
wonderfully safe, and it is fair to assume that he has
been equally cautious on the present occasion.
* * * *
A strike of tin ore in the Vulcan shaft at 1300 ft.
is the best news North Queensland has had for many
weeks. It proves that N.Q. is not all shallow. When
shares kept on declining in face of a high metal market
it discouraged most people ; but many old N. Queens-
landers who follow John Moffat have as much faith in the
Vulcan as in the Scriptures ; so scrip never went below
ss. The output for October was 1000, tons of fair-grade
ore. Hopes will now revive that the mine will resume
the payment of the monthly threepenny divs., which, on
264,000 shares, require 453300 per month.
* » * *
Charters Towers’ dividends for the first 10
months of the year were £84,337 ; the calls ran up to
£69,804. Partly because of an improvement here and
there in the mines, but mainly because the field got tired
of the drain , and took a hand in the copper gamble, this
year's calls show a severe shrinkage compared with last
year’s, when to the end of October £107,834 was got in.
• * » *
At the Golden Alexandra, Charters Towers,
development work is being carried on at No. 6 level,
506 ft. vertical. This level was extended 200 ft. east of
the underlay, where a dyke or vertical crosscourse of
rotten diorite cut off the reef, and it was no easy matter
to pick it up again east of the dyke. A crosscut was put
in 70ft. into the hanging wall, and then another crosscut
was driven about the same distance into the footwall.
Two veins a few feet apart have been cut—the first one
at 45ft. on the footwall side—and if this is the same reef
as that worked on the western side of the dyke, it has
faulted considerably. The reef is very flat, and levels are
only a short vertical distance apart. With breaks and
blanks and a narrow vein, mining comes expensive, so
there is nothing very certain about dividends. The last
crushing of 637 tons yielded £3300 ; but it took a long
time to get. The recent development east of the dyke
was required to prolong the life of the mine. There is a
length of more than 500 ft. to the eastern boundary, but
in broken country that is not enough to get enthusiastic
about.
• * * *
Accounts of the Carrington United, the outside-
Charters Towers gold mine which has largely turned
itself into a copper proposition, have been issued. The
revenue account shows a loss of £4675 for the half-year,
including £260 provided for depreciation. But as no
credit has been taken for the ore broken and awaiting
treatment, the figures are unreliable. Harris, lately of
Queen Bee, Cobar, who is now in charge, thinks well of
the property, and has got everything ready for regular
production of blister copper. Costs will not be low, but
the gold contents will give the co. a big lift. Harris
contemplates a preliminary kiln roasting for the firsts and
milling for the seconds.
* •* * *
Gilmore’s (N.Q.) crushed at Gurrumba 136 tons
of stone for a money value of £2340. Another 100 tons
gave 21 tons of black tin, but the stone, taken from the
lower level of the Midas, is more pyritic, and cannot, at
Gurrumba mill at least, be dressed to a high grade. For
the first time in the history of the co. the tin was sold by
tender, and was secured by Lempriere, of Herberton.
Probably in the future samples will be submitted to
buyers outside the' Irvinebank Co. The co. is handi-
capped by the pack-team system of carting ore to the
mill. It costs £1 per ton, which, with bags, bagging,
and sewing, means 255.. per ton, though the distance is
only seven miles and the road is good. Yet it is alleged
that there are teamsters ready to jump the job at iss.
per ton. There is some talk of the co. erecting a calci-
ning plant, similar to the one in use at Smith’s Creek.
* * * «
Professor Gregory doesn’t fall into line with
some other geologists, including Government Geologist
Pittman, of N.S.W., about the cause of the artesian
water supply. Gregory says the cause is local, and due
to the earth’s heat, and there is the !80deg. Fahrenheit
recorded at the Dagworth bore in Queensland to give that
theory a lift. ' The generally accepted theory is that the
supplies are really the drainage from soft; porous intake
• beds Which soak up the heavy rainfall, probably of North
Queensland in particular. At Blantyre, near the Hughen-
den railway, they have been sinking a shaft for coal, and
the powerful pumps have great difficulty, in keeping the
water down'; so there is a vast natural reservoir of water
200 miles away from the artesian country. This natural
reservoir may be connected with the intake beds.
* 1 * « «
The analyses of the iron ore brought lately from
Yampi Sound, away on the extreme north-west of
W.A., have'proved very satisfactory. The co.—the Aus-
tralian Prospecting Association—which sent a party
round to knock samples off the deposits, consists mainly
of Charters Towers men, most of them interested in the
Iron Island Co., which is supplying Mt. Morgan with
flux. Assays show from 64’9 per cent, to 71‘9 per cent,
of iron, and o's to 7 percent, silica ; while the highest
sulphur contents were o'o9l per cent. Of phosphorus the
worst record was;o’64 per cent., and three samples out of
four showed less than l-35th of 1 per cent. The deposits
are very large, and right alongside water, and, though
remote, it is expected that a profitable market will be
found for ore at once. Work is to be started there in
April, when 'a wharf and ore bins will be built.
The Tasmanian Copper Co.’s S. Australian
people have got a move on, and, spurred by the Mines
Department, have let three of their Far North properties
on tribute. But now comes a knock to some of the miners
who had been hanging on in the hope of getting tributes :
Blinman smelters are to be shortly closed, and no more
ore is to be taken there. A cry is therefore being raised
fora State smelter; but, after its previous disastrous
experience in that line, the Government will think hard
before it does anything. One proposal is that the State
should lease the Tasmanian Oo.’s never-used Mountain of
Light works, at Leigh’s Creek ; but the, movers in the
matter haven’t come forward with any guarantees to
supply certain minimum quantities of ore ; and that is
the first essential.
* • * *
Shareholders in the Taranaki (M.L.) Petroleum
Co. asked for information at the first annual meeting,
and got it. The manager, W. A. Simpson, reported that
the Birthday well, which was much boomed what time
many prominent shareholders were “unloading,” was
full of water... obstructions. .. strainer pipe, and lost
casing.” The co. had been told it was a 400-gallon well.
If 198 were deducted from the 200 and the remainder-
divided by two, it would be nearer the actual result. But
that didn’t say there was no oil in Maoriland. He was
confident that there was, but he was not going to say
they’d get it.... As to the future, he recommended that
the directors continue operations on No. 3 down to a
sufficient depth to thoroughly test the Moturoa district,
in the meantime closing down all the other bores.”

“Imagine a perfect cube of coal 9ft. sin. in
height and breadth and width. Then out of a corner of
that cube break off one cubic foot. That shovelful will
represent all that has been taken ; the hardly scratched
remainder will represent what is left of the workable part
of the N.S. W. coastal coalfields.”-—From a Lone Hand
(November)article on “The Mineral Industry.”

“ G-.5.K.” : Have struck the Meanest
Smoker. He smokes the butt of his four-fifth
consumed cigar from the point of a penknife, and
thereafter, Jwhen matters grow very warm indeed,
thrusts the reeking remainder into his pipe, and
so finishes it to the last fragment of cabbage stalk.
“ Wake ” : Has any Aboliar noted that the
magpie has this year built his nest on the strongest
limbs available, and does it mean much wind ?
Seems so here (South Aus.). Re “Nil’s” com-
plaint (7/11/’O7) that no patriot has preserved
native names of the lovely Adelaide hill flowers.
Perhaps they never had any. Natives were scarce
in those parts. Cordially agree with “Nil’s”
estimate of the beauty of the flowers.
A “BUNNY” FREAK.
Illustration showing skin of a rabbit shot at
Medlow Bath (N.S.W.), on which Nature has
photographed another “bunny” outline. The
Medlow Bath Office, Sydney, holds the skin.
“R.S.R.”: A method of dealing with
snorers. Once whilst putting in al5 weeks’ stay
in the accident ward of a big hospital, I struck a
snorer of the bassoon variety. He was unbearable.
I had heard tell of a cure, and I tried it. Every
time he snored, I whistled, not shrilly but just a
gentle whistle, keeping in good time with his
snore. He stood it for about a dozen snores, then
gave a deep sigh, turned over on his side, and
they woke me at seven next morning. I asked
the snorer about it. He said he did not hear me
whistle. I found the whistle remedy effective
in many cases after that.
“Ja no r” : I
watched a snake get-
ting out of a shal-
low shaft the other
day. He worked
himself into an up-
right position in one
corner until extend-
ed full length—
about 6ft.-—so that,
while his head was
level with the sur-
face his other ex-
tremity (commonly
spoken of as a tail)
rested on the shaft
bottom. Bending
his head at a right
angle with his body,
he got a good hold
of the edge of the
shaft opening with
his chin, or rather
the place where that
feature should have
been. There was a
sudden shortening
of the reptile, and
about a foot of his
business end shot
over the edge of the
shaft. With this he
took a fresh grip,
and another concer-
tina-like movement
landed him on deck.
Seems that snakes
haven’t a possible
of getting out of a
shaft deeper than
their length ; for
large numbers are
killed in shallow
shafts about alluvial
workings. But why
do such cunning,
agile beasties fall
into shafts at all ? I
know they fall down
accidentally, for,
working once in a
16ft. shaft, I was
surprised by a vio-
lent blow between
the shoulders.
Straightening my-
self to make some commonplace but strenuous
remarks to the man on top, I caught sight of a
big tiger snake between my two large boots. I
was quicker with the shovel blade than the tiger
was with his hypodermic outfit, so I am still very
much alive.
“F.H.R.”: “Yarrum” (B. 7/11/’O7)
says a snake could not break an egg if it tried.
Couldn’t it ? Consider the things a snake can
swallow —rabbits, birds, etc., to say nothing of its
own offspring—and then consider the; way a snake
must squeeze a rabbit before the meal passes
through the throat to the stomach. And after
considering those things, tell me whether a snake
cannot break an egg.

“Gum Boot” : I have sometimes found
mountain trout in the gold boxes at the Union
Jack Co.’s claim, Tumbarumba (N.S.W.). These
fish had travelled through a mile of piping after
leaving the dam, thence through a jet with 170 ft.
of pressure, and into the boxes. Still they were
apparently none the worse for their rough passage.
* * * *
A week or two ago N. S. Wales read that
two Western District children had been poisoned
through eating a common European weed called
hemlock. Later they were told that some
hundreds of travelling cattle had suddenly died,
and no one could account for it. First it was said
they had died of eating thistles after rain, then a
doctor said they had died of anthrax, but when
the blood was examined in Sydney there was no
trace of anthrax bacillus. Finally, Botanist Tur-
ner, after reviewing the various theories, starts
another: —
When traversing the country drained by the Narnoi
River I have seen two exotic poisonous plants growing
there, and on some areas very plentifully, particularly
between Gunnedah and Carroll. They are the “ devil’s
fig,” or “ Mexican poppy ” ( argemone mexicana), and the
“ thorn apple ” ( datura stramonium).
The former importation is a comparatively harm-
less narcotic, though it is used in the West Indies
for hocussing; but the latter is a well-known
poison—the seeds are constantly used by the pro-
fessional poisoners of India. In fact, the whole
plant is deadly, and the leaves, if applied exter-
nally, will produce the same symptoms as if
swallowed. In view of what Botanist Turner
says, it might pay to have a careful botanical
examination made of N.S.W. public lands. Per-
haps, considering how much live-stock it would
save, even Aunty-Sosh wouldn’t mind.
“ Jaybell ” : That advice to young mis-
sionaries, “Be good to your horse,” quoted from
“The Man from Curdie’s River” (Red Page,
24/10/’O7), is just the sort of nice dictum one always
expects, and invariably receives, from kirk elders,
moderators, vestrymen, plate-pushers, and all the
rest of the motley horde of uneaten cannibal food
that hangs on to the edges of the Berlessed
Terewth for a stipend and whatever other
profits the job runs to. But the gentle shepherd
doesn’t practise as he preaches. The average
bush missionary goes propping through the
country, on his periodical rounds, bestriding a
prad that looks like a resurrection of 4s. 3d. worth
of zoo meat. Where he picks up the animal in
the first instance the Lord and the missionary only
know. To all appearance he has probably won
the steed in a raffle against death in a ’bus-stable,
knacker’s-yard, or other place of the kind, where
exhausted horses gladly pass in their checks for
the long, long spell. No peripatetic parson of
my acquaintance (and I’ve met them of all shades),
from the red-haired blusterer right down to the
blue-nosed zealot who sports short pants and white
socks, and drinks nothing stronger than diluted
cow-juice, was ever discovered behind or on top of a
moke worth more than its weight as a course in a
menagerie menu. And the chances were that,
even as filling-up material for the raging lion and
the roaring tiger, the weary bones and sinews of
parsondom’s played-out hoss wouldn’t go 2dwt. of
tit-bit to the cwt. The average bush missionary
doesn’t understand the art of glueing his person
to the saddle, and he sits behind his harnessed
locomotive with dread in his heart and fear oozing
out of his countenance. He never cavorts through
the country on a horse that a bushman might
envy ; he’d probably lose his mount the first night
he penetrated the mulga belt, and get blisters on
his parasol hunting the lost animal.

“P. Winkle” : Murray cod is a quick
grower, judging by the results shown on the -Rest-
down (Vic.) Settlement where newly-filled tanks,
dug last season, and filled from the Goulburn chan-
nel through a wire-netting inlet, have disclosed
cod up to 71b. and perch (golden) to 3|lb., when
drying up. There is a glorious opening for a fish-
farm for the breeding and distributing of our
hardy and delicate-eating native fish. Any hole
in an inland stream will yield enough small fry to
form the nucleus of the first season’s crop, and
there is a host of customers awaiting a regular
supply of good fish.

“ Judea” : The ’possum knows enough to
get sufficient tucker to keep him in fairly good
condition without much exertion, and so does
the dingo; but neither of them are in it with
the water-rat, whose hold is always loaded
down to the Plimsoll-mark with the good things
of the earth. My favorite spot
for fishing is alongside a tree
which, undermined hy the water,
has fallen inshore, leaving a small
forest of fine black roots, just a
few inches above the water, about
Bft. or 10ft. from the bank. These
small roots make excellent resting
places for myriads of small birds
to perch on while drinking. I
saw a water-rat swim from the
opposite bank to ahalf-submerged
log about 20ft. below me, where
he spelled for half a minute.
Then he let himself down into
,the water as noiselessly as a
streak of fine oil. With only the
extreme tip of his nose above
the water, and without making
the least disturbance or ripple,
he swam gently to the centre of
the bunch of roots, and apparently
rested on a submerged one. Having
got a foothold, he poked his tail
up out of the water and let it
droop over till it looked exactly
like one of the small roots
amongst which it was. There he
waited. Presently a small bird,
without the least suspicion of
the low-down trick his natural
enemy was putting up on him,
dropped down and alighted on the
tail, thinking it was one of the
hundred roots he had alighted on
so often with perfect safety. He
got one billful of water, and then
there was a convulsion, a squeak,
and an exultant rat with a bird
in his mouth was making for the
log, where he proceeded to have breakfast.

“Melliwa” : The ways of the bush J.P.
are multifarious, but the following should collect
the scone for methodical eccentricity After
harvesting most of the day, he arrived at the
court-house at 4 p.m. Went ahead after tea, and
ambled along on the track of the ca-jos all night,
and gave his last weird decision at 4.30 next
morning. I watched the J ustice and the last pair
of litigants amicably helping one another to
harness up to get back to the harvesting, and
turned into the court-house to find a tired police-
man putting out the lights preparatory to getting
back to his station, 20 miles distant. So I accom-
panied the tired cop down to the local pub to get
some information re the legal position of a sleepy
barman who serves drinks before hours.

* * *
Alleged snake-charmer James Morrissey,
who begins to get on Melbourne’s nerves, has
apparently got the Law tangled-up, lopsi e ,
and anyhow. The third time he began spilling
his snakes around, the Law threatened to put
him away for being too slightly sober too often so
now he carefully keeps on the right side of that
line. An attempt was, however, made to suppress
him on the vagrancy charge ; but Janies assured
the Court that he could catch snakes all right, anci
that there was a living in them, so he was dis-
charged in a severe tone. The trouble with
Morrissey is that he isn’t nearly as careful witn
his long streaks of death as nervous constables
think he ought to be. The police carefully slay
all they find on him when he is arrested : but, so
far, he is known to have had at least two dozen
browns, blacks, tigers, and other assorted dis-
asters, and only about a dozen can be accounted
for. The other dozen are understood to be in city
and suburban drains, private bars, churches, and
other places where a serpent would feel at home ;
wherefore a lot of people who wish to see
Morrissey’s back at a) great distance, are demand-
ing that the Law shall suppress him suddenly.
The Law leans back and breathes heavily, and
gurgles that it doesn’t know how.
“ Nameless ” : Remarkable the number of
people who have good advice to giveaway. Once
resolved to try the Simple Life, and with that
end in view took up some land in a Groper-
infested district of W.A. Was cutting down
some timber preparatory to burning off in March
(burning season). Now I knew that the recog-
nised way was to cut down all the smaller trees
and scrub, and leave the large trees to be burned
down later on; but there were only a few big
trees, and, to gratify my bump of order, I resolved
to make a clean sweep of the lot. Every Groper
that passed (and I think every W.A. native in the
district passed one day) thought it his duty to
leave the main road, and come and explain just
what sort of a fool I was. I took all this in good
part, and if I was a trifle sarcastic at times, I
could have been a lot more so, and they would
not have noticed it. But the limit was reached
when the King Groper of those parts drew up,
beckoned me across, and started explaining that
I should leave those trees. There was a girl in
the trap, sol had to repress the burning words
which rose to my lips, but, in a voice of enforced
calm, I asked him had he ever read Mill’s essay
on “Liberty.” He stared uncomprehendingly.
But the girl said: “Father, this gentleman
claims the right to clear his ground his own way.”
Apologies followed. And if I had not decided
(shortly afterwards) that the Simple Life was too
complex for me, I would have tried to see more of
that girl.
* *■ * *
“ San Remo ” : “ Douglas T.” wishes to
know why collie dogs hate the crow. It’s sheer
instinct. The dog is born and bred for the
purpose of guarding sheep, and, as the crow is the
worst enemy of the woolly one, the collie hates the
black marauder with all the hatred of a High-
lander. Again, the collie’s owner hates crows, and
between dog and man exists a close bond of
sympathy in dislikes, as in likes.

“F.H.R.”: “ X.Y.” asks (B. 31/10/’O7)
why the swaggy puts a couple of sticks across his
billy while it boils. Well, it is to keep the water
from becoming smoked. (And what is more
nauseous than smoked tea ?) A lid, the sticks or
any blessed thing placed over water when being
boiled keeps the steam down about the surface of
the water, and (to put the matter plainly if not
scientifically) while the steam is there, there
is no room for the smoke too.
THE BUSH M.C.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
You will meet him at a party, at a station shearing shed^
Or at Riley’s when they celebrate the day that they
were wed;
Or when Kelly builds a humpy, and his neighbors for a
lark
Drop in to warm the tenement of slab and stringy bark.
He wears a fancy waistcoat; he’s a howling toff, you bet,
With pumps, and green silk handkerchief that forms his
collarette.
And he does it all so pretty, and his waltzing is so neat,
And his scents —the smell of Araby was nothing half so
sweet.
But it’s when a row commences you will see the hero
shine,
He’s like a plug of dynamite a-bustin’ in a mine ;
And it’s wonderful to witness just how peaceful men can
be,
And they’ll never bustle Joey in an argument, you see.
He’ll have no coppin’ partners for a dance or two ahead,
Or waltzin’ “ soosieanners,” or schotteeshin’ them instead )
And it’s “ Snap your trippin’ fancy for the larncers or
quadrille !
Why, can’t yer get a partner, Jack? No rushin’ of it,
Bill.”
He makes’em draw for places, no race for “tops”—no
fear !
“Another couple wanted,” and “Hi, bring yer mutton
’ere ! ”
“ ’Arf right and left—swing partners ! ” I tell you, Joey’s
prime—
He whistles through his fingers when he stops ’em, every
time.
And all night long he yells it out, and tootles undismayed—
“ ’Arf right an’ left—swing partners—advance and prome-
nayde.”

“ Millewa ” : Mine host is an enthusiastic
sportsman, and bags wild duck irrespective of
season. The lake is half-a-mile away from the
house, and one evening this week he relieved the
monotony of life by going in for a paddle. He
was perfectly happy and up to his chin in water,
when he saw a brace of ducks drop into the lake
on the other side. In two ticks he was out of the
water, and ten seconds afterwards his wife and
the two lady visitors, looking for a cool spot
among the bushes, were embarrassed at the sight
of a large and very wet man trotting round on the
wet sand, apparelled magnificently in a game bag
and a double-barrelled breechloader.
How is this for an up-to-date prodigal
return ? Some 30 years, one Jim wearied of
Bendigo, home and mother, and scooted. The
exact direction of Jim’s scoot could never be
determined. Gradually his existence drifted into
a dim and distant memory, and neighborly souls
murmured his name with reverence as something
that had once been. Infact, Jim’s decease in foreign
parts was regarded as a melancholy certainty.
The other week, the “scooter,” tired of humping
his swag along the dusty tracks of his native land,
drifted back to the old home—a derelict—with a
sprig of wattle blossom in one hand (there were
no olive branches available) and a patch to mend
his pants in the other.

shapes. Years ago I grafted for a New England
cocky. He ivas a bonster ! Eight hours
a day, and he wouldn’t work
another minute longer !
The missus, too, was a
brickess. Tucker was
plentiful and Al.
She’d growl like any-
thing if we were
late for meals.
Wages the best
going. There was
no chaff-cutting
and tool-grinding
at night or on
Sundays. Satur-
dayafternoons the
boss, missus, the
kid and “the
man” (that’s me)
used to drive to
the township in
the buggy, and
have a good time.
The boss shouted
for “the man,”
and the missus
for the boss.
Worked eleven
weeks for him.
Would have
stayed there all
my life only the
gypsy blood in my
veins got the upper
hand. Cock y’s
tucker is invariably
good and plentiful.
In the olden days,
when men were
scarce, and reapers
and binders and har-
vesters unknown, far-
mers used to pay big
wages, as much as 10s.
and 12s. per day for binders
in the Goulburn Valley
(Vic.). To coax men out, they
even used to bring samples of
their cakes and puddings into the townships
for exhibition. But the men were independent
(I’m speaking of 1884-5-6), in those good
old days that died. Cocky had then, and
has now, “a set” on the real sundowning,
never-work whaler and the city larrikin pushers.
A genuine toiler is mostly well received and fed
by the cultivators of caterpillars and rust.
* * * * *
“ Jimmie Pannikin ” is stirred to anger :
“ Won-hi ” is to be congratulated on his choice of
a pen-name. Only a one-eyed critic would fail to
understand from my pars that I have spent years
in the bush, and that I write only on things I have
seen and studied. I was reared in the bush. For
three interesting and instructive years I travelled
over 60,000 square miles of West-o’-Darling, visit-
ing every tiny tinny township and smaller settle-
ment. As a boy, I spent most of my holidavs
tramping, fishing, shooting, camping out in the
bush. My Nature study began in the bush over
30 years ago. This par is written in the bush. In
brief, “Won-hi” is a liar, and discrimination is
not in him.

“ Batlow ” : Snakes like above all things
a quiet camp. Noises do not affect them, as the
bush has its bird clatter at all times, but it is
earth vibration that annoys the snake. Owing to
this fact we find the snakes diminish in numbers
as rabbits arrive. Sheep, too, disturb them.
THE RABBIT-TRAPPING
SHEPHERD.
The Bishop of Bendigo, in sarcastic reference to the poor pay of the clergy in his diocese,
applied for permission for the wayback parson to increase his stipend by taking out a
Rabbiter's License.
“ Frank Keen ” : He record shearing
(B. 7/11/’O7). Recently, at Yancannia station, in
the Broken Hill district (N.S.W.), the two
Osburgs headed a list of big tallies, shearing
1126 and 1117 respectively in a week, which at the
present shearing rate (245. a hundred) means over
£l3 per week. At Vindex station, Western
Queensland, last month one man earned £4O in
three weeks and four days, while another went up
to £44 ; and at Dagworth this year D. Mathieson
put through 245 and 237 in two successive days.
Outside individual efforts such as the above,
Vindex, of two seasons back, holds the all-Aus-
tralia belt. In that year 24 shearers averaged
through the whole of the shearing from 160 to 170
sheep per day per man.
MISUNDER-
STOOD.
VISITOR (to country undertaker): “I suppose
you have a monopoly in your business here? ”
UNDERTAKER: “No. We make ’em all by hand.”
“RayX.”: If N.Q. towns are eligible,
Maytown must win in the quest for the most
despondent assortment of houses in the Common-
wealth. Once Maytown knew 20,000 people, and
five banks flourished within its walls, and the
pubs were as leaves on Vallambrosa. Now, save
for about a hundred souls, the place is deserted.
The buildings have mostly disappeared down the
throat of the white ant. Ruins line the long,
dusty main street, whose west end stops at the
Palmer River, now (Oct.) dry. Eastward the street
stretches away until it is a mere faint pad, stray-
ing aimlessly through the bare, brown hills. A
solitary landlord dispenses drinks at a shilling a
time. Opposite his pub stand two stores, run by
Chows. All the long day the only sound which
percolates is the bleat of the wandering goat,
chasing the illusive grass blade about the quartz-
strewn surface. Truly, in its utter dejectedness,
Maytown approaches the standard of old Go-
morrah (in its post-catastrophic period), and the
ruined cities of Tyre and Carthage are cheery
pleasure-resorts beside it.
“ The Prooshan ” : Are animals thought-
readers ? Go into a y.ard of sheep with a mate.
The jurnbucks cram into a corner from you, and
pant. You indicate a certain sheep out on the
edge presented rumpwards to you, and say to
your mate, “I must catch that fellow, he's got
footrot.” Well, as soon as you move a step for-
ward. that particular sheep cuts away quite ex-
citedly, doubling, and even jumping” over his
mates to escape. How does he know you’re after
him, before you grab him, or even advance two
paces towards him ?
* » » *
“ F.H.R.” : Have you ever realised how
simple it is to get bushed in a familiar locality
under snow conditions ? A Cousin Jack miner
and I were on night shift at Stuart Town once
when the steel turned up, and we had to take it
to the blacksmith’s shop, a couple of hundred
yards away, for repairs. The imported man of 30
years’ experience could not even sharpen a pick,
so I, being not quite so useless, undertook the
job. Snow had been falling steadily during opera-
tions, and everything was white. We started for
the shaft, but, after an hour’s dreary tramp, we
were still looking for it. This was cheerful.
Fifty pounds’ worth of hole had gone and aban-
doned itself ! We found several trenches by fall-
ing into them, and bumped against stumps and
logs that came forward and met us eagerly.
Meanwhile the steel was biting into our hands,
which were cold as a stepmother’s kiss, and an
extra large flake of snow extinguished our bottle-
lantern. Then we tried to walk through a wire
fence, but altered our minds, and followed it care-
fully back to the blacksmith’s shop. After taking
our bearings, we started afresh, and suddenly
caught sight of the windlass against the skyline,
like the Horseman that Henry Lawson writes
about. Then we sneaked into that shaft before it
had a chance to escape. If it roamed about
hill to keep itself warm any more that night it
took two half-frozen miners with it.
* * m m
“ Flinderino ” : The bush policeman often
finds life dull and monotonous, and he adopts
various methods of making his existence less
miserable. Know one representative of the law,
planted in a microscopic township behind the
mountains, who found himself becoming rusted
and moth-eaten through idleness. His only pos-
sible customer was a Chinaman. Having nothing
better to do, he made a point of running in the
Chow on every shadow of an opportunity, but as
he required only a small quantity of firewood he
was unable to provide his prisoner with constant
wood-chopping exercise. However, he was deter-
mined that the Celestial’s time should not be
wholly wasted, and he thought out an idea where-
by he could give the Chinlde some exercise, and
at the same time occupy himself. Every morning
after breakfast John was summoned from his cell
to do an hour’s military training. With an old
broom-handle for a gun the Mongolian was made
to “Fall in!” “Shoulder Whoop!” “Quick
march !” “Right-about wheel !” and numerous
other manoeuvres. The constable always spoke
of the cell as “ The Barracks.” Every fortnight
or so he would arrest the Chow on a charge of
Sunday trading, or vagrancy, or sweating, or any-
thing he could think of, and put him into bar-
racks for a week’s drill. Before long Johnnie
Atkins made a perfect soldier, and could halt,
charge, form a hollow square, form fours, and fall
over himself as neatly as any Scottish regiment.
A RIME OF BOSKY MOMENTS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When you wander in the boskage of the bar,
By the tree-ferns and the talking parrot, far
In the lounge’s dim recesses where electric breezes blow ;
When you think it’s time to take your hat and go,
Lest your vagrant fancy land you in disaster with the
maid,
Whom you’ve promised, quite sincerely, that your love
, will never fade,
You may think—“ Why ’m gettin’ boshky —rarsher
hoshky ! ’’—and you are !
When you’ve told her how the tender passion’s fanned
To a blaze inside you : when you’ve grabbed her hand—
Having spent your six ’n’ sixpence on your thirteen drops
o’ bliss—
And you’ve got her bailed for “ jus’ one likkle kiss ” ;
When you’re sure the fair Melinda is a lovely waking
dream,
And you’ll climb across and kiss her if she’ll promise not
to scream,
It may dawn on you “ I’m boshky ! ” And you are —to
beat the band !
When you ask her, in her own, old loving way
To caress you—when you babble bosh, and pray
For a lock of shining top-knot, where the dye is bright
and new—
When you dimly hear her rudely hissing “ Shoo ! ”
When you gurgle in your sleevers over jokes of yester-
year,
And you giggle when you ask yourself to “ ’Ave anuzzer
beer ! ”
P’r’aps you’ll think “ I’m —I’m bo —BOSHKY ! ” And
you’ll find it truth—next day !
vie.
FRANK HENTY.

King Coal indulges In a siesta.
Smoky chimneys cured.
The ancient art of walking will be
rediscovered by the suburbanite.
Priceless adornments, only within the
reach of the rich and powerful.
Owing to a scarcity of fuel, the
ancient custom of burning down a
house to roast a pig will be revived.

owner of a motor-car or boat will have more friends
than he can shake a stick at.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
Mr. Allan McLean, of Christchurch, Maorl-
land, who died recently, left ap estate valued at
half a million sterling, one-third of which is to be
devoted to making provision for females who are
in need.
Died suddenly, at Sydney, last week, aged
55, Edward Hurley, secretary of N.S.W. Typo-
graphical Association. He learned his trade in
S. M. Herald office. He was Government Print-
ing Office’s rep. on the Typo. Board, and suc-
ceeded good old Charles Jones in the secretary-
ship of the Association.
S. Australian Chief Justice Samuel Way :
I am past the age of bearing arms ; I cannot, perhaps,
serve as a grenadier ; but, although I cannot do that, I
should be glad to offer my body, over which the foreign
invader should first have to pass before an inch of the
Northern Territory should become anything else than
British land.
Those who speculate on Premier Wade’s
chances of succeeding Darley in the Chief-Justice-
ship, should recall that in the nearly ninety years
of the Court’s existence only one politician—
Martin —has passed from the arena of active
politics to the chief seat in the N.S.W. Supreme
Court. Salomons and Darley served in the Legis-
lative Council, but that body is not regarded in
the sphere of militant politics.
The Queenslander has scant honor in his
own State. All but one of the Under Secretaries
of the Government departments, the Government
Analyst, Biologist. Botanist, Entomologist, Fruit
Expert, Health Officers, and the managers of the
nine banks doing business in Queen street, Bris-
bane, were born outside the Northern State.
The Bananalander is also in a minority in the
Ministry ; he’s neither Premier nor leader of the
Opposition (they’re both Scotch), nor Speaker
(he’s Irish), nor Chairman of Committees in the
Assembly (he’s English).
QUITE THE REVERSE.—(With apologies to
Pears’ Soap.)
That, while this conference approves of the principle of
a white Australia from a racial point of view, it considers
the importation of colored labor is necessary for develop-
ment of the tropical territory. —Motion carried at the
Women’s Leagues’ Commonwealth Conference.

and dean; we must have a daub of dirt
somewhere! ”
Deceased Frank Barnes was a type of the
hard-working, unobtrusive, non-advertising class
of journalist, with an innate love of variety-stage
life. He wrote almost the whole of Brisbane
Boomerang at one time, and lifted Sydney
Sunday Times and Sydney Truth along the road
of popularity in their struggling days. His latest
press work was on the Worker.

thing?” That is what Victoria wants to know
rnore than anything else just now. Canon Nash,
the Other day, vacated his pulpit in a fashionable
Geelong church, and when a crowd
of earliest Christian admirers, who
