canon with more transgres-
sions than any ordinary
arsenal of canons could get
through in a lifetime by
strict attention to pleasure.
Artist Frederick de
Kock, who died at Kensing-
ton (Vic.), last week, aged
64, was a nephew of French
novelist Paul de Kock, who
writes stories of silly pruri-
ency for young people who
don’t and old people who ought
to know better. He was born
in Sumatra, where his father
was Resident - General, and
came to Victoria 27 years ago,
15 of which he painted out in
Bendigo. His strong point
was illuminating, and he did a
lot of that sort of work for Melbourne Univer-
sity and other public institutions.
A hustling democrat passed out at Adelaide,
recently, aged 70. George Henry Buttery was
born in London, where he
learned cabinet-making. In
1860 he was a delegate to
the International Working
Man’s Association, and was
associated with Karl Marx
and Fred Engels, who suc-
ceeded Marx in the practi-
cal leadership of the social
democratic movement. In
1877, Buttery came to IS.A.,
where he worked at his trade
and became representative
of the United Furniture
Trades Society on the Trades
and Labor Council. He was
president of the Council in
1891, in time to live some
strenuous days during the
big maritime strike. He
helped to form the S.A.
United Labor party, and was
placed first in the initial
plebiscite for selection of
Parliamentary Labor candi-
dates, but declined the chance
of becoming a legislato..
Governor Rawson’s al-
leged “shudder” when a
case goes to hangman B ussell
for final treatment isn’t of
much public importance, but
indicates, if true, that he
doesn’t regard executions as
mere official routine. One
N.S. W. Governor had a jest
for such occasions. Governor
Phillip used to signal the
drop with his red pocket
handkerchief, while walking
about on his verandah. Go-
vernor Foveaux presided with
ardent satisfaction at the
executions in his time. The
condemned were allowed to
make speeches then, and
Foveaux used to break in in
contradiction, and if the
wretch didn’t promptly apol-
ogise, he ordered the lever to
be pulled. Once, at Norfolk
Island, the hangman blun
dered, and Foveaux rushed
to his assistance. With his
own hand he launched a pair
of men to the dull thud, and
bragged of his “clean job”
for long after.
Died, last week, Andrew T. Kerr, for 25
years member of N.fci.W. Nominee Chamber, and
a large squatter m the Orange district, which dis-
trict he represented 30 years ago in the Assembly.
The death notice in the papers was the first inti-
mation the majority of the public had that there
was an M.L.C. of the name.
The original Thomas Arndell, whose 83-
years-old descendant died last week at Windsor
(N.S.W.), had a long and pitiful squabble with
the British Exchequer over a small pension claim.
He had given up his Government billet of surgeon
to the Sydney garrison and settled down as
farmer at Windsor, relying on his Imperial pen-
sion to help things out. But one year the Home
authorities dropped his name off the pension list
without assigning any reason (it was probably on
account of his Governor Bligh sentiments). It
took six years battling with the Exchequer before
the debt due was paid, and in the meantime the old
ex-surgeon had gone through most of the miseries
of the newchum farmer.
Six men took part in the Sydney negotia-
tions that ended last week in Premier Wade draw .
ing up a basis for another meeting between the
Newcastle strikers and the mine-owners. Wade
was backed up by Ashton, who .humps a good
load for an honorary Minister (he has an office in
the Attorney-General’s Department) ; Living-
stone Learmonth was the mouthpiece of the
owners, and city-solicitor “Ted” Simpson was
with him as headpiece; while Peter Bowling
talked for the miners, and had Amram Lewis as his
second. Lewis holds what funds the coalminers
have saved up in their stocking. Learmonth is
grand paniandrum at Newcastle for the A. A. Co.,
and lives out on a fragment of that tremendous
absentee’s coal estate beyond Mayfield, near New-
castle. This is the first big trouble he has had to
handle since he succeeded to old Jesse Gregson’s
job.
Ex-Senafcor Higgs definitely starts business
in Sydney as an auctioneer and estate agent. His
first commission is from the Queensland Govern-
ment to act as agent for the sale of Crown lands.
“ RUBBISHOH!”
KAISER WILLIAM (cleaning up his Court yard):
“ Pooh! The divinity that doth hedge a king
smells devilishly like the other thing at times.”
The N.S.W. Arbitration Court is liable to
have its findings presented in tripping verse now.
Thomas E. Spencer has been chosen employers’
representative, vice J. P. Wright, sick ; and T.
E. Spencer is the man who told “How
McDougall topped the score.”
Died in Paris, last month, Fred Keller-
mann, husband of Madame Charbonnet-Keller-
mann, andfatherof AnnetteKellermann, champion
lady swimmer. He was at one time in N.S.W.
Public Service, but when his wife was leader of
Sydney’s musical profession, he devoted his time
to managing for her. The old Quintette Society
was due to his energy. A few years ago the for-
tunes of the family were in low water, but all
hands swam into affluence again on Annette’s
prowess.
Late Andrew Taylor Kerr’s father was super
to an estate belonging to the energetic blacksmith
parson, Samuel Marsden, and in time became
himself a property-owner. It was on his ground
that the famous big nugget was found by a black-
fellow. The nugget with two smaller pieces found
alongside gave about 19300 z. gold, and was claimed
by the Government. The demand led to much
wrangling, but was ultimately settled in
favor of Kerr. Kerr gave the aborigine 200
sheep as a quittance in full, and the gold went
to establish the Kerrs as bush potentates.
A. I. Clark, who died suddenly at Hobart,
last week, would possibly have been on the High
Court Bench had not his health of late been un-
certain. He was one of the great constitutional
lawyers of Australia, and had a dearer title to
esteem in his robust faith in Australia. It is not
now injudicious to state that The Bulletin was
often indebted to Mr. Clark for an opinion on
constitutional law points. The deceased Judge
exercised throughout his life a strong influence on
the development of Australian Nationalism.
Late Justice A. Inglis Clark possessed a
very small head, and his hatter often used this
fact to explode the fallacious argument that a
man with a large head necessarily had the most
brains. He said that the deceased Judge had the
smallest head he ever made “lids” for. Of
Clark’s sons, one is studying architecture in Phila-
delphia, in U.S.A., and another is a graduate
in Law of the University of Tasmania. The
Judge himself was a very reserved man, seldom
appearing in public. He was a good chess
player ; and it was at his house that Premier
Deakin always stayed when he visited Tasmania.
N.S.W. Premier Wade, who has taken in
hand the two parties to the Newcastle miners’
strike, knows more than most lawyers do about
mining. He was born among the coal at Single-
ton, and his father was a civil engineer who talked
coal and the safe way to win it. ITor many years
Premier Wade has had briefs in most of the
N.S.W. cases that have come from underground ;
and when, after the disastrous cave-in at the
Broken Hill South mine, a Commission was
appointed to inquire how it happened, Wade
carried through the largely-technical job single-
handed ; and he has done the same with several
inquiries regarding the Coal Mines Kegulation
Act.
James Curley, who has had a front seat in
every Newcastle labor trouble for a generation, is
standing out of this one—for the time being, any-
way. Before the pay question began to look like
bubbling over, a Commission was appointed to
find out whether the electrically-driven coal-cut-
ting machines were liable to turn themselves into
bombs at Hebburn colliery, as the miners alleged.
It was a big question, and practically involves the
restriction of the use of machines; and Curley,
as the man who knows better how to hold
the miners’ end up on a job of this _ sort
than probably anybody else, was asked to sit on
the Commission—along with Judge Murray and a
mine-manager. So, as Curley was doing semi-
judicial work, it was suggested that he ought to
stand out of the strike business ; and Brennan, a
younger man, and a membei the miners
general committee of managem vas drafted
into his office. Now the Sea I aen, among
others, have nominated Brennan for the office as a
fixture.
Louis Brennan who, it is reported, is to
receive £60,000 to carry on his monoline railway
experiments, is the youngest of three brothers,
natives of Castlebar, Ireland. Michael, the
eldest, showed a taste for art, and was taken up by
a few appreciative wealthy people, who sent him
to Italy to study. He died there just as he began
to do well. The second brother, Patrick, emigrated
to Victoria. He became a schoolmaster there,
and invited Louis out to share his good luck.
Louis had plenty of trials, but can afford now to
laugh at the memory of them. He talks affection-
ately of Victoria and of the men who stood to him
iii dark times.
A thoroughly Australian Christmas gift
for a friend subscription to The Lone
Hand for 12 months. It will keep your friend
Current with Australian life.
£< Melb.” : I have not heard a more valu-
able lecture than that by W. H. Edgar, M.L.C.,
on his trip of over 500 miles along the Murray
from Echuca to Mildura and Wentworth -with
the Parliamentarians. The National Government
ought to employ a lecturer to go all over Aus-
tralia with a discourse on the Murray from source
to sea. The party left Echuca in two river
steamers. “ The Murray is a revelation,” says
Edgar. He carried our thoughts to the river as
an emblem of national and individual life.
(Remember how superbly Frank Myers wrote of
Kosciusko.) His vision swept over the sources of
the Murray. Like the young Rhine, it has a
wild and rocky course, with picturesque scenery,
beauteous cataracts, and so on. Here you see
fanciful youth, as of the Australian nation, or
of any man. Towards Albury the Hume, Indi,
or Murray settles down to its purpose in life.
It is only a Yarra as yet, but what reinforcements
are to come ! The Murrumbidgee, Darling, Lach-
lan, Campaspe, Goulburn, Loddon, Ovens, and
other streams pour in. You think of the affluents
which have come into this nation, with the
development of its wool, gold, etc. Oh, the
Murray is our sermon ! Let us worship
it with a devotion equal to that of the Egyptians
for their Nile, the Hindus for their Ganges.
I relish lecturer Edgar because he sticks up for the
Murray. Not one word of detraction. As the
man said of his mistress : “ I love her even for her
dear, delightful faults.”
THE LATE MR. JUSTICE A. I. CLARK.
(A good Judge and a good Australian.)
Photo, by Kerry & Co., Sydney.
In Australia Cousin Jacks show a morbid
partiality to corpse. In towns where Tre, Pol
and Pen abound the Dead March in “Saul’ is
preferred to dance music. Sunday is the
fashionable day for the burial ceremony, the
“expired ” being always detained (w.p.) until the
first day of the week. Should the “departed”
decline to remain, the mines declare a half-
holiday, and the shops put up their shutters, for
no one is allowed to pass through the Pearly
Grates without a good send-off. What a daughter’s
marriage is to a Hindu, that is a family funeral to
Cousin John.

Ihe J. C. Williamsons left London for New
y ork and Chicago on October 18, and whether
they return to Australia direct from America, or
go back to London and sprint from there, depends
mainly on the quality of the American winter.
Mr. Robertson Clarke, who has been for some
years on the staff of State Governor Rawson,
leaves for .England on Saturday.

Lady Godiva rode at Wonderland, according
to promise, on Wednesday night; but the rain,
which falls impartially on the dressed and un-
dressed (if the latter chances to be out in it)
started a few minutes before the undressed
act. It was wonderful how small a quantity of
the gentle ram from Heaven it took to scatter the
large quantity of amorous youth and foolish virgin
that stood at gaze waiting for the lady in white
clinging tights and flowing hair. A few months
ago the lady got married. A wedded Yenus, like
a dressed one, seems an anomaly.
If one of the beery brigands who are understood
to write most of the soft and soulful penny novel-
ettes for young ladies had the naming of the
leaders of the strike ructions at Newcastle, he
couldn t have done better than Livingstone Lear-
month and Peter Bowling. Livingstone Lear-
month—who is the coal-owners’ mouthpiece, of
course—-would in the penny novelette marry
Lady Geraldine, and do Noble Deeds; while
f™ er r ) B0 '!iJ n F’ coal-miner, might do almost
any dreadful thing. Peter, in real life is
however a very domesticated apostle. A reporter
° f mme, from Sydney, went to the New-
castle Trades Hall with another reporter friend
who comes from the outer darkness of Melbourne
and tried to find the man whom the Sydney
papers call ‘ the boss of the miners.” “Let’s
see, said the Melbourne barbarian, “what’s Mr
Bowlings front name?” The lesser miners’
othciai who was giving out information turned
pale under his coating of coal-dust and soot.
Wh-a-a-t! he gasped, after a while. “Doesn’t
everybody know his name is Peter? ” But he had
his revenge. As Mr. Bowling wasn’t at the
Trades Hafl, he would probably be at home.
And where was his home? “Go along about a
mile, and anybody will tell you where Peter
Bowling lives. They found where he lived all
right, but lie wasn’t at home—he had just run out
to a neighbor s. And as the reporters set forth to
track him down, they heard something like a
moan from within—“ And he hasn’t got a collar

Lucky owner Clelland, of the victorious Apo
logue animal, used to be a bootmaker in Auck-
land. From bootmaking he rose—or fell—to

Talking of Maorilanders: a prismatic bunch
passed through Sydney a week or so ago en
route for Ward’s own country. It belonged to
Mn Dennis O’Rourke (son of ex-Speaker Maurice
ORourke) and consisted of his wife and five
children. The bunch has been in Europe for
some years Pa wore the latest King Edward
clothes, and had his trousers turned up (our drought
is only of four months’ duration) and his three
schoolboy sons wore equally amazingly faultless
clothes, smaller copies of Pa’s, and had their
pants turned up. O’Rourke married a hugely
rich Christchurch heiress. The inhabitant! of
the flat-place call it Ch. Ch., as if it were two
snorts from a motor car. He is or was the best
amateur jockey in Maoriland.
Another returned Maorilander—he is not a
returned empty either, for he’s been telling the
Herald splendid guff about Siberia—is Mr
G. W. Patterson. He calls himself Major Pat-
terson. Suppose he does major at something, but
he s mostly a reputable gum merchant with a
flourishing gum store. Author and poet J L
Kelly, who has been doing work abroad for "the
Dominion, has also been in Sydney, and the
Mills, returned by the beautiful new Union liner
Marama, are getting all that’s coming to them in
Dunedin.
Cathedral, London, on Oct. 9
Mr. W. L. Robertson (of George Thompson and
Go. London) was married to Miss Mavis Jean I
Anderson, second daughter of Mr. Charles Ander-
son (C. Anderson and Co. Ltd.), of Durham Hill
Albion-street, Sydney. Bride was given away bv
her brother; a reception at the Hotel Cecil and a
honeymoon abroad followed. The happy brace
will visit Australia in the bright bv-and-bye. It’s
up to every Australian girl who marries abroad to
bring her trophy to her own land—on at least a
visit.
Madge Corcoran, a Melbourne girl who has
been playing in drama in the U.S.A. for some
years, has lost her voice through a railway collision
She was travelling to Washington to open in a new
piece, and at Baltimore her train ran into another
that was being side-tracked. Passengers, fearing
that the boilers of the engine would explode ran
out into a heavy rain storm. When, some hours
later, Miss Corcoran reached her destination she
was speechless, and, continuing in that state she
had to return to New York.
From James Edmond, at Lake Manapouri, in
Maoriland:—
Left Dunedin on the 6th, by the alleged express,
for Gore, and thence I proceeded by a series of
short jumps in the wallaby-train that leaps glee-
fully along the little 3ft. 6in. railway to Lumsden.
Lumsden sleeps amid the grass on the Waimea Plains,
and Maoriland steps softly so as not to waken it. On the
7th, a red-painted buggy and a large horse and a small
one took me 53 miles across a wild country and dropped
me here.
There is a big lake in front of the window. There are
32 islands in the big deep lake. There are snow-topped
mountains all round the big deep, damp blue lake. The
sun set behind the snow-toppedest of them last night,
and the snow was red—bright red, like the color of the
language of the man who backed the second horse. This
morning the sun rose behind the second snow-toppedest
range, and the second snow-toppedest range threw
a shadow like a priest’s clothes across the lake on to the
snow-toppedest range,-so that it was dazzling white on
the roof of things and black below. Then the white roof
of things became pink, and after that it became reddish,
like the painted blush on the face of the frisker of Baby-
lon, and after that again it became various ; and just
while it was various, and I was wondering hard how
various it might become, someone rang the breakfast-bell
and said something about ham and eggs.
The lake is blue. There is more water flowing into it
than into any other Maoriland lake. There ig more water
flowing out of it than out of any other Maoriland lake.
The water is in a hurry. It is full of fish. It is nearly
1500 feet deep in places. It foams like the sea when the
south wind (or else the north or west or east wind) goes
across it like a ghost dragging a barrow. And all
round this lake the mountains stand up perpendicularly
like a thousand pyramids with pallid washing on top.
There are trees wherever things aren’t so vertical that
the trees can’t hang, and in many places where things are
so absolutely vertical that nothing could ever hang—not
even the dead body of Judas Iscariot—the trees hang just
the same. They do it out of cussedness, and cussedness
is greater than gravitation. To-day the Government
steam-launch took me out in solitary state, and
showed me all this magnificence for hours and hours—
showed me the kingdoms of the earth and the glory
thereof, even as the Devil did to the Redeemer in the
Scripture—all for 12s. 6d. Amid the immortal silences we
ate corned beef sandwiches, and where the works of God
were thickest we boiled the billy.
There are sandflies at the head of the lake. They ate
two artists yesterday, likewise their tent, baggage, boat,
clothes, and cooking utensils. These artists had gone up
the lake to camp out for six weeks, and our captain
put in at the bay, where he thought they
should be, to ask how they felt. They weren’t
there, but a dead camp-fire was present. I'know the
sandflies ate them. I saw one sandfly wearing a hat that
didn’t belong to him, and another had an unfinished oil-
painting under his arm.
I am going to fetch this lake away under my arm when
I leave. It is the sort of thing which makes stealing
justifiable.
The Villeneuve-Smiths are on their way back to
Sydney; so are Mrs. Jack Want and her daughter.
The Colin Stephens go hence for a European trip
early next year.
Messrs. Southwell and Lenimone are in Mel-
bourne riding the breakers, so to speak, of the tide
of Melba’s concert booking. Seats for the first
concert and more than half those forthesecond were
sold out before three o’clock on the day the plan
was opened. Melba sings here on the 10th and
14th of December.
The “Freddie” Shipmans, perhaps the best-
liked brace of Americans that ever walked up
to the Sydney goods counter, are leaving by
Saturday’s liner en route for India. Mister has
run several showand lyric ventures on thesesmiling
shores, and pretty little Missus’s clothes were as
smart as her talk If Providence only knew His
business He would have left us the Shipmans and
taken (Find the missing word.)
Mosman Musical Society, under the baton of
■M-D A. H. Norman, gave a capital performance
of “Judas Maccabseus ” last week. Miss Rivers,
Mr. Crabtree, and Mr. Beattie were the principals!
and the Mosmanites filled the hall to overflowing!
Miss Ora Bensusan, a sister of Miss Inez Ben-
susan, who played awhile here with Nance O’Neil
and the “What Happened to Jones” Co., married
an American citizen last month in Milan.
Storks, and waterlilies, and their foliag*
are the make-up of the ornamentation of the
memorial fountain to the late Robert Brough,
unveiled yesterday in the grounds of the Sydney
Hospital. It is a gracefully-built water caster
and water carrier, and casteth its spray like the
quality of mercy. “In Memory of Robert
Brough ” is the simple and sufficient inscription it
carries. Premier Wade and Mr. Ramaciotti
made speeches—the eulogy and threnody dex-
terously combined—and Mrs. Brough, her sisters,
and quite a number of theatrical managers were
auditors.
Lady Doughty (late colleague “Tryphena,”
of Melbourne) seems to have started the crowded
hour of glorious life that the poet avers to be worth
an age without a name. Her husband, as a recog-
nised force in English politics and a great fighting
speaker on the Conservative side, has to do a con-
siderable quantity of travelling and speaking all
over England. His party always makes arrange-
ments for the lady to week-end with the wife
of another member of the party. Sir Charles
Cayzer. also a giant in the ship-owning line, who
best-manned our friend’s knight at his wedding,
has been entertaining them at his place in Scot-
land. Of the English women the Australienne
has met, the Countess of Yarborough, whose cas-
tellated humpy is in the Doughty vicinity, seems
to have impressed her most in the matter of men-
tality, manners, and inches and sport. As the
Yarborough earl is sick, his wife is determined to
bring him out here for a trip as soon as he can
stand the voyage. A wise proceeding. We are
always glad to welcome clever, sporty people
whether they have coronets on their blueys or
not.
Two Sydney girls, each a beauty in her way,
have been widowed within the month, and both
aren’t beyond the third year of their twenties—
Mrs. George Gaden, who is one of Mr. Sam
Hordern’s daughters, and Mrs. Nisbet, a daughter
of Mr. Baxter Bruce, the solicitor. Mrs. Gaden
left by the China to join her mother in London.
Lady Northcote bought a number of Miss
Rout’s artistically gotten-up booklets at the
Women’s Work Exhibition. Miss Rout is a
Maorilander, from Christchurch, with a lot of
imagination, a good literary taste in selection,
and a fine color sense, and all these values she has
worked into her exhibit. The paper of the book-
lets is hand-made, in shades of old rose, subdued
greens, daffodil—indeed, every fair tone. The
literary extracts—prose and poetry—Miss Rout
herself has typed in colored inks, and she has
bound the achievements sometimes in silk, oftener
in the jewel and flower-hued rough paper.
Excellencette Rawson’s At Home last Thursday
to the committee and principal workers for the
Exhibition about marks the finality of the enter-
prise. It will close at the end of the present
month. Miss Rawson presented Mr. Somer, the
hon. general manager of the Exhibition, with a
piece of plate. The illuminated address that kept
it company had been wrought with dainty skill
by Miss Wilson.
Mr. Claude McKay, of Sydney D.T., marries,
early in the new year, that pretty little artiste,
Miss Dorothy Sidney, of the Julius Knight Co.
She is a dark, brilliant-eyed young party, and
much-travelled for her age. Her father is a well-
known London stage manager, who, amongst his
other achievements, staged Coquelin’s repertoire
when the famous Froggy actor played in England.
Dr. Cohen, at one time a pet physician at St.
Vincent’s Hospital, has returned from foreign
parts, and is being congratulated on his engage-
ment to Nurse Chilcott, a Sydney girl.
Mr. and Mrs. Stanley Mylius left for England
by the China after a two years’ visit to this sunny
land. Mrs. Mylius is a sister of Mrs. Ambrose
Pratt and Mrs. Harry Austin.
From my Brisbane correspondent:—
The long-looked-for Chelmsford party came off last
Wednesday week. A chill affair it was, distinguished mainly
by the absence of two semi-notables, who, with a rare
naivete, hastened to advertise the fact that they hadn’t
received invitations, in next morning’s Courier. About
400 elderly people turned up, and wandered dazedly
about the brilliantly-illuminated grounds, regret-
ting that they had left their smoked glasses
at home. The Chelmsford dame, in a white satin
robe that gave her the appearance of an icicle trying
to be amiable, received and welcomed the guests, who
then made with haste for more or less shady corners in
which to recount to each other the stories of their lives.
There was a stand-up supper, and just as the atmosphere
promised to brighten somewhat, “Gor’ Save” blared
significantly, whereat the cloakrooms promptly took on
the semblance of so many free-lunch counters.
The official dinner, a night or two earlier in the week,
was a far cheerier gathering.
The Governor and his wife, with their eldest daughter
and Captain Verney in tow, witnessed “The Three
Graces,” and various ballets, by the pupils of Mrs. Mason
Beatty, at His Majesty’s, on Friday evening. There was
a full house. Mr. and Mrs. Geo. Eddington brought their
chick, and Mrs. Herbert Perry, just back from the
Killarney Springs, arrived with her two sons. A perfect
bed of Flowers sprouted in one part of the auditorium,
also a couple of Frosts and the usual Hopkinses, Smiths,
Joneses; likewise the major portion of the Marks and the
Corrie clans. .
The Queensland contingent is leaving England in great
numbers. Mrs. Hanbury has -flitted Continentwards.
And Dr. and Mrs. Robertson arrive amongst their native
bananas once more by the next mail-boat.
From “ Rosna,” Adelaide :
We were all Butt last week, and are still gasping, not
only at the Voice, but the wondrous complexion, eyes,
frocks, grace, and husband that accompany the Voice.
One day they went along to applaud their compatriots at
cricket, and the compatriots returned the compliment by
going in a body to the first concert. That was not as
thronged as the following two, but then it was a public
holiday, when much of the peerage stays in its moated
grange for fear of being jostled by the vulgar throng.
Government House was represented by Miss Clowes and
Lieutenant Boddam-Whettom ; also, there were present
the Chief Justice Ways, the Conservatorium Bevans
Josiah Symon and a daughter, the Lancelot Stirlings’
Miss Hack, Mrs. Robert Kyffin Thomas and some
daughters, and the usual throng of musical folk. The
Ways, Bevans, and many more of the same crowd flocked
to the second concert, which was packed to the doors.
At the Lyric Club evening, arranged bv her chum and
ex-fellow student, Mrs. Fox, Mrs. Clara Butt wore a
chiffony gold and white frock, narrow gold velvet twined
in her black locks, and great gold hoops in her ears. She
trailed in, booming a melodious word or two to President
Bass 6, and attended by secretary Ibbs, one of the Taits
and Mr. Kennerley Rumford. The contralto’s jolly smile
becomes nothing less than a beam when she looks at her
nice, tall, brown husband. The two of them sat together
and applauded the performance generally, and at the end
were carried off to supper in the club’s pretty drawing-
room. 6
Julius Knight caught a visit from the influenza
nond in Adelaide, but in spite of a throat that cjrew
huskier and huskier, struggled on to the end of the
season.
That clever kiddy, little Helen Andrews, had a concert
at Woodville, the other evening, and drew’ a thronged
and enthusiastic house. The infant, who is not more than
eight at the outside, has been studying dancing and
dramatic art in Sydney, and is already a wonderful little
actress. She is a pretty, unspoiled, natural infant, too,
with heaps of originality to help her.
Yours Affectionately,
AKENEHI.
A thoroughly Australian Christmas gift
for a friend abroad—a subscription to The Lone
Hand for 12 months. It will keep your friend
current with Australian life.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne November 18, 1907.
-Vly Dear Yarrie, —
. Nowadays the State Parliament doesn’t look
the federal side of the road, on high days. The
gentlemen who represent suburbs show hoity-
toity airs to the rulers of the nation, and when “a
social gathering” (I hate Suburbia’s way of speaking
“ gatherings”; as if a tea-party were something
ot the nature of a whitlow !) comes, Federal mem-
bers are never invited to enliven it. The reason
ot this unbrotherly coolness is the wicked nephew
ot Aunty-Sosh—otherwise the Labor party. When
invitations to come along and fight for a cup of
coot tea and a cheese sandwich were sent by the
State to the Nation, the unattached, lonely, un-
sociable Socialist occasionally handed his card
over to his industrious landladv, or an unassuming
relation. When the proxy joined in the food-
tray and hand-touching contest, she was recog-
nised by Mrs. Suburbia as an old associate—a
relic of dead days, when Mrs. S. used to do her
own washing, and sometimes borrowed a cup of
sugar or a knob of blue over the back-fence of her
next-door neighbor. Mrs. Suburbia complained
eCfclVe lord thilt “ most undesirable
P.P W( ? re gaming access to the sacred
testivities-in fact, an artisan’s wife had rubbed
shoulders with her. (Oh ! dreadful-it was bad
enough to come m contact with such people before
Mr. Suburbia gave up day-labor in favor of small
contracts and the local council—but now!) That
settled it—the Federal member will never more re-
ceive at the hands of the State member an
opportunity to prove his democratic belief that
ilrs. Smith is fit, on the grounds of common
womanhood, to encounter Volunteer Colonel De
Smythe s lady over a tea-table provided by the
Government. Aie! but it’s a sore trial to
memor -y of its single-fronted,
eather-board past in the small streets which are
popularly supposed to supply Labor members to
the country.

The Kelso King-Kirk wedding, on Wednesday,
at Christ Church, South Yarra, was quiet as
tar as guests were concerned, but gorgeous
m the swagger blossom of frocking. Miss Kirk is
a cousin (or something) to Kirk’s Bazaar, with
a horsey vet. brother. The family is very golden,
as the Mt. Morgan Westey Halls belong to the
clan. The man—the Kelso-King bridegroom—is
or was, your wealthy Harbor-dwelling widower’
and is himself a director of Mt. Morgan and
chairman or something of Mort’s Dock,’ and
dozens of other things with money in them
ihe true-lovers’ bow was knotted by the
Kev. Chnst-Church Canon Tucker. The bride
had a wonderful piece of old lace lent to her
tor the veiling ceremony, and her other drapings
were of the same rank in grandeur—but
oppositely new. Her gown was of chiffon with
the new satin stripe.

being discourteous, and who have the grace to be
merciful when pretending to be only just.

The Anderson demoiselles are the one object-
lesson Sydney has offered us during race week.
Legend says that they make their own frocks •
cats say they import them. Whichever is true
matters little. They have each looked like the
realised dreams of a dressmaking poet, in gar-
ments picturesque and becoming, which were
a tacit reproach to all women who are merely
dressed by an extravagant unoriginal show-
room oracle. Lucky is the woman who has the
inestimable gift of grasping other people’s most
flattering conception of her, and dressing to match
The papers have been printing letters on “ex-
travagance of dress” since Cup week. So far no
one seems to have “got on” to the municipal
lady Who at Caulfield wore a 27 guineas hat
iwas an overcrowded affair (albeit the accom-
panying dress was a dream of simplicity
and loveliness); but the concoction had
been reposing m a fashionable top-notch-
dresser s show-case for an envious and much-
discussed week, and every fashionable eye in Mel-
bourne had noticed it and its price'. Must have
been an ecstasy of joy to feel that everyone knew
how horribly expensive one’s hat was.

Recent weeks have been so studded and
knobbed, and packed and surrounded with Garden
Parties, that I clean forgot to mention one of the
best of the species exhibited. ’Twas the day before
Steeplechase, and Prime Minister Deakin and
his Lady hosted on the neat and close-shaven lawns
of Parliament House Gardens. The day chose to be
one of the goodly blue ones that never meet wind
and the dresses were like the flowers that bloom
in the spring and riot in the summer. Mrs
Deakin gowned in grey-soft grey crepe that
cmled and twisted and glided after her in the
vivid green grass. Mrs. Deakin is one of those
regal people whose walk is a superb progression
Vuite: a sedate air was given to the usual frivol of
a garden spree by the guests filtering through the
pondrous tombrous gloom of the Houses Library.
In the Library, by-the-bye, much interest was
taken in an out-spread set of silver: heavy trav
and massive coffee wherewithals, engraved with a
regretful farewell message to leader Watson
M.P., trom his party colleagues.

Miss Florence Maher, daughter of the Hunter
Kiver Mahers, was quietly married in Elstern-
wmk last week to her John Sutcliffe Rigby, son
of the Westralian Orient-shipping Rigby. The
bridegroom is an officer on an'Orient boat, and
forbidden to take his bride along with him ; so the
little newly-wed followed post-haste by the next
boat, and, as she will do a sprint across country
from Marseilles, will arrive in London on the
same day as her adoring hubby. Liverpool, Eng-
land, is to be the home of the couple.

A strange lady in this land is doing fine
deeds She s Miss Olga Clauscen, who only
arrived from Copenhagen a couple of years agone.
Now, she runs a Scandinavian paper, which is a
record of Norse doings, here and in Europe. It
deals with the arts, births, deaths, marriages
and general views of the people who have Vikings
and Skalds among their forbears, and whose an-
cestors went baresark and were solid on the
subjects of Thor and Odin and Valhalla : and

Women’s Exhibition functions are becoming
something to be steadfastly avoided—the artists
practically outnumber the audiences. It some
concerts the management has patiently waited
half-an-hour over time in the hope of attracting a
quorum. Then the petticoated programme was
alternately dawdled and bolted through to uni-
versal dissatisfaction. By injudicious advertise-
ment, the masses are led to believe that the
classes are m undisputed possession ; so the large

the classes, who are easily tired, have concluded
that they prefer to exhaust .themselves by over-
feeding-at smart routs, to overworking at such an
impersonal occupation as exhibitioning. Lady
JNorthcote is about the most regular patron of the
place, but as she doesn’t permit her comings and
goings to take place to the sound of trumpets,
that fact doesn’t bring along the multitudes who
dearly love a Lady.

POLITICAL
Private Enterprise is on the rampage again
over the decision of the Federal Government to
accept the tender of the N.S. W. Government dock
for the construction of a trawler. It was the
lowest tender, and why shouldn’t the Govern-
ment tender as well as Private Enterprise ?
Everlastingly the mostly-Anti-Sosh man-
on-the-land wants Socialism. The latest request
is from the fruit-growers, who want “ Government
depdts in all the main distributing centres where
fruit can be stored, packed, graded, etc.” Heaven
only knows what that sort of Anti-Socialist’s
“etcetera ” may amount to.
N.S.W. ex-Premier Carruthers proposes
visiting England, and hints are thrown out that
he is to have the post of Agent-General. But
Wade will hardly dare to venture such a scandal-
ous job. N.S.W. has already an excellent Agent-
General in Coghlan, and the only possible excuse
for dispensing with his services would be the
abolition of the office.
The papers state that Deakin has been
asked some question by a Mr. Dugald Thomson.
We seem to have heard that name, somewhere, a
long time ago. There was a Dugald Thomson
who went to London with Lyne and Billy
Hughes; but he has never been heard of since.
Did he ever come back ?
N.S.W. Agent-General Coghlan has been
carrying on a controversy*with Lord Avebury and
others in London Times re the subject of old-age
pensions, and Coghlan scores pretty decisively,
though his noble antagonist thought him “more
forcible than courteous.” In England there is so
much cotton-wool wrapping about everything that
straight Australian seems rude when in reality it
is only logical.
The tariff goes through fairly well, all
things considered, and there is hopeful talk of an
adjournment before Christmas, with the tariff
finished as far as the Reps, are concerned. So far,
the range of duties as passed is, on the average,
higher than those proposed under the Kingston
tariff. If during the current week a satisfactory
degree of encouragement is given to the iron
industry, things will be not so bad.
Prime Minister Deakin, at Melbourne :
It was only five years ago that the first Australian
tariff displaced six tariffs. That tariff was submitted to
Parliament as a tariff for at least ten years, but it left
Parliament as one for five years only. A new tariff had
again been submitted for at least ten years, and it would
be a misfortune if it were again to leave Parliament as
suitable for only five years.
In other words, there can be no “fiscal peace’
until Australia gets a decent Protectionist tariff.
TOLL-PIKE TRADING.
It has always been an object with traders to stand two or three deep between the producer and the consumer, and make a new charge at every handling of the goods.— Age.
Grain and fodder speculators in Sydney
had a glorious time during those weeks when the
fool public was being bamboozled by yarns about
“UGo ~ • . . ~
T lnto P ayin o robbery
prices for anything and everything. A few
months ago maize was, say, 2s. lOd. a bushel. The
end of October saw it over 55., whiie genuine
buyers were trapesing Sussex-street, trying to
get it at under Gs. The same story was every-
where: “No maize.” Then a sprinkle of rain
fell over some of the coastal districts, and appa-
rently the rain produced a maize crop in a sin-le
night, for Sussex-street was at once trapesed by
eager sellers, looking for buyers at 4s. a bushel
For two days the panic lasted, and then Sussex-
street pulled itself together, and began to
hint at 4s. Id., 4s. 2d., 4s. 3d., and so on, up to
ds. 6d. But in those two days many of the
speculators were badly singed.
Secesh in W. A. , after lingering on in a
moribund state for the past two months, has
handed in its checks. O. R. Jones, the local
thinker who wanted to call out the tumbrils and
revolute in style when the anti-Fed. outburst first
started, turned up for the inquest, the other day,
with four others. Jones handled the job of
coroner, dropped a tear over the agitation which
he found to be well and truly dead, and sadly dis-
persed the meeting.
A new tram-shed is to be built in Moore
Park, Sydney, and the Railway Commissioners
called for tenders for 825,000 bricks. Then what
even the S. M. Herald refers to as “the perfect
nature of the organisation of the brick ring ” was
let out of the bag ; for the prices of the tenderers
were exactly the same—34s. per 1000 for common
and 38s. for selected bricks. Still, not having
brickworks of its own, the Railway Department
must pay just what the ring demands. And the
Railway Department is the people. Hooray for
Anti-Sosh !
Queensland M.L.A. Plunkett has taken to
Secesh of late as the Chow takes to opium. He
seceshed from Kidston to Philp a little while
back, so that the Federation, against which he
(Plunkett) cherishes 872 separate grievances, might
be the more expeditiously seceshed from. In the
last Parliament Plunkett sat as an independent
party of one—till the elections came, when he
uprose and seceshed from himself. He now
threatens darkly to secesh from Philp unless the
latter shows himself a more earnest secesher than
he is at present.
Tom Bent uttered a characteristic bellow
at Traralgon the other day, speaking of the
Argus’ attacks:—
We pay large sums of money to these people for adver-
tisements, and then they blackguard us. It might be a
good thing for the Government to publish its advertise-
ments in a paper of its own.
Does Tommy expect the papers to accept his
Government advertisements as bribes ? If so, it
is rational and legitimate to conclude that he
gives them as bribes. The corrupter is as bad as
the corrupted.
The new mail contract arranged with the
Orient Co. is as satisfactory as can be any con-
tract with a private firm. The Orient Co., it
seems, will cut away from its British connections,
and in time become practically an Australian
affair ; the ships will fly the Australian flag ; they
are to be of an improved and swifter type, and
will have good frozen-produce arrangements, so
that we may feed John Bull in comfort; white
labor is to be exclusively employed on the ships ;
and they are to keep in wireless-telegraphic com-
munication with the Australian coast until they
are 300 miles out.
Reid’s virtuous indignation over the
phrase “pauper labor,” applied to Britain, calls
softly to mind the epithets used by Reid, or in his
placid and unruffled presence, about the citizens of
different States of the Commonwealth. He put
a poster, “Beware of Victorian gold,”in King
Division. The insult was plain. He compared
the other colonies to “five drunkards.” He
laughed when Jack Want called the people of
the other States, especially Victoria, “wolves and
thieves.” He (Reid) said the South Australians
wanted to “steal our rivers.” In fact, he has
said things twice as bad about Australians as the
member did about pauper England. To find
fault with Carr’s phrase needed the cheek of a
highwayman.
With its customary acumen the Melbourne
Tramway Co., after talking matters over with
Chief Sec. Peacock, “ voluntarily ” reduced the
hours of its gripmen and conductors from 60 to 54
per week, without reduction of wages, thus raising
the rate for first-class gripmen from lOd. to lid.
per hour. M.L. A. Bromley’s promise to move an
amendment bringing the big monopoly’s employees
under the Factories Act threatened to produce
some unpleasant remarks, with the possibility of
drastic action to follow ; so the bear came down.
But there are still many employees working the
60 hours in engine-houses, on the tracks, and else-
where, and this partial reduction to 54 doesn’t
seem adequate reason why the Labor party should
now go to sleep.
The mayor and aldermen of the bankrupt,
ill-kempt, pocket-handkerchief borough of Cam-
perdown (Sydney), asked Minister for Works Lee
last week for a special endowment for their dis-
graced patch. Their rates are in the receiver’s
hands, and they had no money to keep their back
lanes clean. But the City Council of Sydney is
prepared to take over Camperdown, clean it up,
and make it a self-respecting part of the city.
That, though, would mean the extinction of the
mayor and aldermen, so the offer has been re-
fused. Minister Lee spoke kindly to the mayor,
and softly to the aldermen. Which was very
silly of Mr. Lee. The municipal authorities of
Camperdown deserve nothing but censure.
Ifc is more than likely that the Licenses
Reduction Board, now sitting in Victoria, will
prove utterly incapable of cutting out a single
license. Still, the salary paid to its members
will not be wholly wasted. When the pub-keeper
is upsides with the Licenses Reduction Act, and
there is involved a question of compensation, he
isn’t a hesitant about stating every individual
bean he makes per annum. And that informa-
tion will be of much interest to the Income Tax
Commissioner later on.
If Yes-No Reid gets the chance he will
probably take Representative Hedges (W.A.)
out into a quiet place and lose him. After making
a fearful blunder about the yarn imported into
Australia, this freak Foreign-trader chirpily
replied, in answer to a query whether he had gone
to the woollen mills for his information—“ No,
I wouldn’t go to the enemy for that,” Poor
Greid had to sit and listen to this Australian
legislator, openly calling Australian manufac-
turers his “enemies.” As for Jocook, his face
was a study in manslaughter.
Whenever it was mentioned that there
hadn’t been any difficulty in enforcing Arbitration
awards in Maoriland, the reply was always
thrown back that the Maoriland experience didn’t
count for anything, because there never were any
labor troubles while things were prosperous, and
Maoriland had been prosperous ever since Arbi-
tration arrived. Newcastle knocks out that reply,
anyway. For the coal trade never was brisker,
and the district never was more prosperous, than
it was at the very moment when the men knocked
off work. Some other reason to account for the
success of Arbitration in Maoriland will have to
be invented.
The preferential Customs allowances to
articles shipped from Britain will have to he
watched. Francois de Oastella lately supplied
Premier Bent with particulars of a choice concoc-
tion, “manufactured in England,” from dried
Grecian grapes, sugar, water, and certain
chemicals, and miscalled wine. There is, of
course, no preferential duty to help this abomina-
tion to compete against Australia’s natural grape-
juice ; but the British manufacturer is up to tricks
in all trades, and wine isn’t the only thing he
monkeys with. The preference should apply
only to goods that are British—and honest.
Varney Parkes was accepted by an Orange
gathering in Sydney the other day as his father’s
successor in the r&le of insectarian. If he thinks
that the Australian public is now what it was
in the time of his father he errs egregiously.
Australian interests have largely taken the place
in its mind which used to be occupied by
memories of sectarian faction fights and legends
of the Boyne and Vinegar Hill. The poison of
such memories and legends is not, it is true,
wholly eliminated from the Australian system;
but he is a dull bigot who does not realise that
most of it has gone, and the balance is going
rapidly.
Gov.-General Northcote, on Asiatic exclu-
sion, at Melbourne Gaud Mayor’s banquet, the
other day :
The position of Australia, and the possible danger from
abroad, reminded him somewhat of the position of the
British Parliament when the Bill for the exclusion of
James 11. from the throne was under discussion. Colonel
Titus, a member of the House of Commons, made a
speech, which was afterwards summarised in verse :
“ But Titus said, with his uncommon sense,
When the Exclusion Bill was in suspense :
‘ I hear a lion in the lobby roar ;
Say, Mr. Speaker, shall we shut the door,
And keep him there, or shall we let him in,
And try if we can turn him out again'? ’ ”
That seemed to him to be the position of Australia at the
present time.
Queensland has another political crisis.
Kidston wished to “ swamp ” the Upper House
so as to force his programme through. The
Governor refused to make the necessarj'- appoint-
ments. Kidston resigned and Philp was sent for
to form a Ministry. Kidston —as he was quite
entitled to do under the circumstances—refused
Philp an adjournment to form a Ministry, since it
was quite obvious that such a course involved a
waste of time, as Philp could not hope to
carry on. Then the Governor interfered and
asked Kidston to secure an adjournment for Philp,
and Kidston consented. So Philp is trying to get
a gang together. It is obvious that the only
power which can grapple with the obstructive Tory
House in Queensland is a Radical combination of
the Kidston following with the Labor party.
That combination, so far, seems impossible.
Whose the blame, it is hard to say. But the
country suffers whilst its saviors quarrel.
' Iron Bonus Bill episode (Lyne ex-
plaining) :
Members might not be aware that in the Dibbs tariff
in N.S.W. the duty on pig iron was postponed for 12
months in the hope that by that time the industry would
have been established ; but no steps were taken, and the
duty then became operative, and remained until removed
by the Reid Administration.
Joseph Cook : There never was a duty on pig iron in
N.S.W.
Lyne : I know what I’m talking about.
Cook : 1 again say that there never was a duty on pig
iron in N.S. W. /
Well, section 2, part 11. of N.S.W. Customs
Duties Act of 1891 (Dibbs tariff) provided : “ The
duty on pig iron shall be levied and paid at the
rate, and from the time mentioned in Schedule
‘A.’” And Schedule “A” said: “Pig iron,
from the first day in January, 1893, per ton 10s.”
N.S.W. Customs Duties Act of 1595 (Reid tariff)
repealed the pig iron duty ; which is exactly what
Lyne said. So far Cook hasn’t apologised.

A Riverina deputation, the other day,
asked T. Bent, of Victoria, to try to get them a
railway. Their chances look like Buckley’s.
Bent can’t build a line in N. S. Wales territory,
and Lee, of the Wade and Waddle Works
Department, “declares his unwillingness to do
anything simply for the benefit of Victoria.” So
because, if a railway were built, the traffic would
go to Melbourne instead of being hauled a vast
distance to Sydney, these people must do without
a railway and stick to the bullock system of
transport. The Wade and Waddle policy really
amounts to this : “ We won’t do anything that
won’t benefit Sydney.”
Sydney Telegraph is writhing in a
horrible tangle over the Newcastle coal strike.
First, it threw mud at the Arbitration Act, and
when it had well plastered the system and the
Court, it found a big strike raging, and the public
squealing for Arbitration. Then, to save its face,
it said that Arbitration was a splendid thing;
but, of course, the Arbitration Court was an
absurdity, and no self-respecting employers
would look at it, and a “special tribunal ” was
necessary. And just when it had committed
itself to that, the coal-owners resolved that the
Arbitration Court was the proper tribunal, and
that a special tribunal was an utterly wrong one,
because it would necessarily be made up, not of
impartial men, but of partisans. So the Tele-
graph doesn’t known what to do.
From an American Labor paper :
While Labor is objecting to Chinese and Japanese
workers being- admitted to America, capital is being
admitted over there, and will soon begin to flood the
world with goods made by pauper labor, even by child
labor. In Shanghai there are 20,000 women and
children workers in the silk mills. The maximum wage
is 26 cents for a 13-hour day, and many of the women
get five cents and the children three cents for a day’s
work. The wages and hours are much the same in the
cotton mills. These goods produced by this labor must
come into competition with the union-labor products of
America.
Well, they will first have to climb the Americar
tariff wall. Here in Australia, however, Brud
Smith will meet them with a soft spot and a wel
coming hand, while the Labor party is out in th
harbor sinking the fiscal issue.
Maoriland is now a country without an
orator. Joseph Ward is inaccurate and inconel’
sive, and very often unconvincing. Massey, who
said to lead Maoriland Opposition, is worse—it is
Massey’s impotence of speech that has given Ward
his reputation as a debater. There are now rocks
ahead. The true strength of Seddon as a leader
lay in his recognition of the weakness of the
Opposition, and his steady determination to keep
it crushed. Ward, who is not strong in that way,
dallies and placates, so that there is at least a
possibility that Massey, who is Tory to the teeth,
may some day be Premier. True, Massey
has one thing to commend him to the tolerance of
democrats. He disapproves of the wholesale'
borrowing of the Ward party. !
A thoroughly Australian Christmas gift'
for a friend abroad—a subscription to The Lone
Hand for 12 months. It will keep your friend
current with Australian life.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
“B”: A review of the English cricketera on
their Adelaide performances :
Fane, who starts with A. O. Jones, is a dull plodder
with few strokes and little power, not an international
man by a long way.
Captain Jones is attractive, but his batting is not safe
on fast wickets with a good field.
Hayes is lively and a trifle “ agricultural,” but didn’t
stay sufficiently long to show what he can do.
Hutchings has a magnificent straight drive and a daring,
graceful pull stroke. If he survives the first half-dozen
overs he will put up some big scores. An uncertain
starter.
Hardstaff appears to be the soundest and most versatile
bat of the side. Has a good style, and scores all round
the wicket.
Braund is still a careful, solid bat, who makes no
attempt to pile on runs before settling down.
Crawford will be a prime favorite. He hits hard with
no apparent effort. A splendid square cut and a fine cover
hit are his best strokes.
Rhodes is a greatly improved batsman of the solid
staying order, untroubled by nerves. He will prove
useful when things are going badly.
Barnes has learned something of batting since last here
with McLaren.
Humphries, a safe keeper.
Hobbs, Young, Blythe, and Fielder did not bat in
Adelaide.
Barnes will prove the mainstay of the attack depart-
ment. He has lost a little of his pace and gained in
accuracy.
Crawford has any amount of vim, and varies his stuff
cleverly. Will be very dangerous when the wicket gives
some assistance.
Blythe is accuracy itself, but not deadly on hard Aus-
tralian wickets. On a mud heap he would'probably get a
strong side out for under 50.
Rhodes seems to have lost a little of his skill in propor-
tion to his advance in batting.
Braund is dangerous to right-handers who are impa-
tient to score, but he is a gift to Clem Hill.
Hayes, who gets a fair number of wickets for Surrey,
will get fits from first-class men on hard wickets.
Fielder did not perform.
Altogether, Australia should recapture those ashes, as
the visitors are more dashing than sound in batting, and
their bowling should not be at all deadly on “shiny”
wickets. They are an excellent lot of fieldsmen, which
will help the bowlers ; but writer predicts some tall scor-
ing by the Australian Eleven.
* * « »
“Adelaide”: The one thing that definitely
went flop in the England-S.A. match was the reputation
of O’Connor, imported from Sydney by S.A. as a guide
and example for all young bowlers. Till the game began,
he was quite arguably one of Australia’s very best; but in
it he never looked like taking a wicket. As for Mcßeth,
imported at the same time, his name is already plain
Mud in Adelaide. While the earlier English batsmen
were always in doubt with Wright, and watched Roy
Hill’s fast medium stuff with respect, they pasted
O’Connor and Mcßeth without hesitation. Even when
the mighty smiting began, and one bowler was as good
as another (20 fours, two sixers, and a wicket was a fair
thing), it was Claxton and young Chamberlain who
induced the mishits that meant catches. O’Connor none
for 108, Mcßeth none for 98—such was the gloomy con-
clusion. The moral seems to be that a crack bowler who
would voluntarily leave his happy home to go and deal
with the shirt-front wickets of Adelaide Oval w'ould be
better advised to try running an ice-rink.

Peter’s College (Adelaide) team on December 19 and 20
• * * *
South Australian trundler A. Wright followed
up his fine form against the English team by securing
six wickets for 57 runs against West Torrens in Adelaide
district cricket on Saturday. Wright’s fine bowling
suggests a claim for a position in the Australian XI.
* * * *
The Adelaide Oval, the scene of the English-
men’s first serious cricket match, is a busy place these
times. It is the headquarters of S.A. Cricket Association
(which controls it, under a lease from the City Corpora-
tion) and Football League. All specially-important
lacrosse matches are played on it. There are strong
bowls, lawn-tennis, and croquet clubs, composed of its
“full members,” and always at play. It has now
gathered in the Lawn Tennis Association, which agrees
to play all club (as well as inter-State) matches on its
central courts. And it had a monopoly of cycle-racing,
while there was any. S.A. Government has now defi-
nitely retired from competition with the Oval, havino-
given the Agricultural Society, for Show purposes, a 50-
years’ lease of the Exhibition Oval, which at one time
gathered in all cycling and tennis, and even (in one
winter of squabble and “ crisis ”) all the football.
“0.K.”: Judged by the performance of four
of their bowlers against the Victorian team in the first
innings, the Englishmen have a poor chance of win-
ning three out of five test matches. On a wet wicket
they might knock up a decent score, and get their
opponents out very cheaply, but under typical Australian
conditions their matches against the strongest elevens
will be tests of patience in run-getting. They haven’t a
bowler who can be expected to do great things against
first-class batsmen. Australia will put up some long
scores, and the Englishmen, even if they have the batting
power, will lack the sticking power—the patience and
resolution—necessary to win a six-days match. Tarrant
and the young left-hander, Ransford, made as free with
the bowling as Clem Hill did in Adelaide. Ransford, in
fact, was hitting them all round the wicket up to
the time when he smote what was practically a sixer off
Braund. The ball happened to go so very directly in a
line with an outfield that the tall young man in question
put his right hand over the rails and made a catch. But
in rare cases of that sort the batsman deserves a sixer
more than he deserves to get out. I have seen other All
England teams play Victoriain bygone November months,
and this was the first time the visitors lacked a bowler
who could “ stick up ” the younger batsmen. It may be
argued that Blythe and Rhodes were standing out of' the
team. But they had been on the job in Adelaide, where
they were not at all deadly. Who is going to prevent a
picked Australian XI. from collaring the bow-ling on a
hard wicket?
* * • *
The recognised bowlers in the N.S.W. team to
meet the Englishmen on Friday got rough handling in
Sydney first-grade competition last Saturday. Cotter
gathered in a solitary wicket for 69 runs, Noble also got
one wicket at a cost of 43 runs, while Johnson had 40
runs knocked off him without dismissing a batsman.
Macartney, however, took four wickets for 70 runs
(against the weak Redfern team), and Kelleway secured
four for 38.
* * * *
In Sydney first-grade competition, North Sydney
nd Paddington scored three-point wins over Petersham
and Sydney respectively. The margin in each case was
10 wickets. Redfern downed Gordon by 125 runs, Middle
Harbor accounted for University by 17, Balmain beat
Central Cumberland by 13 runs, and Glebe knocked out
Burwood by 63 runs—all on the first innings. North Sydney
is now at the head of the competition with 10 points, and
has a substantial lead of four points on Paddington,
which holds second place. Middle Harbor is third with
four points, and Central Cumberland and Glebe together
with a brace each, while Redfern is just out of debt
with a solitary one; after that they taper off
to Sydney, which is 10 below zero. The sensa-
tion of the round was the unexpected walloping- of
University by Middle Harbor. With 197 required to
win, the students made the century with the loss of only
three wickets. Middle Harbor’s fielding was the cause of
the downfall, and, as the game went on, it grew keener
and keener, and five wickets fell for 77. Still, with two
wickets to go, the necessary 20 might be made up.
Then the end came suddenly, the two last wickets falling
for two runs.
* * * *
The N.S.W. Motor Boat Club opened the season
on Saturday last by proceshing to Bodd Island for a little
picnic. Although a heavy sea was running and the
wind howled along in spiteful gusts, the company
had a g’ood time. The cruiser race was started, and con-
tested over four cheerful miles, during- which the boats
shipped numerous seas and staggered back to the finish-
ing line half full of water. The result was a win for the
limit boat, Seagull, which got away 18min.34sec. ahead
of the others. When Secretary Holdsworth looked at the
drenched boats, and thought of the discomfort of the ladies
who had to go home in them, he scratched off the Scouting
Race, in which a number of flags had to be picked up
from floating buoys while going at full speed, and proposed
another round of refreshments instead'. His motion was
carried unanimously. A feature of the show was the re-
appearance of the Baby de Dion, which Mr. Sandeman
has altered and improved. In spite of the rough water,
the altered and amended flyer cut out about 15 miles an
hour. Invincible is away in Maoriland.
* * * *
Prince Alfred Yacht Club’s race for the Raw-
son Oup on Port Jackson on Saturday was a glorious
affair. A wild nor’-nor’-easter made for excitement
which was increased by the fact that all previous winners
were competing, and that if Mr. Cutler’s trusty Kuku-
burra could score, it would be his third and ‘decisive
victory, which would land the trophy on his sideboard as
his exclusive property. But the gallant Cutler was right
up against it, for the mighty Rawhiti was there, and the
speedy Awanui, Mr. Saxton’s beautiful flier, the racer of
Port Jackson, was also in the hunt. Many looked upon
the affair as a contest between Rawhiti and Awanui. But
a rapid succession of accidents changed the aspect of
things entirely. Rawhiti’s jib carried away, and 108
precious seconds were lost effecting repairs. Then Kuku-
burra’s luck blew right out, something going wrong with
the head sails, compelling Cutler to lower away and
drift to leeward out of the race. Down at the
Heads, Fleetwing’s head-sails also went wrong, and
Mr. Saver had to retire. ■ Oulwulla led the way
to Manly, with Herreshoff and the flying Sunbeam
close behind, with White Wings, Cooya, Awanui, and
Rawhiti following in that order. Culwulla flashed away
across the Heads, leading past the Island with Sunbeam
only a minute and a half behind, and White Wings soar-
ing up closer every minute. At Fort Denison Sunbeam
danced to the front, with Culwulla and White Wings
second and third. A short tack brought White
Wings within striking distance, and in a desperate
effort to make up the lost ground Awanui’s skipper
kept the topsail aloft, although the beautiful boat
fairly staggered under it. At the Pile Light White Wings
h id got to the front, Awanui being unable to hoist the
topsail owing to the gear having jambed. But both
Culwulla and Awanui had passed the ’Beam, and they
finished in that order, White Wings winning by 4min.
4see., while Awanui was 33sec. behind the second.
But, as Dan’l O’Oonnor would say, “ th’ glory
an’ sphlendor av that gusty afthernoon ” fell brightest on
the professionals in the open 18-footers. Their race was
worthy of a contest for Neptune’s holy trident. The
fleetwinged Maritana, with her crew defying gravitation
on one side, challenged the heavyweather champion
Crescent to a rough-water duel, and beat her handsomely,
winning the first heat with a margin of 30sec. in
a race that saw one craft capsize on the way to
the _ line, and three others forced to drop out on
the journey. The second heat saw Mona fling out a full-
size suit of sails to the whistling breeze, and beat Aus-
tralian, although it was steered by that Nestor of the
game, Chris Webb. The brunt came when all the cham-
pions met in the final—Maritana, Crescent, Australian,
Eileen, Arthur, Donnelly, Mona, and Scot. Eileen
beat Australian for the wind at the start, and
led right across the Harbor to where a white
squall almost lifted her from the water, and
threatened to carry. her right away from the rest of
the fleet. But behind came the heavy-weather boat
Crescent, bowling along steadily, making quick little
spurts that meant a lot, fighting a great duel with Mari-
tana, whose gigantic ballooner dragged her along in spite
of the fact that Orescent had the weather gauge. Then
Maritana juggled out, and, bounding away to windward,
won out with half a minute to spare, after a most pic-
turesque race.
* * * *
The Australasian Championship meeting, to
decide which is the swiftest boat in the Southern seas,
will eventuate on Port Jackson the second Saturday in
March. Queensland will be represented by a boat
specially built for the purpose, and there is a possi-
bility that Auckland may find a flyer to represent
Maoriland, while N.S.W. will have several champions.
So far, it is to be regretted, there is no word from Mort’s
Dock about building a new Invincible to down the lot,
and carry the Australian flag to victory. The only
restriction imposed by the Club is that boats must not
exceed 45ft. in length, and into that sized hull owners
can place any power they like, and can carry as many or
as few passengers as they like. All competing boats are
to carry efficient mufflers.
* * » *
Victorian Four-Oared Championship attracted
two competitors—Albert Park and Mercantile—over the
miles lower Yarra course, Saturday afternoon last. Re-
gards weight, the crews were very evenly matched, the
“ Parks ” averaging list. 71b., having just a 21b. advantage.
At the start, Parks had the advantage of the pile
sheeting side of the course, which is well sheltered.
Striking the water, however, Mercs, got a smart
niove on, and the first 100 yards or so saw ’em
in front, but they were lurching, and the boat was
burying considerably in the wash of a steamer. At
the Pumping-Station Jim Donald had picked his crew
together without apparent effort, and was three-quarters
of a length ahead of Mercs., who were still a trifle rocky,
though the boat was travelling better than at the get-
away. Crossing to the west side at the Chemical
Works, Ivens made an effort in the smooth water
to pick up the leaders, but the old *un in front
was too knowing to lose any advantage at this
stage, so kept going. From the Works to the finish it
was a mere procession, Donald eventually passing the
judge three clear lengths to the good, easing up, having
put the journey behind in 16min. 55 2-ssec.

Webb handed in the second deposit of £2OO
last week in connection with the sculling champion-
ship. Tresidder’s money was paid at Sydney (by
special arrangement with Webb), but there are grave
doubts whether he will be able to gather in the remainder
of the stake-money. A circular has gone round to
gather in funds.
• * • *
“The Kaiser had a good day’s shooting at
Windsor, and at the close of the day’s operations had
bagged 700 birds.” So says a recent cable. That means
140 birds an hour for five hours. Possibly the Kaiser, who
can do no wrong, had a duck gun, and wiped out a dozen
birds at a time. This is the sport of Kings ! It makes
one reflect with something like satisfaction on the fact
that kings themselves have been made the sport of
groundlings.
*■ * * *
The general result of the Pioneer Motor Cycle
Club’s reliability contest was to demonstrate that the
weak spots of the motor bike are the tyres and belts, and
that the low-powered machine is just as good as the other
sort for general utility. Of the five men who qualified for
the run-off, four rode machines whose engines ranged
from 2J to 3 h.p., and the other was mounted on
alf h.p. cycle. The 3J to 4J, and 4J to 6 h.p. men were
right out of the hunt. In the final, Tatham’s 2£ h.p.
Minerva won, with Olouting’s 8 h.p. Fafnir and Brook’s
I 3-4 h.p. Speedwell a dead heat for second place. Palmer
had to retire from the final owingto his belt carrying away.
The road was far from good—was rough, in fact; but all
the machines maintained an average speed of 20 miles an
hour. On top of the many other demonstrations in
various parts of the world, this ought to just about kill
the craze for high-powered motor bikes. There is too
much vibration about them.
* * * *
Nominations for the Austral Wheel Race meet-
ing close with the Melbourne Bicycle Club, on Saturday,
November 23 ; and handicaps will be declared on the 26th.
Unless the M. 8.0. can get a wooden and properly banked
track laid down for the event, the Austral meeting will
be about the most ghastly frost that has been seen in even
much-bitten Melbourne. Racing on the grass is too slow
for the modern follower of the game.
* * * *
Brigadier-General Gordon has suggested that
the N.S.W. motorists should form a military motor
corps. If the motoring doctors can be roped in, that
corps will be a deadly organisation, and equal to an
ordinary army corps. Consider the number of people
the motoring medicos kill now when they are trying
not to. Human nature shudders to think of what will
happen when they are out to do their worst, and best, in
the killing line. The Automobile Club will consider the
suggestion at the annual meeting, to be held on the 28th,
and there is a large probability that something in the
shape of a fighting machine will be evolved. The idea is
a good one.
« » * *
It looks as if, though the coming Austral may
be a “ sassiety success,” it won’t amount to a box of corn
plasters as a cycling carnival. On King’s birthday, the
three returned track champions, MacFarland, A. J. Clark,
and Pye, took a few turns out on the Carlton Oval, in an
alleged sports meeting, by way of breaking themselves in
to grass riding for the big meeting. The ground was in
its usual place, and several bikists happened along; but
the crowd had lost itself on the road, likewise the
prizes were as small as the attendance. Neverthe-
less, two world’s champions and the holder of the
five-mile record of this earth pursued one another
many times round the grass for the edification
of about 200 adults and 20 small boys. A mile handicap
went to R. H. Lynch on the 160yds. mark, and even in the
5-mile scratch the three once mighty warriors finished in
the ruck, the all-pervading Thomas pushing his jigger
home first, Bob Morgan second, and P. Hehir third.
Looking at the circumstance casually, this paper has a
suspicion that the M. 8.0. would be in pocket if it
dropped the Austral altogether for a space or so, and
handed Thomas a moderate sum as the equivalent of
his chance.
* * * *
The British Museum authorities have ap-
proached S. F. Edge, the Napier driver who drove his car
for 24 hours at the average speed of 65J miles an hour
round the Brooklands track. The Museum wants the
Dunlop-shod, six-cylinder, 60-h.p. petrol car just as it
stands, to show future generations what sort of a
vehicle it was that first carried man over a mile a
minute for a full day. To make the thing really com-
plete, Edge himself should be carefully stuffed and
seated in the car, just to show what sort of men they
were who did it. There is no reason why Egyptian
kings and queens should have a monopoly of the lime-
light in the hoary old Museum.
* » * *
“ Quan ” : Bulletin (31/10/’O7) mentions several
branches of sport, etc., in which Australians hold first
place. It’s a very decent little lot, but I want to add
another item to it. John Hamilton, of Queensland, holds
the world’s revolver record, 123 points out of a possible
126, made in 1897. The merit of this performance may
be gauged from the fact that the whole of Hamilton’s 18
shots were fired consecutively, while Warrant-Officer
Raven, of the visiting British rifle team, who has been
champion revolver shot of Great Britain for three years,
and who won the revolver championship of Queensland in
Brisbane last month with a score of 117, got that total
under new rules, which allowed him to count his best six
consecutive shots at each of the 20, 30, and 50yds. ranges.
Hamilton did not shoot in the last Brisbane contest.
* * * *
The half-smothered objections to theV.R.C.’s
control of Vic. racing bubbled up again in Melbourne
during Cup week, moved, apparently, by Wren’s Ascot
Two Thousand. Illogically enough, the irreconcilables
railed against the grasping policy of the proprietary race-
course owners, and then wept because the V.R.C.
wouldn’t let them run their nags at the unregistered
meetings—all proprietary. Rightly they claimed that
the big club had merely assumed control of racing, and
that a body properly representative of all racing clubs
throughout the State should undertake the job ; but they
didn’t explain how they proposed to enable such a body,
when elected, to get control. The only suggestion of any
value was that the tote should be legalised, and that the
profits thereof should be legislatively diverted from the
pockets of private course-owners. The English Jockey
Club allows proprietary clubs to snavel only 10 per cent,
of the profits, but the V.R.O. hasn’t thought fit to follow
such a good precedent.
» m * *
“ Billy T.” : The Spring Carnival in Melbourne
is becoming a painfully circumspect affair. As an artist
I ask for light and shade in all things ; at Flemington on
Derby, Oup, Oaks, and Steeple days there was no shade.
It was a flood of kindly light, a mammoth exhibition of
the bright side of human nature. There was no black-
guardliness to lend diversity, no sins and wickednesses
to provide effective color. Without wickedness we cannot
have literature, and Melbourne Cup will add nothing to
the sum of letters if the scamp, the spielers, and the man
witht.heintellectualgooseare rigorously forbidden. I know
the Jud and his kin talk with frothing lips of the scarlet
sins of Flemington, but in their eyes sin consists in the
prosaic operation of putting two spare half-crowns in the
bag of a raucous and excited Hebrew. To my mind, that
is a form of infamy as stainless as church tennis, and
scarcely lends the interest of a little white fib to the car-
nival. Talk about the Oup as a saturnalia 1 Why, on my
word of honor as a Sunday-school scholar, a Little Bethel
scrub picnic is a whirl of Pagan unrighteousness com-
pared with it. The fact is, Oup Day is becoming too good
to be true—too good to be true to human nature ; and
I am inclined to shed tears for its departed rascals, its
lost iniquity. The mug is safe at Flemington now, but is
he happy ?

* ■* * *
Melb. Gap winner Apologue should be worth
every penny of the £l5OO paid by the Indian buyer. On
the day before he was auctioned the horse received
another good advt. in the Williamstown Cup victory of
Thackeray’s. Thackeray was backed down to 6 to 1 on
the strength of his having run second to Apologue at
Caulfield over the same distance (11 furlongs) at a differ-
ence of 301 b. or more. Judging from their running at
Caulfield, the burly Maorilander could have won the
Williamstown Cup, with 9.12 up. In his absence,
Thackeray got home with a bit to spare. Before being
shipped to India, next year, Apologue—now transferred
to Higinbotham’s stable—is to be raced in any w.f.a.
events that offer, and if he should meet his late stable
companion, Poseidon, punters will take short odds about
his chance.
* « * *
Chairman Reid, of Victorian Owners and
Trainers’ Association, has discovered that horse-breeding
in Victoria is a dying industry. Twenty-five years ago
Victoria had 752 brood mares to N.S.W.’s 762. Twenty-
two years later Victoria’s lot had shrunk to 640, while
N.S.W. had increased to 1440; and at present the Ma
State leads with 1500 to 600 ; and at last Randwiek sales
blood yearlings brought £40,000, as against £4OOO realised
at Newmarket (Vic.).
.*• * *
At Rosehill Club’s two-days Birthday meeting
the heads were literally tugged off some of the weU-
backed-by-the-public nags to prevent them winning.
Amply-justified-by-previous-form backing of horses had
to be carried on at buying-money rates, after which the
horses suddenly slithered into rank outside positions.
Which taught the public that if it will get on before the
stable it must pay the penalty. A. J.C. stipendiaries were
there, but were as dull as usual.
Most promising two-year-old seen in Sydney
for a long while is Malt Queen, by Malster from Her
Majesty. The geegee was backed solidly in Rosehiil
Nursery Stakes, Saturday, and showed utter contempt
for 14 other starters, none of which, but Maid Merton, had
raced before.

“ Gr. Elb.Poseidon won the Melbourne Cup
last year carrying 7st. 61b., and Apologue this year
humped the awful burden of 7st. 91b. I’m afraid there is
almost as much snuffle about racing as about religion.
Racing improves the stamina of the noble prad—ye gods,
when the champions carry 7st. mannikins ! The modern
gee of the city racecourse is a polished, finely-drawn gal-
loping machine, warranted to run down early. There are
very few races of over 1£ mile ; the majority are Sheol-
for-leather sprints. A hoss that can win over two miles,
when carrying about 9 stone in all, is spoken of with bated
breath ; but it is a fragment of a man who can ride at
under 10 stone, and, except on a racecourse, two miles is
no distance at all. lam not Judding, but why can’t we
be honest about the business ?

C. Quinn, the Adelaide trainer, has made what
the local papers call a “ rather peculiar discovery” about
the gelding Fruitful, bought as a yearlirg at the Sydney
sales last March. Fruitful has run in several races in
Melbourne, claiming the usual allowance, and now it
appears that he is—well, not a gelding 1 Fortunately he
did not win any of those races, or a curious position
would have arisen.
* * * *
Last week, in M.L., 13 bookmakers were sent to
gaol for two months, and five others for one month, for
trespassing on a racecourse and betting in opposition to
the totalisator. There’s no hope for Ilcey Mo in Maori-
land, and the law is evidently sufficiently strong to keep
illegal betting strictly under heel.

“M.P.A.”: Grus Rennert’s display against
Buttan Sjngh, t’other week, fully substantiated B.
6/6/’O7, which classed him as a showman, and Singh as
the real pea. “Hank” (8.13/6/’O7) commented on the

marked fiackenschmidite. Apart from exhibition
goes, Rennert had no claim as a match ’rastler,
and his association with the Russian was his
only means of securing recognition. Hackey’s many

except what he required in music-hall displays.
Except in the case of Kock, a fine German athlete,

* » » *
At the Athletic Pavilion, Melbourne, the other
evening, two lightweight Australian championships were
decided in a couple of hours. Horace Prime, an
Australian wrestler, beat Lew Langford, of England,
getting two clean falls out of the British specialist in
short order. Then Sydneysider Hock Keys, Australia’s
brightest lightweight, put it across Hughie Meeghan, the
Melbourne lad who has come up towards the top very
suddenly. Meeghan is a clever, game lad, but he has a
lot to learn before it will be in him to whip Keys, who has
raised the art of lodging his own blows and frustrating
the other fellow’s, to an exact science. Keys lacks
punishing power, however, and Meeghan was still with
him when the 20th round was called on. Kej's won on
points.
• * * *
“J.G.”: I saw a man the other day at one
of the Victorian up-country townships who bought an old-
fashioned, hard-tyre boneshaker bike for three half-
crowns. The tyres promptly flew to pieces. The cyclist
then used up two balls of binder twine, which he bound
round the hike wheels to act as tyres. Next day, after
he had got over about a mile of his trip to Melbourne, his
twine tyres started to cut out, and the cyclist left a trail
of mangled twine behind him. Being a bit of an in-
ventive genius, he dismounted, and cut out about a
hundred yards of No. 8 wire from a cocky’s fence, and
bound it round his machine wheels in place of the twine.
After a few hours’ hard graft he again started. And
although the machine weighed something under a ton,
and made a row akin to that of a traction engine, the
rider seemed to get up a fair pace on it when going
down hill.
