Dusky cricketer Ranjitsinhji recently insti-
tuted a royal (coffee-colored) cricket team, and imported
the Sussex (Eng.) cracks, Vine anr | q ox> coach the
mgs. Now he has further added top-notch stumper
Lilley to the imperial leather-belting staff.
* * •
“ Talbot ’’ : London Referee, tired of drawn
matches, propounds a new system of cricket. It is
simply that each batsman have a fixed time at the
wickets—say half-an-hour ; that he make all he can, and
be penalised for each time he gets out—say 10 for the
first time, 20 for the second, and so on. The disadvan-
tages of the scheme are obvious, but it has its good
points. Every game would last a definite, pre-arranged
time, and, given fine weather, no game could be drawn.
Busy men could play more, because when their side was
in they would need only attend for half-an-hour at a given
time ; and the spectator wanting to see Jessop or Clem
Hill could consult the advertised time, and would not be
disappointed if the favorite got “out” first ball. Slow
batting would be at a discount. Also, though the Referee
hasn t thought of it, all-round players would flourish,
because the mere bowler who got out eight or ten times
in his half-hour would lose the side more runs than he
was worth ; finalty, it would be an immense advan-
tage for a side to bat last, and know just how many runs
it must make in the time to win.

BILIOUSNESS.
If you recognise that the Liver is the sewage
farm of the body, you will see the
importance of its free action.
The blood is easily poisoned by morbid pro-
ducts formed within the body, and
delayed in excretion.
A lazy liver is responsible for constipation,
the greatest disease —breeding disorder
of the body.
The retention of septic matter in the bowels
is a constant source of danger, affecting
blood and nerves.
The question is—How can we keep the liver
in order?
According to the most distinguished medical
evidence, the Liver is amenable to
Kutnow’s Powder.
The Liver is responsive to the action of
Kutnow’s Powder, without the patient
being hardly aware of it.
Kutnow’s Powder is delightfully gentle, pain-
less, and soothing, but its beneficial
effect does not finish with the Liver.
It flushes the kidneys, cleanses the bladder,
regulates the bowels, clears and purifies
the whole alimentary canal.
No artificial remedy can supersede the
natural efficiency of Kutnow’s Powder,
it has an immediate effect.
It makes you feel relieved and gay ; you can
then put your heart into your work and
do your best.
It gives you vigor, and vigor is contagious,
furnishing untiring strength and vital
force.
An inactive Liver has a most depressing
effect on the temperament, but Kutnow’s
Powder quickly relieves the tension.
Kutnow’s Powder keeps every organ in the
body fit, free, and in thorough working
order, hence health follows as matter of
course.
THE LIVER
ITS USE AM ABUSE.
The Liver, like the Kidneys and other organs, is liable to
various acute and chronic diseases. It is the great wheel of
life’s machine ; it regulates the whole mechanism of man ;
when it works well—then every other gland and factor in the
system works well, and there is general good health. That is
the use of the Liver. The abuse is brought about by our
mode of living; the consumption of rich food and alcoholic
stimulants is a conscious abuse ; we know it, but neglect to
arrest the damage by neutralising and correcting the mischief
until the liver becomes sluggish. The first sign of oncoming
disorder is headache, then a disagreeable taste in the mouth,
loss of appetite, biliousness, and constipation. The remedy
for this state of affairs is a dose of that famous Liver regulator, >
Kutnow’s Powder. A desert spoonful in a glass of water,
before breakfast, acts like a refreshing bath on the whole ,
system ; it cleanses the stomach, liver, kidneys, and bowels,
and gets rid of the poisonous debris so injurious to health.
I
TO KEEP THE LIVER ACTIVE. /
Perhaps you are not aware that the enjoyment of life-
depends to a large extent on the healthy activity of the Liver.
If you wish to enjoy your food, to be able to digest almost
anything, take a few doses of Kutnow’s Powder, which rouses
the liver, flushes the food passages, clears away the sour
fermented debris, expels the surplus uric acid, and invigorates
the system.

and know

EIGHT YEARS SUFFERING.
A PERMANENT CURE.
THE CASE OF MRS. A. WEAVER.
BY A SYDNEY REPORTER.
o one can tell the self-denying work which
good men have done in the early days of this
country, neither can we exaggerate the nobleness
of spirit which animates present-day people in a
desire to ameliorate distress as much as lies in their
power. Therefore, the public should be informed
ot some of these efforts, one of which was made by
Mrs. Alice Weaver, of No. 7 Louis Street, Redfern,
who replied to the reporter by saying :
About this time last year I was living in
lelopea Street, and it was just before I left there
for this place that I was fortunate in casting aside
a complaint that had worried me for no less than
n'j v.^ earS ‘ a fearful life of suffering mine
had been all that time, to be sure, and to think that
nothing in the world could benefit me made my
spirits droop lower than they will ever be again
I hope.”
“What made you think that nothing could
benefit you ? ” queried the writer.
, ‘‘ I arrived at that conclusion because nothing
had done me good so far, and I believe I tried
every medicine going. At the time my second
child was born I was under a doctor, who did
everything he could for rue, but still my health re-
mained as bad as it could be. Indeed, at that
period I was nearly dead with indigestion, al-
though I dieted myself and did all that could be
done to keep my torments in check. It was of no
avail, though, for when I ate anything I could
hardly breathe owing to a feeling which came like
a lump in my chest, from which place a sharp pain
went right through to the centre of my shoulders.
1 then remained in the utmost agony until the food
was vomited up again, after which I felt so ex-
hausted that I cquld not think of doing a thing in
the house until I had had a good rest. It would
surpriseyouifyou only knew how poor my appetite
was. You would wonder how anyone could keep
alive with it, and as for my head, oh, niy sufferings
with it were awful. The most piercing pains at
onetime, and the most depressingachesat another.
Really, I think it was a marvel how I kept my
senses with the agony I endured in the head ; and'i
it’s strange that I did not meet with many a nasty ,
accident through the giddiness which used to over- j
take me. I was a misery to myself and everybody
around me, and to tel] you the honest truth I never
thought I would live through it all. My eyes used
to feel heavy enough to drop out of their sockets,
and I was affected in the mouth by such a bitter
taste that it made me feel sick of a morning, after
putting in the usual restless nights that had done
much towards making a wreck of me. Even in the
daytime Iwasalwaysimagining something terrible
was going to happen, and these were just the very
things that Clements Tonic, which I commenced to
take, showed itself to be the master of; for it sent
such a vigor through my frame, when I had been
taking it a couple of weeks, that I could not help
feeling joyful in the belief that at last my health
had taken a beneficial turn. I got more Clements
Tonic, and as each bottle was finished, so did my
prospects improve, for I commenced to take my
meals with a degree of heartiness which astonished
my people as much as it pleased them ; and to my
own satisfaction anddelight theformer discomforts
attending my meals were quickly out of existence.
Oh, dear, what a changed woman I am now to then.
My limbs do not ache from weakness like they
formerly did, and I can go about my work as
cheerfully and as actively as any housewife in the
country. Ido not spend any restless nights now;
never dream during my sleep, and the pains that
used to abound have long since been driven away
from every part of my body. Well, Clements
Tonic made me the healthy woman I am, and for
the sake of other sufferers I trust you’ll publish
this interview in any way you think fit.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Alice Weaver, of No. 7 Louis Street, ltedfern, in the
State of New South Wales, Commonwealth of Australia,
do solemnly and sincerely declare that I have carefully
read the annexed document, and that it contains, and is
a true and faithful account of my illness and cure by
Clements Tonic, and also contains my full permission to
publish in any way my statements, which I give voluntary,
without receiving any payment, and I make this solemn
declaration conscientiously believing the same to be true,
and by virtue of the provisions of the Oaths Act, 1900.
(Signed) ALICE WEAVER.
Subscribed and declared at Redfern, this sixth day of
September, one thousand nine hundred and four, before
me, L. BREARLEY.

SPORT AND SPOOF.
Nulla: I met the “sweep” promoter at
Coogee t’other day, and the nonchalant way he hawked
around a book of tickets tempted me to inquire what he
thought of his prospects of getting into gaol. My fears
amused him. “They can’t do nothing,” he said posi-
tively. The Ack says a fine ;in default, levy’n’distress.
Well, I’ve got no home, no furniture, no nothin’, so what
can they do?” Then I also wondered what the sweep-
winners could do, and didn’t invest.
* * * *
To get a fair idea of one’s chance of not draw-
ing a prize in a Tatt. sweep the hopeful speculator should
apply for, say, the first 10 and last 10 tickets in any
100,000. Has No. 1. or No. 99999 ever appeared on a
result sheet? In this year’s 10-shillings sweep on Mel-
bourne Cup (which was duly filled) No. 172 drew a non-
starting horse, and Nos. 239 and 234 drew a £lO and a £5
prize respectively. These were the lowest on the list
The highest number to get anything was 99780 (a non-
starter). 32069 was Apologue’s number, Mooltan was
72147, and luck hovered in its vicinity, for 72102, 72180
72131, 72185 and 72194 drew small cash prizes. ’
* * * *
‘“lf you win, I’ll break iviry bone in your
miserable little carcase,’ ses Murphy to me, an’ they had
a crimson steward at every furlong. ‘ Why don’t you fill
the mare with water, blast yer?’ I ses. Murphy ses
nothin’—he on’y glared. S’help me, the mare bolted'from
the tapes, an’ I had to flog me boot with the mop to kid
I was ridin’ like hell w’en I took a pull. An’ it was over
sticks. Strike me dead if I didn’t see meself a mangled stiff
in thebloomin”orspital. Mullany’spraddropped atthefirst
fence, and Naylor’s brute carried away the rail alongside.
That left me an’ O’Brien’s mare an’ Tom Rogers’ old"
camel. They wedged me in two jumps from ’ome, an
I had to let her go to lose the ambulance. Gord knows
what Murphy backed. Put me in the morgue if both the
cows didn’t refuse, an’ Murphy’s mare had another mile
in her. I spots Murphy’s 'red whiskers wavin’ in the
gentle breeze near the corner as I comes at the last fence,
an’ I ses to meself, ‘ Here goes ! ’ When she riz, I slips
me trilbies out of the irons and rolls kickin’ on to the
track. It was a soft drop, an’ I plays rabbit till they
brings a flask along. None of ’em finished. Murphy
booted me, the hog.”
* * * *
“ Snell ” : What struck me more than anything
else during two days spent at Flemington this season
was the large number of maimed men discoverable in
the saddling paddock and loitering on the fringe of the
crowd under the Elms. I drew the attention of a more
experienced friend to the curious circumstance. “ In-
jured jockeys ! ” he answered, Taking
the trouble to make inquiries in a few instances, I
learned enough to satisfy me that the man of experience
had diagnosed correctly. These small, wizened men
with the wry limbs, bulging breasts, twisted backs, and
distorted features are the martyrs to the game. Bred in
an atmosphere of horse, they are never happy out of the
smell of the stables, and they gather at the great events,
pitiful reminders of what Australia pays for its favorite
game. But, while the mortuary cart trots in attendance
on the hurdlers, and rarely fails to return with either
killed or wounded, the placid public still believes that
pugilism is the only sport deserving of the firm condemna-
tion of right-thinking people.
CAN A CYCLIST BE A CHRISTIAN
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Cyclist Brook has got religion,
He’s a much-converted youth,
Who’s been punctured in the conscience
By the arrows of the “ trewth.”
He has cut the track for ever,
So the daily papers say,
And his jigger’s pointed skyward
To the shining milky way.
But he slangs his ancient cobbers
In a manner that’s sublime,
And he,more than hints that Satan
Was a cyclist in his time.
Quite an intimate acquaintance
With the subject he betrays,
While the blooming gaff he’s blowing
On the wily wheelman’s ways—
With a realistic shudder
At the most immoral code
Of the blokes he used to chum with
On the track and on the road.
All his pals gape wide with wonder
As he gives away the show ;
For he makes it plain that cyclists
To a warmish place must go.
And his pathway up to Heaven,
We are quite prepared to say,
Will be thickly strewn with tin-tacks
If the wheelmen get their way.
He was riding, so he tells us,
Down the primrose path to Hell,
With his jigger geared to ninety,
And no lamp, or brake, or bell.
He was practically “ coasting ”
Down a mountain, he explained,
With a certainty of roasting
When the bottom wa,s attained.
He was ridin’ loose and careless
Down this most attractive track
With his bearin’s runnin’ easy
And his chain a trifle slack.
And the grade was one in forty,
And he mentions with regret,
That quite cheerful-like and rorty
Was the company he met.
Ne’er a sign-board gave him notice
There was danger on the hill,
And no warning voice apprised him
He was taking risks, until
An evangelistic person
He encountered on a ship
Ups and gives his nibs the “ office,”
Or the “ griffin,” or the “ tip.”
Then his hair stood up in horror,
Like an angry cocky’s crest,
And he started to back-pedal
Like a cyclist who’s possessed.
And he never ceased his prowess
With the pedals to display,
Till his jigger was arrested
And turned round the other way.
Then he straightway dropped his handles
Half a foot, and bene his back
Till his Grecian nose was scratchin’
Up the gravel on the track.
And hepedalledin a manner
Quite unknown to the “ purfesh ”
As he sprinted from the temptings
Of the Devil and the flesh.
Now he says he’s full of riding
On the pleasant primrose way ;
That he’ll chance the burrs and tin-tacks
On the straight and narrow way,
And he warns all sinful sprinters
They should take up other roles,
Or the Devil with his pitch-fork
Will be puncturing their souls.
N.S.W.
IRONBARK.

Melbourne Argus gets this from one of its
country correspondents :—
The Neerim South Progress Association has been en-
deavoring to have 700 acres of land, adjoining the town-
ship, acquired by the Closer Settlement Board, and sub-
divided into small blocks. The price demanded by the
owner, Dr. Peacock, is, however, so high that it is pro-
bable the proposal will be abandoned'. It is more than
double the shire council valuation.
Yet no doubt the Argus will still stick to its old
yarn that land taxes and other measures for bursting
up big estates are quite unnecessary, owners being
prepared to do the bursting-up themselves with-
out any hastening cracker being tied to their coat-
tails.

BRONCHITIS.
A SOUTH AUSTRALIAN STOREKEEPER TESTIFIES TO THE EFFICACY OF HEARNE’S BRONCHITIS
CURE.
A SUFFERER 70 YEARS OLD CURED BY IT.
WELL AND HEARTY NOW.
Mr. W. O. Hearne. Dear Sir,—l am a storekeeper, living in this township for the last 20 years. One of my
oldest customers has been subject to Bronchitis for a number of years, and has been in the hospital with it
several times. Hearing* of your valuable medicine, he tried it, and found that it gave him great relief. One
bottle was sufficient to put him right. This gentleman is over 70 years of age, an old bushman, who has been
in these parts for the last 30 years, and is well known. He makes my house his home when here, and got the
medicine from me. He is at my place now, and he says that this winter is the freest he has had from Bronchitis
for a number of years, and he wishes to return you thanks for your valuable Bronchitis Cure. He is well and
hearty now. You are quite at liberty to publish this or any part of it you like, as it may be of great benefit
to other people suffering from Bronchitis.—Yours truly, .
M. COGLIN, Storekeeper, Yunta, South Australia.

BRONCHITIS AND ASTHMA.
A COMMERCIAL TRAVELLER SUFFERING INTENSELY CURED BY HEARNE’S BRONCHITIS CURE.
HAS SINCE CAMPED OUT AND TRAVELLED MUCH WITHOUT CONTRACTING A COLD.
Mr. W. G. Hearne. Dear Sir,—ln 1898 (I was for four years previously in Queensland travelling representative
for a mercantile house), having contracted a heavy cold, I placed myself under a medical man, and used all kinds
of chemists’ prescriptions without avail. I was then staying at Hayes’ Terminus Hotel, Brisbane. The manager
did all possible for me, for which I shall always feel grateful. Mr. Duncan Currie, at that time acting manager for
the New York Life Insurance Co., came to see me daily, and he advised me to try your Bronchitis Cure. I was about
done for, could not eat, or scarcely swallow ; in fact, used to nearly suffocate a dozen times a day, suffering intense! v
Guess of my thankfulness when Mr. Currie brought me a bottle of your Bronchitis Cure. A few doses actually cured
me. I took another bottle away from the city with me, and gave part of it to a young girl at Southport audit
also saved her life. The balance I gave to Mr. T. McMillan, Tweed River—three persons cured by your valuable
medicine. Although I have camped out and travelled ahout 10,000 miles since my illness, I have not contracted a
cold since.—l am, Sir, your best well-wisher,
THOS. ROSS, Lebasa, via Suva, Fiji.

unprincipled persons to make imitations, each calling his medicine “ Bronchitis Cure,” with the object of deceiving the simple-
minded, and so getting a sale for an imitation which has none of the beneficial effects that HEARNE’S BRONCHITIS CURE
has. Consequently it has become necessary to draw your attention to this fact, and to request you in your own interests to b«
particular to ask for HEARNE’S and see that you get it.

“Rotorua” : Re the hostility of banks
towards the birth-rate, as shown by the fact that
they refuse to allow any employee to marry till he
attains a certain salary, and then don’t let him
get that salary till he is so old that he wants a
bath-chair rather than a wife—I have great
doubts about the legality of the rule. British
law, at all events, approves of the birthrate, and
disapproves of any restriction upon it. Some
thirty years ago a cantankerous Englishman left
a will which provided that if his daughter married
a Scot she should be disinherited. The Court
ruled the disinheriting clause null and void. The
girl, it held, might be mashed on a Scotchman,
and not being allowed to marry him might
remain in celibacy. Thereby the large family
which she might have had if she had married
would remain unborn, and the sovereign’s possible
supply of soldiers would be diminished; con-
sequently the clause was held to be contrary to
the monarch’s safety, dignity and prerogative.
It seems long-winded and bathetic, but so long as
the best results are arrived at it doesn’t matter so
very much what polysyllables the Court uses on the
road.
“ Itan ” : I am interested in Sunday-school
picnic evolution. It is astonishing how often the
male visitor strikes Eve there, even if he misses
the Serpent. How the female adults roll up !
Went to one the other day in a Sydney suburb, in
search of an inspiration from a gathering of
Juvenile Australia. Instead, I mostly struck
Full-grown Australia, and Old ditto carrying
gamps and wearing spectacles. Although the
money which was paid for the eatables was sub-
scribed specially for the scholars’ regalement,
they did not have a very good eating time on the
stale buns and staler sandwiches. But the
teachers and the older dames dined sumptuously
on the best the foodshops could supply. The
same misappropriation applied to the sports
prizes ; for while the kiddies ran races for the
cheapest German toy-trash, the girl teachers
scooted with pretty little shrieks over a brief
50yds. for some very decent articles. When the
girl-folk were not supping with the disciples in
the marquee, they were mostly swarming over and
racing around the only parson and curate present.
The famine in men gave that lucky pair a great
time.

The Scalp Needs the Protection of
Zam-Buk Soap to Safeguard it
against Impurities & Disease.
* 1 ♦—
Neglect of thorough and frequent cleansing of the scalp very often leads to disagreeable and
painful sores and inflammation, if not to that more serious trouble, ringworm. No mother
should neglect this very important duty, not alone for the avoidance of disease, but as a
preventative of the impurities which usually settle in the heads of young children, who
are more prone to scalp troubles, due to their habits of play and their mingling together,
whence the trouble is very quickly communicated from one to another. Again, scurf and
scale, in .addition to being distasteful in the scalp, become a veritable breeding-ground for
myriads of disease germs and septic impurities, and, in addition, blocks the tiny pores
from fulfilling their duties as they should. Mothers often wonder why their children’s hair is
so thin and dry in appearance. The cause is usually to be found in the accumulation of
scurf and other impurities clogging the passages which supply the natural oil necessary
for aiding its growth and maintaining its softness and lustre.
For the washing of the scalp the use of common soaps should be avoided. . They do
not remove the trouble, but very often add to it, owing to the injurious substances they
contain. There is no better preparation for cleansing and purifying the scalp and skin
than Zam-Buk Medicinal Soap. Its medicines remove the impurities from the scalp, cleanse
the pores, thus allowing free action of the natural oil, and, being a powerful germicide and
antiseptic, protects it from disease. When thoroughly rubbed into the head in the form
of a shampoo, Zam-Buk Soap proves a refreshing and beneficial tonic for that soreness of
the scalp and the heavy, aching, tired brain.
No lady who values the appearance of the hair and the purity of the scalp, and no
mother who realises the importance of safeguarding her children from skin and scab
troubles, should neglect the healthful aid of Zam-Buk Soap.
Mrs. Eliza Bowen, Lucan Street, Bendigo, Victoria, writes:—
“ 1 find Zam-Buk Soap the best soap I have ever used for toilet purposes. It
makes the skin beautifully soft and velvety to the touch. I also use it for my
little son, on whom it has a very soothing effect, and it removed an obstinate
scurf from his head, which gave me a good deal of trouble to get rid of.”
Zam-Buk Soap is sold by Chemists and Stores, or may be obtained direct from
THE ZAM-BUK MFG. CO., 39 Pitt Street, Sydney.
Perfect
Business, Robbery, etc.
.About the last public action of Charles Tucker,
. e Aus. Customs expert, before he retired to lead the
simple life, was to move for a Commission to inquire into
the methods of produce merchants. That was nearly 18
months agfo, and the Commissioners, who sit in camera,
are still finding- out why the turkey sheds so much tissue
m transit to the merchant’s sale-rooms. No report of any
nature has yet been issued ; but probably the Commission
is waiting- until Tucker is available to give expert evi-
dence on business methods.
The salt works at Mackay (Q.). which were in a
weakly state owing to want of capital, have been pur-
chased by Frank Fraser, of Ingham, who proposes to
manufacture 20,000 tons of salt a year, and one merchant
has offered to take the lot off his hands. The business
has this considerable pull over many North Queensland
mining ventures—it isn’t likely to run out of raw material.
* * * *
The Northern Territory is the Paradise of the
robber whose lib. is 140 z., and whose gallon only runs
to 3J quarts. The Weights and Measures Act applies to
the N.T. just as it does to the rest of S. Aus., but through
all these years S. Aus. hasn’t worried about supplying'
the N.T. with standards, so the law of good weight and
full measure is a dead letter. Or rather, it is a letter that
hasn’t yet been horn.
* * * *
“Ken R.” : A tale of Australian ingenuity in
the food line comes from Ramornie, the meat establish-
ment in the Clarence River district, where the problem
of corned beef plus beef extract has been solved by an
Australian extract maker. The difference between corned
beef and boiled beef, from the manufacturer’s point of
view, lies in the fact that the boiled beef process is rapid,
and returns beef-extract as a valuable by-product,
whereas corned beef takes considerable time, more plant,
and returns no extract. Corned beef, however, has a
readier, sale ; so that any process that would cheapen the
production in point of time, or by, saving the beef ex-
tract, would be of considerable value. O’Keefe’s method,
as practised at Ramornie, gives a perfect corned beef,
pork or mutton in ten (10) minutes, and the ox-tongues
treated with this pickle are said to be ahead of any done
by the brine process at these works. The old way of mak-
ing corned meat was a matter of days ; and the value of the
extract lost was from 265. to 28s. per ox. This made the
corned article a thing to be avoided by the meat artisan
as long as boiled was in demand. The “0.K.” pickle
should make a big difference to the small butchers. A
piece of (fresh) meat, selected by a customer, can be cut
off the carcase, dipped in the pickle for ten minutes, and
will then, when boiled, be the real corned substance.
Reports from the London office of the Aus. Meat Co.
declare the shipments of “0.K.” beef to be the best
sample of corned stuff produced for the season. As an
instance of the rapidity of the action of this pickle, 45
bullocks were converted into corned beef in nine hours.
This was just ordinary business —not a struggle to put up
a useless record.
* * * *
“Hypo”: My work takes me amidst frozen
mutton, butter, and rabbit, and the way they are branded
hurts me. I want to see “Australia” in big letters on
the cases and boxes. At present the cold thing is
marked—
WILLOW VALE,
Victoria,
Australia.
I want to see that order reversed. For butter boxes, let’s
put on the map of Australia, bold. The factory might
have its locality indicated on the map by a square with
the name attached. That should satisfy the usual
hump of local self-importance. But let “Australia”
be writ large every time. Then the world would soon
know the name as it knows the magic letters “ U.S.A.”
The world has no time for provincialisms. If it waited to
consider them motor cars would run over it.
* * * *
“K.”: “ Silvertail ” (8., 10/10/’O7) asks—
“ Can anyone tell me why some tobacco is put up in
the form of 5£ plugs to the pound ? ” I can. It is put
up in this way, so that the storekeeper may make a
legitimate profit in this line, and the blackleg store-
keeper may be euchred.
F’rinstance, the price of light Yankee was 4s. 6d.
wholesale before-the. tariff. (’Tis now 6d. per lb. more.) .
The blackleg storekeeper so greatly cut the price in the
past that light Yankee was generally sold at 4s. 9d. per
lb. Say, the railway freight to this town amounted to
(roughly) one penny per lb. That made the nett landed
cost 4s. 7d. They sold at 4s. 9d.—2d. profit on 4s. 7d.,
which is a shade over 3J per cent. ! It takes a bigger per-
centage than that to run an ordinary country business.
To stop that ruinous game, it was devised that each
plug be made slightly smaller, so that it would take 5|
cakes to weigh one pound.
Now to work it out. Those 51 cakes cost 4s. 7d. landed,
10-llths of the pound were sold for 4s. 9d. (not necessarily
as lib. nett weight). Ten-elevenths of 4s. 7d. amounts to
4s. 2d., and the odd 1-llth amounts to sd. He makes7d.
on 4s. 2d., as he gets 4s. 9d. for his outlay of 4s. 2d.
That is exactty 14 per cent. At the same rate, he gets
5 7-10ths for his odd cake ; if allowed to accumulate until
he sells 10 odd cakes as an additional pound (of course
the charge for a single half-cake would be 6d.). He
therefore actually gets 5/2 7-10ths for his original pur-
chase, which cost 4s. 7d. That is 14 per cent, profit.
Now the working exes, of a country store run from 5 to 8
per cent. So, say, 6£ per cent, is an average. There-
fore only 71 per cent, is cleared ultimately in this line—a
fair thing when it is considered that the’country store-
keeper is forced to “ book ” at this price.
Melbourne Board of Health recently let light in
on the alleged egg-powder, and the guileless consumer
begins to get a glimmer of what struck him. 'Of several
analysed, one comparatively harmless sample panned out
wheaten-starch and coloring matter exclusively, while a
second responded with maize-starch, tartaric acid, and
aniline dye! The Health Board sa’vs a standard of
purity for egg.powder is urgently wanted, and suggests
the necessity for a suspicion, at least, of hen-fruit in the
allegedly powdered egg.
* * * *
A 25-ton lugger, commanded by two Japs and
run by 11 aborigines, lately, in three weeks and three
days, scooped up nearly two tons of b&che-de-mer from a
spot on the Barrier Reef, the value of the catch beino-
about £350. Once a delicacy for the Chinese palate only,
now sea-slug- is rapidly becoming an accepted tit-bit for
the white epicure, and b&che-de-mer is like to dispute
with the turtle the privilege of lubricating aldermen.

“Quidam”: Despite the depression of Kal-
goorlie (and it is awful), rents hold their heads up proudly:
A 6 x 8 mansion of galvanised iron and bagging brings in Vs.
a week just about the price for which one can get a decent
four-roomed cottage, with kitchen, garden, water laid on,
etc., in many nice little towns in Victoria. Considering the
fact that 7s. in Kalgoorlie is about as bard to get as it is
in Vic., this matter appears to reflect something on
somebody.
* * * *
The Melbourne Licenses Reduction Board is
getting a few ragged scraps of interesting information
mixed with cargoes of irrelevant matter. Most of the
“ pubs ” that let for £3 a week or so would, it is alleged,
bring in only 255. or 30s. as ordinary shops or private
houses. One of the beauties of owning a hotel, somebody
else pointed out, is that it is never vacant like a shop is.
Apropos the same subject a witness explained how the
City Council has been “ had.” The rent of a house would
be £3 or £4 a week for a five-years lease, with a £2OO or
£3OO bonus every time the lease was renewed ; vet when
the Council valuer was asked how he figured up the
valuations for rating purposes, he admitted that he
merely asked the tenant what the rent was, a d then
capitalised the value of the property on a 6 per cent, basis.
“ Had he known anything about the bonus,” he said, “he
would have added it to the rent.” The forgetfulness of
the tenants with regard to the bonus seems to have been
enthusiastically unanimous.
A big Melbourne rag and fluff firm has upset
peaceful little Adelaide’s habit of going to bed early.
For years all the big shops have closed at 1 o’clock on
Saturdays; then the big Melbourne bull put its foot in
the crockery shop, and all Adelaide’s good resolutions
went smash. A few still advertise that they close on
Saturdays at 1 p.m., but Adelaide hasn’t got enough
sense to voluntarily keep out of the fluff shops after hours,
so those that shut at 1 p.m. are anxiously counting the
cost, and the shop assistants are just as anxiously looking
towards T. Price.
* * * *
Branscome’s manner of turning over his small
capital is original and so far profitable. He tours
Sydney’s suburbs, picks out elegant villas with vacant
allotments alongside, and ascertains a few social facts
respecting the occupiers of the houses. Are the occupiers
owners ? If so, one step is gained. Are they fairly well-
to-do? Second step. Fidgety, cantankerous, or crotchety ?
If so, then it is time to work. He buys the vacant allot-
ment, messes about the piece of ground for a few days,
gives it out he is about to build, and makes himself as
offensive as he can to the adjoining neighbor. The build-
ing is to be of a kind to which he has learned the neigh-
bor has the most pronounced objection. It may be a
workshop, or part workshop, and if so will be just outside
the scope of noxious trades legislation. The neighbor
concludes after some parleying that nothing remains but
to buy out or sell out, and usually he buys out. But
some day Branscome will meet a man who will just let
him build.

Something like a dividend. At a meeting of
the Bank of Hew South Wales held in 1825, presided over
by Mr. Samuel Terry, it was shown that “ the half-year’s
net gain was 11,199 Spanish dollars, allowing of a divi-
dend at the rate of 62 percent, per annum on the amount
of capital invested.” The discount rate at that time was
10 per cent.
“ Belter” : The glories of the rag-trade :—•
“ JfSrainy youth, anxious to get on, wishes obtain com-
mercial experience— salary no object —apply, etc.”
Now, what chance has a man against this sort of thing ?
Here am I now out of a job, and if I competed against this
brainy youth ” for the next cop, I would get left, as
salary is an object to me, and Fat would soon decide that
the other’s credentials were better. Ten years ago my
benighted parents apprenticed me to the rag-trade.
Screw to begin, 2s: fid. and sundry rises; so that in three
years I was the proud recipient of 12s. 6d. a week. Then
the boss reckoned I had reached the end of his possibili-
ties, and I rushed a job at 20s. a week in a large firm. I
was on that for two years, but seeing no chances of a rise
I took a holiday, and sta3 r ed with the old people for six
months of the winter season. Then I made another try
to “get work.” I answered advertisements innumerable,
and eventually got a leg-in with a big suburban firm as
“ head of the hat department.” “ What salary do you
want ? ” said the boss. I ventured to say 30s. "Thirty!”
said the boss in an awe-stricken voice—“ Couldn’t think
of it.” So we agreed to 255. a week; but, after several at-
tempts to reduce even that, the end came over tea-money.
This firm—headed by a “ devout Christian,” be it noted-
only allowed fid. tea-money in lieu of four or five hours’
overtime. “ Come back to-night” was the almost inces-
sant order of the boss. “Go to office for tea-money.”
Being full up of Scrag- and Co:’s, I went to the office as
requested, and saw my sprat on the counter. “ Here’s
sixpence left behind,” I chirped. “ That’s your tea-
money,” said the brilliantined clerk, from his perch.
“Tea-money!” I .yelled. “Never got less than a bob
for tea-money in any other rag-shop, and I’m not starting
now.” After a big row with the boss and half-a-dozen
under-gangers, I got the shilling—and the sack. A youth
at 15s. a week got my place as “ head of the hat depart-
ment,” with promises of a rise if he “showed an interest
in the firm.” That was two years ago, and apparently he
hasn’t shown the interest required, as he’s still toiling
for the same old 15s.
* * * *
The coupon nuisance is assuming alarming pro-
portions in Melbourne. The co-operative system which
flourishes in Sydney was blocked .years ago by law in
Yarra-side, but somehow personal coupons were left un-
touched. Now each manufacturer includes in his pack-
ages his own coupon, and a number of these entitles the
bearer to certain allegedly valuable articles. These
coupons are not interchangeable, and each manufacturer
strives 1 to offer something more valuable than his com-
petitor. The trouble usually begins thus : Under Mrs.
Fortybob’s door is thrust a leaflet an nouncing that in future
a ticket will be enclosed in each lib. packet of Winks’s
Pizen Flour, and 50 of these tickets on presentment
at the office will be exchanged for an invaluable teapot
worth ninepence. Mrs. F. jumps at the offer, and for a
while uses nothing but Winks’s flour. She uses plenty of
it, too, with her eye on that teapot. But when she has
twenty tickets another leaflet is thrown over her front
fence, referring this time to Smithson’s Flour. If she
collects 75 Smithson coupons she will own a full-dinner
service of eight pieces. She usually burns Winks’s
coupons, and commences to use Smithson’s flour,
which is not as good, and costs Id. per lb. more.
In the meantime Mr. Fortybob is smoking more tobacco
than is good for him, in the hope of collecting
enough tags to exchange for a silver-mounted pipe ;
while eight-.year-old Bill Fortybob is cramming himself
with villainous stickjaw, so that by November Ist he may
have more stickjaw labels than any other unfortunate
kiddie in Melbourne. For this proud achievement he will
receive from Stickjaw, Ltd., a football worth 2s. 6d.
Hundreds of families are suffering from acute attacks of
this coupon disease. The law should put a stop to it, in
the interests alike of manufacturers and consumers.
* * * *
“ Ejayem ” : Re sundry B. pars, dealing with
the wily pursuer of the drink of infinitesimal cost. In
my town is a newspaper proprietor and manager, with
whom the giving of hopeless cheques has developed into
a habit. Two of his comps got hold of a£2 cheque,
drawn by him, t’other day, and began a round of the
pubs. Their method was to have two drinks, and then
tender the cheque in payment. In every case the pub
keeper cut his losses by asking the revellers to get outside
and take the cheque with them. After the 14th brace of
drinks had been negotiated, the worthy fellows lost the
cheque, or swallowed it Or something, otherwise they’d
be knocking it down yet.
A WAIL OF MO.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Lord Rothschild states that stocks are low because
Governments all over the world are aiming heavy blows
at Capital. —News Item.
Oh, listen to the most distressful ditty
Of one who feels his nose is out of hook !
Paralysis is creeping on the city,
And no one at prospectuses will look.
It cannot be the gudgeon-folk distrust us—
That credulous investors have grown few —
No, no, the wicked Governments have “bust” us
By coveting the profits of the Jew.
New Court is in the doldrums very badly ;
It’s years, and years, and years, or so it seems,
Since speculators vied together madly
To underwrite our high financial schemes.
And High Finance is drowning in low water,
And melancholy marks us for her own.
It almost makes one wish that Pharaoh’s daughter
Had let our great progenitor alone.
Dear money is, perhaps, sufficient reason
To generate a timid atmosphere ?
I reckon not ; there never is a season
When money that is Hebrew isn’t dear.
It is not higher interest that’s sinking
The Stock Exchange so deeply in the dumps ;
But Stocks and Shares and Capital are shrinking
Beneath the Labor agitator’s thumps.
He’s murmuring, the discontented creature,
That while we pocket dollars, he gets dimes.
His cheek is just about the strangest feature
Throughout these queer and topsy-turvy times.
He’s anti-national and eontra-Nature,
An atheist, immoral, and a thief,
And yet he’s nobbling every Legislature !
Ilinc illce lacrimce, or, hence this grief!
Take off the gloves, on this our day of danger
You have to hit, to cripple, and to kill.
Or Socialistic dogs-within-the-manger
B ill whittle Private Enterprise to nil.
Then there shall be no borrowers, and lenders—
Abednego and Shadrach, and myself—
The pound-of-flesh requisitors of spenders,
Must And our occupations on the shelf.
A'.S.W.

MICK SIMMONS

The Rain Upon the Roof.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I HAVE heard a goddess singing,
And her songs were all of love,
I have followed all her winging
Notes that rose to Heaven above.
I have heard the plaudits thundered
As her last note thrilling died,
And my heart stood still and wondered
At the woman deified.
I have heard an army marching
With a drum-beat pounding wide—
Tramping men and horses arching
Glossy necks in fire and pride.
Well 1 knew the war-drum’s story,
As I watched the army pass—
All its tale of war and glory.
And of blood upon the grass.
I have heard a bird at dawning,
Ere the sun had kissed the sea,
To the rose of eave and awning,
Singing greenwood minstrelsy.
And I thought of woodland questing,
And of dew that fell in showers,
And of breaking ground and breasting
Through the throat-high ferns and flowers.
I have heard the storm-song shouted
On the sands at dead of night,
When the clouds above were routed
Like an army put to flight.
And I thought of all the daring
Of a thousand ships of old —
Homeward coming, seaward faring,
Bearding rock and reef and cold.
I have heard (when Nature grew sick
And the stars of midnight set),
Sure to God, the sweetest music
That has ever sounded yet;
For I thought of cattle calling
To the rain-clouds looped aloof,
And I woke to hear the falling
Of the rain upon the roof.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
The Best Policy.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
JEREMIAIi MAUNDER crossed Collins-
street at an acute angle, and took up his
position at the corner of a narrow passage,
or alley. From this coign of vantage he glowered
balefully at the form of Samuel Sharp, who leant
against a lamp-post on the opposite side of the
way. This temporary dissolution of a long-estab-
lished partnership had been brought about after a
brief but wordy warfare, in which Mr. Sharp
had called Mr. Maunder such things as “ cock-
eyed walrus,” “mangy goat,” and “fat-headed
shrimp.” It was at this point of the dispute that
Mr. Maunder crossed the road, breathing noisily
and nasally.
The principal highways of the city were gaily
decorated with flags, floral arches, bunting, and
tinsel adornments of the crimped-paper variety,
for it was the year 1901, and distinguished visitors
from a far country were honoring Melbourne by
their presence. A steady stream of sightseers
surged through the streets, jostling regular
habitues into the kennels, and driving perspiring
men of business to exasperation by the delibera-
tion of their movements.
Rousing himself from his state of lethargy,
Mr. Maunder, forgetful for the time being of his
perfidious friend, became absorbed in contempla-
tion of the multitudes that perambulated the
pavements. He was disturbed in his philosophic
meditations by a light touch upon the arm. He
shook the obtruding hand off.
“ Git away, Sam.” He spoke with some firm-
ness. “I don’t want ter ’ave no more words
with the likes o’ you.”
“ But perhaps you want a job,” said a gentle,
hurried, feminine voice.
Jerry brought a pair of hard-boiled blue eyes
to bear on the speaker. Astonishment and in-
decision struggled on his usually inexpressive
features. True, for some years the greater part
of his diurnal existence had been ostensibly spent
in looking for a job, but now that one had stolen
upon him tip-toe and unawares, so to speak, his
shyness of the stranger was akin to that displayed
by an unbroken horse when it meets man. He
grinned coyly, and breathed even harder than was
his wont. His interlocutor was tall and slight of
figure ; she was heavily draped in black garments ;
her face was hidden by a thick veil; but she had
a soft, persuasive, albeit a trembling voice.
Jerry was masculine, and also human. He
plunged boldly.
“ Don’t mind if I do,” he said, with an embar-
rassed chuckle ; then, aghast at his own temerity :
“ Wot is it ? I carn’t walk fur, an’ I carn’t carry
no weights ’cos of my roomatics.”
The female produced a fair-sized hand-bag from
beneath her cloak. “ It’s only to take this to the
booking-office at Prince’s Bridge station,” she
said, reassuringly. “ Leave it there, and bring
the ticket to the Treasury end of Fitzroy Gardens
in half-an-hour’s time. I shall be waiting for
you.” She glanced at a diminutive watch set in
a gold wrist-bangle. “It is now 11 o’clock—
at half-past I shall expect you. The bag’s locked,”
she added, meaningly, as she thrust the article in
question into Jerry’s unwilling right hand, at the
same instant slipping two half-crowns into his will-
ing left. Then, turning sharply on her heel, she
hurried out of the alley-way and was lost to sight
amidst the throng.
Jerry gazed in bewilderment alternately at the
bag and at his retaining fee.
“ Lor’ lumme ! ” he exclaimed, “ a real proper
soft job, an’ five bob fur doin’ of it ! Why, she
must be wot S.am Sharp calls a Grease-us. Well,
she’s greased me orlright, so ’ere goes. ”
“ Wot’s wrong, Jerry?” said a familiar voice at
his elbow. “Got a job? Never mind. Don’t
look so ’eart-broke. ’Ere’s yer old pal ready to
lend you a ’and.”
Mr. Maunder cast a look of ineffable scorn on
his partner in many iniquities.
“ You be orf, Sam Sharp,” he said, with deci-
sion. “ I don’t require your ’elp —thankin’ you
kindly orl the same. I wouldn’t demean meself
to be see’d walkin’ with you in the public streets.
Five bloomin’ bob ! ” he concluded, in an out-
burst of exultation.
Sam shook his head sadly.
“You’re dreamin’, Jerry,” he said. “Six-
pence wos wot the lady said she wos goin’ ter
give you if you made ’aste, an’ quite enough too.”
Mr. Maunder disdained retort, but partially
opening a cautious and grimy hand, displayed
two undeniable half-crowns.
“Well,” said Sam, with a burst of bitterness
too deep for eloquence —“well, I’m jiggered!
I’d ha’ done it meself fur a tanner. ”
During his necessarily slow progress along the
crowded street, Jerry was aware of the mute pre-
sence of his faithful ally, nor was his exasperation
softened by Sam’s manifold good counsel.
“ It’s too ’eavy fur you, Jerry,” he remarked,
with kindly solicitude. “ I kin see it’s too ’eavy
fur you be ’arf. Best let me ’elp you a bit.”
Jerry preserved a lofty silence, but tightened
his grip on the bag. His color came and went in
patches, and he urged Sam to go to perdition.
“ You’re exhaustin’ of yerself, Jerry,” con-
tinued the reproachful Sam. “I warned you as
’ow you would. Why not ’ire a railway van or
borrer a wheelbarrer ? Remember yer pore ’eart
wot the prison doctor said ’ad got a fatted regene-
ration. Any’ow let me carry one o’ them ’arf-a-
crowns for you. I carn’t go on seein’ you do orl
the work like this ’ere.”
Jerry came to a full stop.
“Are you a-goin’ ter leave me alone, Sam
Sharp, or are you not ? ” he demanded, with
ferocity. “ ’Ere am I doin’ a ’ard day’s honest
work, an’ you come cadgin’ on me. S’elp me,
it’s enough ter make a man spit ! ”
“ Fust job in twenty year,” said Sam, ab-
stractedly addressing the chimney-pots. He
paused, then turned suddenly on the indignant
Mr. Maunder. “ Wot’s that tickin’in that ’ere
portmantle o’ yers ? ” he asked, abruptly.
Jerry so far forgot his wrath as to incline his
untidy head for an instant. A slow and steady
ticking sounded distinctly from the interior of the
bag.
“’Ow should I know?” he said, with cold
dignity. “ I s’pose it kin tick ef it likes, carn’t
it ? You ain’t the on’y one what goes on tick, Sam
Sharp.” He chuckled with a senile appreciation
of his own little jest.
There was a moment’s silence, unbroken save
by the hurrying steps of foot-passengers and the
methodical, mysterious tick-ticking. Then Sam,
who had gone strangely white, spoke in strangled,
husky tones:
“Jerry, you blamed fool ! Don’t you tumble
to it ? Prince’s Bridge station ! The Dook and
Dookess’s procession this arternoon ! It’s a
’fernal machine-—a bomb; they alius goes by
clockwork. That ’ere woman wos a lady anna-
crist. Gawd ! I wouldn’t be in your boots for
summut ! So-long, I’m orf. ” He made a feint
of flight.
Jerry stood irresolute.
“ Somebody must ha’ give you a knock on the
’ead some time or other, Sam,” he said, pessi-
mistically. “ Wot would anyone in their census
want ter blow up a railway station fur ? ”
“ Didn’t a woman give you the bag ? An’ wot
does it matter to a woman wot she blows up, as
long as she blows up summut?” replied Sam,
scornfully. “If you wos a widower like me,
Jerry, you’d know orl about that. Well, I’ll be
gettin’.”
“’Ere, ’old’ard, Sam; ’old ’ard,” cried the
agitated Jerry. “ Wot am I ter do with it ? You
wouldn’t desert yer old pal —you ain’t got the un-
feelin’ ’eart ter leave me alone with this ’ere ? ”
‘ ‘ Ain’t I, though ! ” retorted Mr. Sharp. ‘ ‘ Ketch
me stoppin’ within’ a mile o’ that thing. No
fear ! ”
“Thenl’ll jest leave it ’ere,” said Jerry, despe-
rately, and he deposited the bag in the exact
centre of the footpath.
“ Pick it up, you mug ! ” said Sam, savagely.
“You’ll c’llect a crowd an’ git us both run in.
D’yer expect the beak’ll listen ter a yarn about a
woman give it you ? Not ’im ! It’s your funeral,
Jerry, old pal, an’ I’m sorry fur you.”
“ Then I’ll take it ter where she told me,” cried
the badgered Mr. Maunder.
“ I wonder at you, Jerry,” said Sam, reprov-
ingly. “ ’Ow kin you tork o’ sech a thing ? You
wouldn’t never smile agin, wot with thinkin’ of
orl them pore innercent people blown inter little
atoms. No, fetch it along to the p’leece-station
—there’ll be a reward, not ter speak of your name
bein’ in orl the newspapers as the ’ero wot risked
’is own life fur the good of ’is suff’rin’ feller
creeturs. Shouldn’t wonder if you didn’t git the
Victoria Cross. It’ll be the makin’ of you, Jerry.”
“ I don’t want no Victoria Cross,” said Mr.
Maunder, with simple self-denial. “ ’Ere, ketch
’old, Sam. It’s too ’eavy fur me. You’re wel-
come to orl you kin git.”
“ No, no, Jerry,” replied Sam, firmly. “ I
arsked you once afore if I shouldn’t ’elp you, out
o’ the kindness of me ’eart, an’ small thanks I got
fur it. Besides, you’re a old man with on’y a
few more years ter live at most, so it carn’t make
much difference ter you if it’s to-day or to-morrow
—nobody won’t miss you. But me, Lor’ bless
you, I’m on’y jest beginnin’ ter enjoy life.”
“ You shall ’ave one o’ them ’arf-a-crowns,
Sam,” urged Mr. Maunder.
Sam shook his head.
“ Both of’em, then,” cried Jerry, in the accents
of a gambler staking his last coin.
“Not if you wos to go down on yer bended
knees fur a week, an’ offer me a thousand ’arf-a-
crowns,” replied his companion, inflexibly. “Be
a man, Jerry. The longer you stops ’ere wasting
val’able time, the more likely the thing is ter go
orf sudden-like.”
The few loiterers in a certain unfrequented side-
street cast curious glances at the dilapidated and
elderly gentleman with the agonised expression of
countenance, who shuffled hastily along the
pavement, carrying a black hand-bag almost
at his arm’s length. Sam Sharp, with the
lust of greed hot in his heart, yet keenly
alive to the fact that caution is akin to the
truest courage, kept the breadth of the road
and about a hundred paces between himself and
his friend, all ready to beat a swift retreat in the
event of emergency. At length, after a great feat
of rapid pedestrianism, Mr. Maunder, for the first
(Continued on next page.J

and last time in his chequered existence, made a
voluntary incursion into the barren territories of
his hereditary foes, the police.
“ A lady Annie-kissed give it me,” he said,
breathlessly, as he planted the hand-bag gingerly
on the floor of the station-room. The Inspector
in charge, who was writing at a tall, railed-desk,
dropped his pen and surveyed his dishevelled
visitor with a look of suspicion.
“ Eh, what?” he said sharply.
Mr. Maunder breathed heavily through his nose.
It was at this juncture that Sam slipped un-
ostentatiously into the office, and, elbowing the
speechless Jerry aside, explained the situation in
a few well-chosen words. Several times the
Inspector interrupted his statement with acidu-
lated and hypercritical interpolations.
“ Well,” he said, after Sam had concluded an
eloquent peroration with a gentle hint as to the
risks he and his companion had run, coupled with
the expression of his conviction that Government
should suitably acknowledge the same in coin,
amount unspecified—“ well, our men have been
on the look-out for anything of the sort the last
month or more, but I’ve not heard of any suspi-
cious characters in this particular line being under
observation. Still, it’s always best to be on the
safe side. J ones, fetch along a bucket of water. ”
A youthful constable, who had been engaged in
inspecting Mr. Maunder, hastened to obey his
chief’s behest.
“It’s heavy enough—and locked,” said the
Inspector, as he placed the bag carefully on his desk.
He took a bunch of curious-looking keys from
a hook on the wall, and tried one after another.
At the ninth attempt the bag opened.
“I’m damned!” muttered the police officer.
Then he swung round on Sam. ‘ ‘ What cock-
and-bull story is this you’ve been faking up ? ” he
demanded, brusquely. “ See here.”
Sam and Jerry drew near and gazed in helpless
amazement; and for perhaps the first time in his
life Samuel Sharp found himself bereft of the
powers of speech.
At this psychological moment a cab rattled up
to the door, 'and a thick-set, alert man, dressed in
an ill-fitting, navy blue serge suit, hustled into the
office.
“ Mornin’, Pearce,” he said, with a nod to the
Inspector; then his eye fell upon the bag.
“ How in thunder did this come here ? ” he cried.
“ I’ve been chasing that bag half over Melbourne
the whole blessed mornin’. Well, if this' isn’t a
stroke of luck ! ”
He plunged a hairy fist into the receptacle and
drew forth with loving care a heterogeneous
collection—rings sparkling with gems, valuable
watches, bracelets set with precious stones, gold
chains, and an eight-day travelling-clock which
ticked with a solemn persistence.
“P’raps you can explain matters, Palmer?”
said the Inspector. “ I can get nothing in-
telligible out of these men,” he added, un-
gratefully, with a basilisk glance in the direction
of Sam and Jerry.
“ Elizabeth-street jewellery robbery,” said the
detective, concisely. ‘ ‘ Betty Flanagan and Dick
the Dodger both in it up to the chins. I got on
Bet’s tracks this mornin’. Lost her in Collins-
street owing to the crowd, picked her up again at
the corner of Bourke and Swanston streets, and
lost her, like a bloomin’ fool, in Little Bourke-
street. She must ha’ twigged me, I think ; she’s
as cunning as fifty monkeys. How did you come
by this ? It’s the best part of the loot.”
The Inspector expounded briefly.
“ The booking office at Prince’s Bridge ! ”
ejaculated the detective, with a little whistle.
“ Now, I wonder what her artful game was ? At
all events, this is checkmate with a vengeance.”
“That’s so,” said the Inspector as he re-locked
the bag. “ All right, Maunder and Sharp, you
can go. We know where to find you when
wanted. You’ve nothing particular up against
either of them just now, eh, Palmer ?”
The other laughed good-humoredly.
“ Why, no ; I can put my foot on ’em at any
moment,” he said.
“ It’s big enough,” said the embittered Sam,
with a disparaging glance at the detective’s full-
sized boots. “Ain’t there no reward fur two
pore, honest men, wot ’ave done their level best
ter ’elp you, an’ lost ’arf a day’s work a-doin’ of
it ? ” he inquired, tiuculently.
“You’ll get your reward all right,” replied the
Inspector, grimly. “We won’t keep you waiting
long, I can promise you that much. You can
clear out now—the pair of you’ll be called as wit-
nesses later on, I shouldn’t wonder. Good-
morning.”
The two crestfallen recoverers of lost property
passed slowly out into the street, Jerry still cling-
ing with the tenacity of a limpet to his well-earned
specie.
“ See wot comes o’doin’a good action,” re-
marked Sam, summing up the situation with con-
centrated venom. “ Not even a bloomin’ thank
you. Honesty the best policy, be blowed ! Never
no more.”
“ You’re a nice un, Sam Sharp,” said his com-
panion, bitterly. “’Fernal machine, indeed!
Why, ef we’d on’y opened the bag as I wanted to
’ave done, we might ha’ kep’ the lot —who’d ha’
bin any the wiser ? ”
“We shouldn’t,” retorted Sam, enigmatically.
“We’d ha’ bin bound ter be nabbed sooner or
later, you kin bet yer bottom dollar on that—
necessaries arter the act. Five years ’ard. You
wouldn’t never ’ave ’ad your freedom no more this
side o’ Jordan, Jerry, old pal. Orl the same, I’m
sorry we upset Betty Flanagan’s apple cart.”
Mr. Maunder preserved a dignified silence,
what time he nibbled hungrily at the rim of one of
his half-crowns. Sam’s quick eye caught the
glint of silver, and his face brightened visibly.
“Ah, well,” he said, with a self-satisfied sigh,
“it’s orl the fault o’ my ’magination wot runs
away with me. I carn’t ’elp it—it’s jist a gift, I
s’pose.”
“Take my tip an’ don’t let it run away with
you agin to the p’leece station, wot ain’t a ’ealthy
sitiwation fur neither of us,” put in Jerry, with
conviction.
“ If you on’y ’ad one quarter o’ my ’magina-
tion,” continued Sam, ignoring the interruption,
“ you’d know ’ow thirsty I was at this’ere blessed
moment, arter torkin’ to them Johns, while you
stood by mum as mutton. Come on, matey, let’s
git acrosst to the “Orns,’ an’ change them bits o’
pewter o’ yours.”
LAWRENCE B. JUPP.
The Water-Finders.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
n\TO rain had fallen for a year, and all the
tanks were dry,
’ And like some dreadful cloud of doom
the ibises flew by;
The wind was like a furnace breath, the skies
were all aflame,
When thro’ the silence of the West two water-
finders came.
They’d tramped, they said, a weary tramp that
' started ere the sun
Across the dazzling wastes of sky his journey had
begun ;
And it was very plain to see
That they were on the wallabee,
And tramped from run to run.
Now, Mitchelmore, our manager, was deemed a
canny Scot,
But in his big, receptive ear they whispered quite
a lot,
Of how they made the desert bloom with many a
luscious crop—■
For subterranean rivers they had hoisted to the
top !
Of how in Northern Queensland, where the fat of
man is fried,
They had tapped the bubbling water under deserts
drear and dried;
By north and south, and east and west,
Their victories were manifest—•
And could not be denied.
They had a swag of documents to inkily declare
That water could be drawn from stone by this
unrivalled pair.
They were “The Wizards of the West,” “The
Nozzles of the North,”
For from the hidden stratas deep they dragged
the torrents forth.
The sheaves were signed by magistrates ; and one
amongst them bore
The special affidavit of a Queensland editor —
“We certify these fellows are
The best in all the land by far,”
That distant pressman swore.
“These prove our bona-fides, sir, without a
doubt”; and then
They made a bet with Mitchelmore of fifty pounds
to ten
That they would make a Paradise, and gladden
ev’ry heart
By reaching seas of water if he’d only let them
start.
(They wanted board and lodging and a gig to
drive around
What time they made incisions in the hard and
thirsty ground.)
“And, mister, we shall leave our fee
To your own generositee
When great cascades are found.”
The water-finders started out one stifling after-
noon,
While voices seemed to whisper “We shall have
the Deluge soon ! ”
A six-horse team the outfit hauled, rope, keg, and
windlass-gear,
Plus countless small accessories that won’t be
mentioned here.
And then they made a start to reach the subter-
ranean sea
That lurked beneath the surface of that scorched
localitee,
And waited for the brain and heart
Of men like them, who knew the art
Of Meteorologee.
They sank and sank, aye, ever down thro’ sand,
and grit, and clay,
Till sceptics winked and tittered in an aggrava-
ting way.
The days went gliding into weeks, and into
months they rolled,
But still the heart of Mitchelmore was sad and
un consoled.
“I’ll give you chaps another week,” he said,
“ and then you quit,
Unless you strike the water, and
I want you now to understand
Your pay depends on it.”
The given week went by, and then a comprehen-
sive swear
Expressed the bitter fury of that raging manager.
“ You’ve knocked us off,” the seekers said; “ our
job’s unfinished yet;
We’ll deem it quite a favor if you pay us up
our bet ;
For don’t you see the world is round (and thank
the Fates it’s wide).
With water all around ic, sir—that cannot be
denied !
We shan’t retract, and if you like
We’ll keep on sinking till we strike
The sea the other side ! ”
Then Mitchelmore orated in his free and forceful
way : '
“ I’ve been duped and I’ve been diddled, yet I’mi-
man enough to pay ;
But you’re frauds, and great delusions, stinking
fish and cablegrams ”
(The air was impregnated with a multitude of
damns);
“You’re plagues, and public scandals, and a most
outrageous shame—
And you’ve twenty minutes’ notice now to go the
way you came.”
They shouted as the gate they shut:
“ You quite forgot our ref’rence, but
We thank you all the same ! r
**• * *
For eighteen months no rain had come, and all
the tanks were dry,
And, like a messenger of doom, the choking dust
went by;
The wind was like a furnace-breath, the skies
were burning red,
As thro’ the grassless wastes of sand the water-
finders fled;
They trudged toward the waiting West beneath
the blistering sun,
With thoughts that came in unison, with hearts
that beat as one ;
And it was plain enough to see
How they had made their £ s. d.
On many a droughty run.
N.S.W.
